THE RED PAGE.
E.P.0.: As to George Moore. When, three
years ago, he announced his conversion to the Irish
National movement, nobody in Ireland took him seriously.
The Dublin Leader, after remarking that George Moore
was a funny man, jestingly calculated that it would take
about three years to make him weary of Ireland. Moore
has ne\ er understood the current movement in Irish
national life. During the past decade there has
undoubtedly arisen a great interest in the Irish
language and in Irish History, but, in the main
the national movement is hard, democratic, am
practical in its trend- The bulk of the nation
have no desire to remain a picturesque peasantry
They wish to establish manufactures, to obtain demo-
cratic reforms, to maintain and improve on the acknow-
ledged high standard of local government, to discourage
porter drinking and picturesque “Celtic” idleness, and
to strengthen the economic position of the country.
When Moore decided to leave degenerate London, he
thought he was coming back to the old Celtic Bohemia
But Instead of that his artistic instincts were hurt by
the orderly spirit which the Catholic clergy are fostering
Most of the R.C. priests are taking a great and practica
interest in the industrial revival, which, though handi-
capped by Free Trade with Great Britain, i» making
BR AB f for to Protestantism, the position
is really this. Moore, as a Nationalist, leaves i the K.u.
Church, which, with all its faults, is a National Church,
and joins the Protestant Church, which is the great marK
of the anti-national caste in Ireland. When we hear that
Moore attempts to found a Rational Church as a protest
against both factions, then, and not till then, can we lane
his “ Irishman’s protest ’’ seriously.
Better light without sun *
Than sun without light;:
Better right without law
Than law without right...
Better truth without words
Than words without truth
Better youth without gold
Than gold without youth...
Better flight without wings
Than wings without flight;
Better sight without eyes
Than eyes without sight.
Better heart without head
Than head without hearrt:
Better Art without works
Than works without Art-
Better Pan without pipes
Than pipes, without Pan
Belter man without God
Than God without man.
—W. 3 Lampion, “ after the Persian,” in Thb Rbadk».

Rex. It was John Cleeland, of Bourke-street Albion
Hotel, who supplied W. M. Akhurst for his Melbourne
Xmas pantomime Of 1870 with the lines :
“ For Baillie and Butters have put up their shutters
And toddled away to Fiji."

RENUNCIATION.
Beloved, ah, beloved! in those days
When we still peeped through tangled gold that curled
On baby brows, in our young eyes a maze
Of awe, and wonder at the sweet new world
Bedimmed all else: how could we guess the truth t
A little, laughing girl, and sunkissed lad,
Treadirig the fairy fiHds of life's first youth.
Stretching imperious hands for joys that had
No measure of content ?—how could we know
Aught of the mysteries of love and woet
Yet even then there reached a subtle line.
Unseen, impalpable, but adamant.
That linked in one your unstirred soul and m#ne.
Ah me l the lords of life and death, who grant
Good gifts, but hold back knowledge till too late —
Surely they , sport with us in wanton power t
Ah me ! relentlessly they fixed our fate
And touched the secret thread till hour by hour
And year by growing year, more close and fast
It held us, and 1 woke to love at last.
\
And then I was a woman, quick to count
Each virtue merging in your sum of man ;
The lofty purpose, the unfailing fount
x Of sympathy for all beneath the ban
Of sin and grief, the cburage and the strength—
I loved you for them all, and for these too :
The beauty of your young limbs’ lissom length.
The sweetness of your eyes’ unshadowed blue.
The warm, red mouth, where lurking kisses lay,
The laugh’s keen music, and the smile’s qitick play.
Oh, ay, I was a woman—not a thing
Such as the purblind world would have me be.
Passionless-cold ; but quivering to the spring
Of being, at your presence. Woe is me !
The woman's curse, the curse of cotvard fear
That keeps us.silent while our wrung hearts break,
Assailed me. Oh, the cruel taunt and sneer
That are our portion if we dare to take
Our lives in our own hands and overcome
Custom’s hard rule. I dared not, and was dumb.
And now it is too late, dear ;’t is too late,
Too late to disobey ; too late to rue
Submission to the tyrant we create.
Who bade me sin against myself and you ;
Too late to hope that in our mortal day
The lords of life and death will lift the veil
That clouds your sight; — nay, rather will I pray
That it may darken till your senses fail:
So keep you blind, and her whose life has grown
Between us. Crime and curse are mine alone.
Therefore Igo ! For I indeed have seen
Full knowledge tremble for a moment’s space
Behind your eyes, and lest it burst its screen
And wreck your life, I swear that face to face
Never on earth shall we two stand again ;
Though well I know the ruthless ones who wrought
This evil may themselves not loose the strain
Of the firm withe in which my sold is caught,
That draws me, draws me, even while I speak
The alienating words that flush your cheek
With sudden sense of malice undeserved.
And wrath and wonder. Love, can you not hear
How false my voice rings ? While my lip is curved
■ To studied scorn, do not its accents sear
Your heart with shadowing anguish ? No, ah no !
Too well have I performed my task, too soon
Have, taught aversion ; silent now I go
( Was ever such a bitter day in June ?)
Pierced with the. frozen arrow of your glance.
Whose wound will ache for ever. Save perchance
In brief, rare dreams ivhen I may slip beyond
My stem self-custody, and folloiving free
The guidance of the yearning *, pulsing bond
That spans the gulf between us, I may see
The love-light dawning in your eyes again
And foretaste all that shall be, when at last
Omniscient as our rulers now, we twain
Shall feel each other’s arms about us cast,
And knovfthat though the spheres may cease to roll
Vows cannot limit love, nor gods control.
Oh dearest , these are visions ! This is true:
1 live, and I have looked my last on you.
The long years beckon me, a mournful band,
And each has dropped the poppies from her hand.
E.H.M.
Old “Sydney Punch.”
A PAPER of many associations and
vicissitudes, deaths and resurrections.
Re established, if not founded, in the
hope of doing something more than
had so far been done for literature in Aus-
tralia ; contributed to by the best and brightest
men of their time ; put away now with the toys
that amused us once, but whose interest and pur-
pose is gone.
Punch, was started in Sydney at the date
of the birth of New Australia, when all human
tides of enterprise, adventure and villainy, con-
verging on Port Phillip, swamped the old order
of convietism and worse, A Dr. Revel Johnson
conceived the idea of an Australian. Punch, not
with any very worthy intent. The doctor was
a libeller, a lampooner, an in-and-out-of-gaol
man—and for libels neither justifiable nor excus-
able. He lasted but a few weeks, and then Punch
went down into his box for five years. The
diggers managed to do without him ; although,
had lie struck out as a genuine Australian, there
could have been little doubt of adequate support.
The next start was in 1857, with late Unde-
r Johnson as a leading con-
tributor. A clean paper thence on till the close.
Archie Murray, wood-earver and playwright, was
proprietor and as usual chief loser. A short life
this time, and not a very merry one. Then, a lapse
till the year 18G4, when Walter George Mason,
wood-engraver (once of The Illustrated London
Neivs) roused up the old ghost and re-incarnated it
in better form than previously. (That same Mason
started also The Illustrated Sydney News, a useful
Australian sheet in its time.) From that date
forward Punch had near twenty years of life,
with hardly a break; and during these years
continued to tap the brains of a good many
of the very best men of Australia. Messrs.
Gibbs, Shallard and Co. became printers, in the
old ivy-mantled building nearly opposite the
Currency Lass hotel, in Pitt-street. (What
madness induced any proprietor to change
the name of that old inn?) It was there
that the late H. W. Hicks began the
connection which developed ultimately into the
proprietorship, and it was during these years that
there were names enough on the contents
page to guarantee the success of any paper.
Deniehy, Dailey, Kendall, Martin, Harry Mos-
tyn, Coveney, Frank Hutchinson, G. B. Barton,
poor old P. J. Holdsworth, Monty Scott, Alfred
Clint, Courtenay, Charlie De Boos, T. Moser,
Sheridan Moore, W. A. Daley, Martin (Miss),
Gordon McCrae, Garnet Walch. Everybody who
was anybody in the literary world of that time
seemed to be there. And yet poverty was on the
lintel, decrepitude on the door-posts', and “ please
call again ” pinned to the panel year after year.
J*
What hindered the rise that might so con-
fidently have been predicted ? I find one reason
in a volume of Punch Staff Papers issued from
Punch office in the year 1872. In the preface to
the book this naive admission is made: “We
may here confess to a mistake having been made.
There has been no editor. The result is each con-
tributor is responsible for his own contribution. ”
And the printers’ devil for the whole, we may
assume. There is no doubt at all that here was a
ghastly mistake more than enough to account for
everything. And yet there were other reasons
which I will set forth by-and-by.
What did they do, all these distinguished
people, in the way of making even a crude
beginning with an Australian literature in the
higher vein ? The answer is, Nothing—absolutely
nothing ; and therein is the main cause of their
failure. Badham did a bit of Horace or Virgil
into English. Deniehy reviewed Browning rather
in Edinburgh Review than Punch style ; and that
brilliant gentleman does not rise in one’s estima-
tion when we find it set down in that review that
Browning is unmistakeably the greatest British
dramatist since Elizabethan days, and that it is
extremely doubtful if either Goethe or Schiller
gave the world anything equal to “The Return
of the Druses.” I do not think “Luria” was
written at that time. If “The Return of the
Druses” places Browning above Schiller, “Luria”
should lift him to the same plane with Shakspeare.
Dailey did best with the Chiniquy papers. They
are essentially Punch- y, and fit to have a place
within the covers of the old fellow who first trotted
out Dog Toby. Kendall’s verses would have done
better for Macmillan or Cornhill. Garnet Walch
wrote by the mile, and there were no spots in any
mile fit to be marked with a white stone. Harry
Mostyn did n’t get any hoof-beats or good horse-
talk in; and Frank Hutchinson was always better
fitted for The Saturday Review than for a pro-
fessedly comic paper. The paper was only comic
when some contributor let himself loose. The
attempts at jokes in that period were feeble, the
paragraphing puerile. We scarcely need to be
reminded that there was no editor.
Hicks retired for a while in favour of the late
Neville Montagu, but again came in and again
sold out to Edward Greville. Greville attempted
to put new life in.to the old fellow, whose voice
had become intolerably raucous. He offered for a
new title-page design 20 1., which went to Alfred
Clint, and the page is familiar to all of this
generation who remember the last of Punch.
Greville held on till 1883, when Hicks and J. G.
O’Connor, who had been connected with the paper
from its beginning, became proprietors, but came
to a quarrel over the Redmond brothers. Hicks,
his old Cambridge training stirring him to a
narrow and bitter patriotism, desired to
lampoon the Redmonds, who visited us about that
time, and O’Connor would have none of that.
Consequently there was a split. The broad man
bought the narrow man out, and carried on for a
year. And that was the last year of any Punch
fit to be remembered or talked about. For
Carlisle’s flutter of 1888 is scarcely worth men-
tioning. J. G. O’Connor lost 400 Z. over the
printing, and became, and still remains, residuary
legatee.
It is a doleful sort of story, but only what might
have been expected from a paper started in Aus-
tralia which never dared to be Australian, which
made no appeal to a native Australian genius.
The gossip which lies about its various lives is of
more interest than its actual pages. So many of
its contributors could talk real humour, but wrote
most infernal rot.
J*
Heyday ! It was another sort of world when
Punch was almost on his last legs—-say twenty-
five, even twenty years ago. It was a dull clod
who could not pass a good hour when such men as
Tom Moser, Charlie De Boos, old Frank Brewer,
Hicks, Hutchinson, and some few others met.
They talked of old, old days, and they told old,
old yarns, and with no more reservation than ex-
purgation. Then along a bit was Gus. Wangen-
heim at his inn, the walls thereof covered with a
marvellous panorama of the political and Bohe-
mian life of N.S.W. And Bas. Hodge was not
far away with his saxophone, a monster like the
father of all Meerschaums, out of which he blew
rare music. Richmond Thatcher, moreover, was
to be found thereabout with a joke-wallet always
full. Richmond’s humour was grim and practical;
he was running a paper and a parliamentary can-
didate once, and laid himself out to give the
other fellow a rare dig. He reported his own
man well. And then “ the Opposition candidate
addressed the meeting something after this
fashion.” There followed a column of “ pie.”
Clark Irving, one of the brightest and least
fortunate of our merry men of the wig and
gown, was always in the set, although not
mucli of a writer. He had been a chum
of Lord Rosebery’s at Eton, and they had
many good hours together while Rosebery cl welt
with us very sociably for a while. Alas, Clark
Irving ! He went away with the blue devils, and
I do not think that need have been had all the
people concerned done their duty. Clark used
to tell a good story of Alfred Clint, perhaps the
best of the old Punch artists. Clint, with
'Hutchinson and Hicks (inseparable from Cam-
bridge days onwards) took the trip to Manly, and
a thunderstorm took possession of the city during
their absence. A splash of rain in those days
used to make all the open space between the
water’s edge and the “Paragon” fetlock-deep in
mud. It was the Devil to cross, and if half-a-
dozen cabs were about they were snapped up in
as many seconds. The three stood on the edge of
things, despondent, when suddenly down went
Clint—eyes rolling, muscles twitching. A fit!
and a bad one. “Epilepsy,” said-H. Looks
like it,” replied the other H. “What’s "to be
done?” “Done?” “ Must carry him over to the
Paragon.” So these two middle-aged, dignified
and portly gentlemen uplifted the stricken artist
and bore him to the pub. abreast the quay; fet-
lock-deep in mud whicli splashed abominably.
They took him into an inner room and laid him
on a couch, themselves dead-beat and very filthy.
Then suddenly did Clint upheave his proper self,
all unbesmirched and say, “Let me congratulate
you, oh, my masters ! you have carried genius.
And now what may I offer you to drink?” They
replied, but not exactly in quotable terms.
Good old P. J. used to write for Punch as he
dressed for Shakespear ; not so as to deceive the
elect. G. B. Barton, a highly-intellectual swell
in those days, got off. a lot of venom and paid for
it pretty dearly. Dr. Bad ham was never seen,
never came to town unless something super-
natural dragged him thither. “1 might meet
Parkes,” he would say, “ and I should be picking
up" his aitches in my dreams for a year after.”
Dailey beamed from afar, and was best, as in
many other connections, in talking of bygones.
He was the father of the poor, who were with
Punch always, according to Apostolic prophecy.
I do not think any man with half a tale, real or
concocted, ever approached the little man in vain.
Remember one night, in his own house, an urgent
request coming for two pdunds.■ “ ‘Baby sick ;
wants medicine.’.. Must have it; but if baby
swallows all that, baby will be where the wicked
cease from troubling before very long. .. ‘ Knew
me in old Punch days.’... Ah, yes, I knew many
of them, and they cost me a little money,
but perhaps it did a little good. Poor old
Nomen was the worst (he never gave a man
away). Let us look up Nomen’s record. This is
the butt, I think. January ... yes. Baby born.
Five pounds. Poor baby dead; another five !
September; eldest child broke a leg. Never
believed a word of it; but still . Three more,
I think, in the next year; and then the Lord took
poor Nomen off my hands.”
Kendall, in the mediaeval period of Punch, had
a long paper on the Australian genius or the lack
of it wherein he made a strange assertion : “ The
reason why genius never proved its presence here
may be set down in half a dozen words —genius
never had a chance.” Um ! Punch was estab-
lished mainly to give genius a chance ; and, as it
was for a considerable period unedited, genius,
once admitted, had full fling. Naturally a good
deal of that cheap and common fustian which
masquerades as genius got in under those condi-
tions ; but still, the real stuff had a bit of a field,
and should have made its mark. Can anything
keep true volcanic genius down? Can any super-
incumbent weight render Enceladus motionless ?
Keats had the livery stable behind him, and
poverty all about, a drink passion plaguing him
frequently; Jeffrey’s ice-cold hand on his throat,
and Fanny Brawne on his chest. And yet amongst
all the stars of the firmament ot this world s song,
there are none which shine brighter than the
“Ode to Psyche,” and the “St. Agnes’ Eye.”
Burns went from the plough-tail to the odious
gauger’s work. Poe died at last with only his poor
girl’s petticoat between him and the bitter New
England winter, and yet he had done “The
Raven ” and many a weird melody which will
chime adown the ages. Ido not think such bitter
waters of circumstance were ever found as would
quench the fire kindled by a living coal from the
sole altar. And what then ? Have'the bearers of
the live coals not yet proceeded from the altar
hitherward ? Let the question stand. Ido not
care to venture on a positive reply. But this
much may be said, I do not find evidence affirma-
tive of the presence of the highest inspiration in
any of the pages of those days. Nor yet of such
flakes, parks, and wild-fire flashes as come to
those who know the Bush, and the fire in it
impressing occasionally a command imperative as
that which came unto Moses.
Punch, it may be said, is not the place for that
sort of thing ; but Sydney Punch, through some of
its periods, did certainly endeavour, or propose, to
constitute itself that place. Poor old Punch!
it was clean, it was honest, it was well-meaning.
But, on its own confession, it was generally
unedited. It was not Australian, it was generally,
indeed, contemptuous of things Australian. And
therefore Australia lives and has its being, not all
so bright or sympathetic perhaps as we might
desire ; and Punch is not.
Down amongst the dead men are its contribu-
tors —alas ! so many of them ; and more passing
day by day. It is one continuous harvest-time
with these few who are left. Every month,
almost, sees another sheaf bound ; and .God be
merciful in the threshing and the winnowing !
F.M.

BELT.
SUFFERED TERRIBLE AGONY.
From wearing an Electric Belt
charged by the use of
SULPHURIC 71CID.
Mr. W. Folley, Alfred Woollen Mills,
Williamstown, Vic., writing to the Dr.
McLaughlin Electric Belt Co., tells how he
was scarred and burned by the use of a
Free Belt , which he had erroneously
been led to believe was as good as theirs.
He paid £4 for the Free Belt because
he had been convinced that by paying
this cash price he was obtaining a similar
appliance to Dr. McLaughlin’s Famous
rejuvenator which has gained Wordd
Wide fame as a Curative Agent. It goes
to show how misleading are advertise-
ments which seem to offer something for
nothing.
Gentlemen —In answer to your kind in-
quiries after my health, I beg to state that
I have been completely cured of the Scia-
tica that has troubled me for years. I
cannot thank you enough for your gentle-
manly treatment and fair dealing with your
patients, as I know from others that their
treatment has been the same as mine.
I may just as well let you know my ex-
perience in seeking a cure for my trouble
before I purchased one of your High Grade
Belts. I had been under doctors and re-
ceived scant relief; and also tried various
quacks, but it was all the same. I then
placed myself in the hands of a Specialist
(in Melbourne) guaranteed to cure Rheu-
matism and Sciatica, but received very
small benefit from the treatment. I then
had mv attention drawn to Electricity.
Well, 1 thought, “ Never venture, never
win,” so I PROCURED first of all a Belt
for £l and was very much disappointed
because I was informed that it was neces-
sary to take a long and exceedingly ex-
pensive course of drug treatment. I would
not do this and then I wrote for, and ob-
tained a so-called Free Belt, with a
most “ Wonderful Pedigree,” at a cost of
£4. I wore the Belt according to the in-
structions given faithfully for a period of
five weeks, and by that time I was Burnt
and Blistered so badly that I could not
wear it longer, and still no relief was
gained, although the Belt was guaranteed
to Cure Anything in Sciatica in One
Month. You bet I was very much dis-
couraged in regard to Electric Belts.
However, I was persuaded to come and
just see you, gentlemen. I then found
that I had been wearing a Torture Belt
and very Wrongly Applied, and quite
enough to give and patient a Horror of
so-called Electric Belts. After the con-
sultation I decided to place myself in your
hands. I knew my case was very diffi-
cult, as I was compelled to follow my em-
ployment, and standing at the vice was
irksome, and the complaint was a very
stubborn one. However, gentlemen, your
Belt, coupled with your advice, has made
me a grateful patient. I thank you kindly
for supplying me with a remedy which has,
Completely Cured Me, and strongly recom-
mend it to all and sundry that I find are
suffering from the distressing malady-
called “ Sciatica.”
I have not been wearing the Belt for the
past fortnight, but have passed it over to
my wife, who has received great benefit
for nervous troubles.
I remain, your grateful patient,
W. FOLLEY

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Ragged Jack.”
ALL in a fair, wide, busy street,
Four old grey walls of stone
Stand grimly, amidst houses neat
And new—a ruin lone.
With golden hopes and visions fair
The Builder’s brain did glow
Who raised those old grey walls in air,
So many years ago.
And, as from day to day he wrought
Upon each rising wall,
He built therein his dearest thought,
And built his heart withal.
He was a lover of the great,
Stern type the old days knew,
With faith as fixed and firm as Fate,
And heart as heaven true.
They say that she was fair to see,
In years long past and dead,
Her yellow hair hung to her knee,
Her lips were scarlet-red.
And as the mirage, false and fair,
Lures lost men on to Death,
She lured him with her shining hair
And with her honeyed breath.
For he was then, as old folk tell,
A man of gallant show ;
With him the world went very well—
’Twas forty years ago.
Yea, young he was, and blithe, and gay,
And walked with springing tread ;
No brighter eyes than his, they say,
Could shine in any head.
And she had made a promise sweet
Which made him feel a king—
That, when the Home was built complete,
Their wedding-bells should ring.
And that was why with visions fair,
And hopes, his brain did glow
Who raised those old grey walls in air
So many years ago.
She left him for a lover old
And rich, of high degree.
The tale to him was swiftly told,
But never word spake he.
No word he spake to man or maid :
But on the building lone
One last stone in its place he laid —
And laid no other stone.
His face grew as the face of one
Who turns him to the wall
And dies ; for light of moon or sun
He cared no more at all.
He cared no more for gear or gold ;
He strove no more for gain ;
The man had suddenly grown old,
With broken heart and brain.
• » • * •
The ruin grim, and all forlorn,
Is slowly turning black ;
“ Rats’ Castle ” it is called in scorn—
Its tenant, “Ragged Jack.”
And there in one small squalid room—
It is a tale for tears —
That lover, in a living tomb,
Has dwelt for forty years.
O give to me the lightsome heart
That soon forgets its pain,
And laughs away the keenest smart—
And turns to love again !
But unto Nature all must bow—
She lias for .each her plan—
The grey old ruin shelters now
The old grey broken man.
N.S.W.
VICTOR J. DALEY.
At the River=Crossing.
OH ! the quiet river-crossing
Where we twain were wont to ride,
Where the wanton winds were tossing
Willow branches o’er the tic.e.
There the golden noon would find us
Dallying through the summer day,
All the weary world behind us—
All its tumult far away.
Oh ! those rides across the crossing
Where the shallow stream runs wide,
When the sunset’s beams were glossing
Strips of sand on either side.
We would cross the sparkling river
On the brown horse and the bay ;
Watch the willows sway and shiver
And their trembling shadows play.
When the opal tints waxed duller
And a gray crept o’er the skies
Yet there stayed the blue sky's color
In your dreamy dark-blue eyes.
How the sun-god’s bright caresses,
When we rode at sunset there,
Played among your braided tresses, -
Gleaming on your silky hair.
When at last the sunlight’s glory
Faded off the sandy bars,
There weTeanit the old, old story,
Riding homeward ’neath the stars.
Tis a memory to be hoarded—
Oh, the foolish tale and fond !
Till another stream be forded —
And we reach the Great Beyond.
N.S.W.

A DISMAL TIME.
Vitality Restored to
an Enfeebled System.
The Case of IV|r. A. W. LYON.
(by our special reporter.)
Among the instances in which an attempt
has been made to deal with questions attain-
ing a wide popularity is that appearing here-
under, and the facts that have been adduced
by Mr. Alfred William Lyon, of No. 56
Brougham-street, Glebe, will enable a fairly
intelligent man to come to a definite con-
clusion upon those phases of the subject
about which he may now be doubtful.
“I can trace the origin of my adversities
to a mishap which I encountered Whilst in
the act of taking a stone from the hoof of a
horse,” said Mr. Lyon to our reporter. “ As
I stooped to raise his hoof I received a kick
on the head, and although I was almost
stunned I managed to pick myself up, and
was making my escape when the horse
kicked me again, but that time on my
ribs.”
“It is a wonder you were not killed,”
interrupted the writer.
“It is, indeed ; but I was not out of
danger fora long time, as I was laid up for
fourteen weeks and became quite a wreck.
When at last my injuries were repaired I
went back to work, although I was almost
too weak to do so, and on the 20th March,
1599, I again met with another accident. I
was removing scenery and effects to a
theatre, and whilst taking a heavy box to
the cellar I severely strained myself. Mind
you, I felt pains about the kidneys from the
time I was first injured, but I did not think
they were in any way diseased. I thought
the pains were ihe direct result of the heavy
blow I had received, but the acute torture
which now caught me across the back made
me realise only too bitterly that my kidneys
were greatly disordered. When I stooped
I could not get up again, and had to sit
there. Such terrible agony I had never ex-
perienced before. 'I here was a sharp pain
in my side as if the flesh was being drawn
from me, and I could not remain in an up-
right posit ion for the excruciating tortures
that were bearing me down.”
“ I suppose you iviyre compelled to leave
your woi k again ?’ > gfe
“ Indeed I was, afifl was so helpless that
for three weeks I could not leave my bed. I
really thought it was all over with me, my
parents also being deeply concerned at the
ten ible condition I was in. You see, I fell
aw ay in flesh so rapidly that at the end of
the third week after my accident I was two
stone lighter. There is no doubt that I was
just like a shadow, and so miserable at
being ill and unable to do my work that I
did not have a moment’s cheerfulness of
mind either day or night. I never had the
least desire for food, the smell of which
turned me sick, and if by any chance 1 did
succeed in getting anything down, every bit
of it came back again, for I hardly
remember a meal which was not soon
followed by a v olent fit of vomiting. That
was the main reason why I become so fright-
fully thin, but another cause of the hopeless
feeling which weighed on my spirits was
that I could not get a quarter of the amount
of sleep I requited. Through lying down
all day my body was so racked with aches
that I could never feel comfortable, bub
occasionally I became so overcome with
fatigue that I dozed off in spite of all my
tortures, and even then I was not much
better off, as I always had horrible dreams
that woke me up with a trembling start.
VV oat a dismal life it was to be sure ! My
head ached to such a maddening extent that
I could not bear the weight of my hair, so I
had it shaved off. If I stood up I felt top-
heavy and dizzy, and had to hold on to
something tc steady myself, while the
swollen feeling about my eyes was so dis-
tracting that sometimes I could hardly see.
This dreadful state of affairs went on for
seventeen or eighteen iron hs, increased
| *uffering being my portion from day to
I day ; and I can tell you I was thoroughly
sick and tired of it all. My stomach
| bulged out with wind, which also
gave me a lot of pain, and many
an anxious fear I have had through my heart
palpitating to excess. I ceased to care how
things would end, for I was almost too weak
to hope for a recovery, and everything
possible seemed to go against me. Why,
even my taste was destroyed by a sense of
bitterness which would not go away, and
my tongue was so rough that it was more
like a brush than anything else. The
medicines I took to remove my painful
symptoms were not the least good to me,
and when there was not a thread of hope to
cling to I realised what an awful thing it is
to suffer in despair. I gave up asking
advice, but the best I had yet received came
when it waB most urgently required.”
“ What advice was that ? ”
“It was to take Clements Tonic, and as I
was willing to take anything at all, I bought
some—not that I thought it would cure
me ; but when I had drank one bottle I wa3
certainly no worse, so I purchased a couple
more. Then 1 seemed to liven up a bit, as
though Clements Tonic had put some
vitality into my enfeebled system, and in
another week I found that this idea wa3
right, sure enough. The suffering I had
endured all these months was undoubtedly
minimised, the pains in my back being won-
derfully relieved, and, to my utter astonish-
ment, iny appetite came back. Very little
more time elapsed before the vomiting
troubles had entirely ceased, and from then
I moved along quickly to the recovery of
my health, which nothing but Clements
Tonic could bring about. I felt as jubilant
as any man could be, and who won hi not
feel so at such a surprising liberation from
physical captivity! A new and happy
existence came to me by using Clements
Tonic, which rectified dizziness, headaches,
and stomach pains so surely that they have
never troubled me since, and by soothing
and quietening my nerves I gained so much
comfort from the use of that medicine that
I was soon able to sleep as soundly as I
could when a boy at school. My mouth
was freed from its nasty taste, my tongue
was cleaused, and in all respects I gained
by using Clements Tonic, even to being
released from the pains in my side and
chest and having my heart affections
abolished. I was soon able to go back to
work, but my employer did not know me, I
had altered so ; but by sticking to Clements
Tonic the lines of suffering were obliterated
from my face, and my body again filled out
with flesh.”
“ Did Clements Tonic cure you thorough-
“ I can conscientiously say it did, and as
I am sure Clements Tonic is the most
reliable medicine in the country, I always
recommend it to my friends, and you may
publish our conversation in any way you
want.”

THURSDAY, DECEMBER 24, 1903.
The Triumph—and Where New South
Wales Doesn’t Come In.
The Australian election is ovei’, and it leaves
things, on the whole, better than they were. The
Deaktn Ministry, unless it quarrels with the
Labor party, still stands four-square against the
winds of heaven. If the Labor party is elimi-
nate!, theDEAKiN party is distinctly stronger than
the Reid party. Therefore, even if the Labor
party shcu'rl. by some improbable combination of
events, transfer its alliance to the Reid party —
through Reid repeating his famous offers to
abandon Freetrade, and to pass a strong
Arbitration Bill, or for some other cause—a
Reid Ministry wouldn’t be so strong as the
Deaktn Ministry is now, and it would depend
more upon Labor support, and would have to
grovel to Labor very earnestly indeed to hold
office—even more earnestly than when the grovel-
ling of N.S.W. State Premier Reid to Labor-leader
M ‘Go wen was the jest and scoff of the Sydney
press But though the figures have not
shifted materially in the aggregate, the balance
of power as among the States has shifted to a
certain extent. New South Wales has become a
shade more Tory, more reactionary, and more
Freetrade —that curious variety of Freetrade
which consists in damning Protection and yet
practising it to a considerable extent, and shriek-
ing about the blessedness of Freetrade, yet pro-
mising not to provide the blessings if the party
gets into office. Regarded as a victory for Free-
trade, the proceedings in N.S.W. were a dire
failure, for every Freetrade candidate, without
exception, secured his seat by promising or imply-
ing that he would not practise Freetrade if he got
into office. The man who declared that he yearned
after the blessings of pure Freetrade was
everywhere. The man who declared that he
would vote for having the alleged blessings—for
abolishing the Protection granted to wine, beer,
sugar, tobacco, and everything else, for abolishing
the preferential railway rates in favor of local
agricultural produce, for raising the bulk of the
revenue by direct taxation as in England, and for
letting in the rjfreshing breeze of free competi-
tion, and making the country rich and prosperous
with his blastiferous nostrum—he was absolutely
nowhere. But though the N.S.W. vote was not
much of a success for Freetrade, it was a consider-
able victory for parochialism, for internal
jealousy, and for the campaign of lies, slanders,
and misrepresentations which the Reid party
conducted. Also, the ineffective Yellow Pup of
sectarianism had a look in and gained very little
thereby.
* * * *
The greater part of N.S.W. decided, by its
vote, to t>3 still the lone, sulky, separate, sore-
headed minority in the Australian Parliament.
Australia is still Protectionist—a shade more
Protectionist than before; but though N.S.W.
asked the other States to coalesce with it in the
first place, and did so with a full knowledge of
their policy, the dominant faction of the State
still reviles that policy, and reviles its partners,
and insists on being’ the lank and dismal
exception weeping by itself in the corner.
Therefore Victoria, as the State which supplies
the strongest contingent to the ruling party,
will, of necessity, run the show to a very great
extent. While N.S.W. insists on wailing by
itself on a back seat things can’t be otherwise.
The majority must rule, and the State which
supplies the largest section of the majority
must do most of the ruling. The principal
change is that N.S.W. has decided to keep
rather further aloof, to be more of the solitary
irreconcilable, to howl more lamentably in its
corner. By declaring itself more bitterly hostile
to the other States than it was before, it has com-
pelled the other States to be more definitely a
coalition, in the interests of Australia, against
N.S.W. Where there are five men and a gorilla,
the_ five men are naturally one party and the
gorilla, the other, and a majority in N.S.W., under
the guidance of Geokge Reid and the Telegraph
and Herald, has resolved to be the gorilla.
When N.S.W. decides against the policy of the
great majority, then the only way that Australia
can be governed is by shutting N.S.W. almost
completely out of the real work of governing
Australia. The minority has to expect that sort
of thing.
* * * *
It is not only on the fiscal question that N.S.W.
has done its best to cut itself adrift from Austra-
lian sentiment. N.S.W. was the first State to
adopt the Education Test for the exclusion of
black aliens and foreign criminals, and when
George Reid passed his famous law to that effect
it cheered wildly about its advanced and pro-
gressive legislation. Then the Commonwealth
adopted and improved and honestly enforced the
old N.S.W. law, with the cordial support (at the
time) of the Reid party. But because the Deakin
Government was Protectionist and was supported
by Victoria, the Herald and Telegraph and the
politically infamous George Reid told N.S;W., a
little later, to denounce the law it itself intro-
duced to Australia, and to declare it a
degrading Victorian outrage on the “liberty of
the subject.” And a very large section of
N.S.W., with a servility which was beyond all de-
scription, obeyed. It gave a Black Labor vote and
a vote for the free introduction of foreign criminals
—at least, its vote goes that way, if the men it
returned have the courage of their opinions. The
progressive party, the party which wants to keep
Australia white, to keep out foreign criminals,
and to keep down foreign borrowing, has its
strength mostly in Queensland, Victoria, Westralia
and S.A., but in this respect also N.S.W. has
decided to be, in the main, a lone and .cantan-
kerous item sobbing in an angle of the premises.
The ruling section in N.S.W. has decided to
keep so strictly aloof from the policy of the rest
of Australia, that either N.S.W. must govern the
other five States or the other five States must
govern N.S.W., and, as the former is impossible,
the latter is inevitable. The only way in which
N.S.W. can get any reasonable share in the work
of governing Australia is by giving up its role of
antiquated and obstructive hermit in a cave, and
deciding to be an ordinary fellow creature.
» * * *
Some other curiously inverted results of the
attitude of N.S.W. are the strengthening of the
Federal Labor party and the splitting up of the
Yellow Pup vote. The Labor party dominates the
House of Representatives solely by reason of the
cantankerous and secluded minority from N.S.W.
It was a remarkable result to be achieved by the
bigoted Toryism of Bruce Smith and the retro-
gression of Reid and the antiquated fossilism of
the Herald and Telegraph, but they arrived at
the result most successfully. While a solid
N.S.W. phalanx of Freetraders (who don’t believe
in Freetrade) sits, wrapped in gloom, on the Oppo-
sition benches, Labor will hold the casting vote
and run the show—if it wants to run it. There is
no hope that Protectionists who follow their belief
will give it up in deference to the sham Free-
tr iders who haven’t enough faith in their own
creed to use it when they are in power; so, while
the faction of Reid, and the two Smiths, and the
two Sydney Freetrade dailies which are so mad at
losing their own 50 per cent, of Protection, prevails
in N.S.W., the breach is irreparable. And while
that breach remains no party can form or maintain
a Ministry except by the aid of the Labor mem-
bers, and on their terms—if they like to impose
terms. The Bulletin likes this arrangement as
little as does Bruce Smith and co., but for a
different reason. It wants to see the Labor party
develop into a National party, get a wider and
more progressive platform, and take the responsi-
bility of forming a Government itself, instead of
being merely a tail and selling its support in
return for concessions. But meanwhile the
present arrangement is something to go on with ;
and the fact that the power of the Labor party
arises out of the dull incapacity of the nigger-
loving and Tory Bruce Smith, and the blindness
of the party that howls along with him against
“ Labor domination, ” is something that is calcu-
lated to make the gods look down on the creeping
myriads of Smiths in wonderment.
* * • •
The Dill Macky faction in N.S.W. managed
its affairs as badly as the rest. It threw in its lot
with the importers, and lent its Bible and its
alleged deity and its Dill to the party which
seeks to deprive Australia’s mostly-Protestant
workman of a job, in order that the much more
Catholic workman outside may supply the
goods instead and make the profits. Now,
as a consequence, the N.S.W. members who
arrived on the Protestant ticket must sit in
opposition to the much larger Protestant
section from the other States. They help
to form the sore-headed minority from the
least Protestant State of the Union, and help it
to curse the Protestant Deakin Government and
the strongly-Protestant States of Victoria and
S.A. from which that Government derives the
bulk of its direct support. The Protestant vote
is split in two; the bulk of it is Protectionist, but
the Freetrade section of it from N.S.W. helps to
nullify that. If the Dill Mackyists from N.S.W.
were a Catholic conspiracy, formed to hold
N.S.W., the most Catholic of the States, aloof
from the more Protestant States of the South, and
to create all possible ill-feeling against these
southern States, and to do its utmost to drag
down the present strongly Protestant Govern-
ment, and to promote the interests of foreign
Catholics in Australia, the position would be un-
derstandable. As it is, it seems to show that in-
telligence is one thing and beautiful clerical calves
are quite another.
* * * *
One thing which seems reasonably certain is
that the recent electoral campaign will give a bad
set-back to the attempt to fix the Federal capital
at Lyndhurst, or anywhere else near Sydney.
That campaign has made Sydney appear, in the
eyes of Australia at large, as the most degraded
and morally infected spot in the Commonwealth.
Its morning press carried on the campaign with a
wealth of curse and vituperation directed at other
States in general, and at Victoria in particular,
which had no parallel. The always unscru-
pulous Telegraph, and the once pious but
now degraded S.M. Herald, went, so far
in their campaign of lies and frenzied mis-
representations as to almost directly charge
Premier Deakin with murder, on the strength
of weird insinuations about the imaginary
drowning of some shipwrecked colored sailors,
said to have been refused the right to land
on this inhospitable shore because of the Aliens
Anti-immigration Law. The same lie was
circulated in other places, but nowhere was it
told with such rage and fury and circumstance as
in Sydney, and in no place was it reiterated so
persistently by excited politicians, and in no place
was it accompanied by such violent denunciation
of the sister States of the Union. The invitation
to fix the Federal city as close as possible to
Sydney must now appear to the other States
merely as an invitation to fix it within con-
venient mud-throwing distance of the most
coarsely virulent, degraded, and unscrupulous
press in Australia. It is an invitation to come as
near as possible to Dill Macky. It is especially
an invitation to the Victorian members, who form
the bulk of the Government following—and who
do so simply because N.S.W., in the main, is
resolved to be an angry and miserable person
sulking in a corner—to place themselves within
easy hearing distance of a press which regards
“ Victorian ” as the worst epithet it can throw at
its enemy. Unless some complete political revo-
lution upsets the present order of things, and
turns everybody’s ideas into a new channel,
Sydney, with its black labor politicians, and its
howl for the free admission of foreign criminals,
.and its morning press which hardly stops short of
false accusations of murder in order to suppress
a political opponent, will probably be regarded as
a place from which the capital of a progressive
White Australia should be as far removed as
possible.
• * * *
And the most unaccountable circumstance is
that all this frenzy on behalf of Freetrade is
raised by a party which, j udged by its record, would
sooner die than practise its alleged gospel or ex-
perience the alleged blessings of its faith. It was
in power in N.S.W. for nearly half-a-century,
and the highly-Protective tariff of Bruce
Smith and M'Millan was a shocking thing for
any pure Freetrader to look at. Even under
Reid N.S.W. raised £700,000 a year by Protective
duties, and spent about £500,000 a year in bonuses,
direct and indirect, to local industries. Every
Freetrade candidate at the recent election appealed
to the people on the ground that he wasn’t going
to practise pure Freetrade, that he wasn’t going
to make the country great and rich and pros-
perous by the full application of his beneficent
gospel, that he knew how to save the country and
would see the country hanged first, that he
wouldn’t “throw the tariff into the melting pot,”
but only “prune off excrescences” and admit
niggers and foreign criminals. If the dominant
F reetrade party amon g N. S. W. poli tici ans believed
in Freetrade and desired to get into power
in order to adopt Freetrade, the position would
be at least intelligible. But the members of that
party demand that they should be put into office
in order to adopt a large measure of Protection,
and yet insult Protectionists, and declare falsely
that any prosperity which follows their semi-Pro-
tective policy is the result of Freetrade. The
whole position is unintelligible, except on the
assumption that enough Freetrade is wanted to
let M‘Millan’s calico in free, and that two
infuriated morning papers would turn Australia
upside-down in order to recover the Protection of
their gratis postage.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Election Slanders.
On the morning of election day Sydney Tele-
graph did a thing that was refreshing in its
ingenuousness. It headed the effort “Election
Slanders,” and proceeded thus :
The heat engendered by a keenly-contested election is
generally held to excuse a certain amount of tongue-
license which under other circumstances would not be
permitted. It would be useless at any time to complain
of that. But there is a limit to even this, and when that
limit is wilfully and persistently transgressed, the effect
in any wholesome-minded community will always be to
injure the side on which such tactics are employed. In
two metropolitan constituencies personal slander has
this time been used against the selected Freetrade can-
didates to an extent that has, fortunately, been very
seldom witnessed in the bitterest election contests
known to our history. The efforts made at Lang to stab
Mr. Johnson by every kind of false and disgraceful
innuendo furnish lamentably conspicuous examples of
this fact. ... In South Sydney, also, this kind of fight-
ing has been largely availed of throughout the whole
campaign. ... It is gratifying that on the Freetrade
side none of these degrading election devices have been
resorted to. Everywhere, as far as reports go, the elec-
tion has been clean.
This weep from such a source is a unique speci-
men of moral dampness. The Telegraph and
some other papers, but more especially the
Telegraph, went further in the way of foul and
dirty-minded personal slander than was probably
ever before known in Australia. They got as
near as they could well venture to accusing every
member of the Australian Government of cold-
blooded murder. When the colored seamen of a
stranded ship were taken off in safety, well cared
for, and transferred to a vessel bound homewards,
they shrieked in a carefully-selected tone till they
really persuaded ignorant people that these
shipwrecked Asiatics had striven to swim ashore
in a raging sea, and were drowned because
the Commonwealth Ministers refused to
let them land. If a carefully-implied charge of
brutal murder wasn’t something in the way of
“ personal slander ” it is difficult to imagine what
is. The Freetrade papers, and sundry poiliticans
of their way of thinking, carried personal slander
to an extent that should put them out of the
pale of decent society. Behind every paper which
lies and maligns there is somewhere the personal
and individual liar and slanderer, the hired per-
verter and anonymous slayer of character. So
long as decent men will take that individual’s
unpleasant hand, and so long as decent women
will associate with him, Australia isn’t likely to
have any more honorable press than it has. And
some sections of the present press could hardly
be more dishonorable if they tried.

The Crains and the Losses.
Nowadays fights end, for one’s own side, in only
two ways—in Victory or Moral Victory. The
main difference between them is that about three
lines of figures suffice to prove the one; the other
calls for a lively imagination and several columns
to put it in, and the Foreign-trade papers
have supplied both. All the elocpience called for
by a victory such as that which was won last
week by the Federal Labor party, can, on the
other hand, be put as briefly as follows:
Labor. Opp. Minis. Total.
Old M’s.P. Missing ..1 11 9 21
New M’s.P 13 6 2 21
Gain 12 Loss B Loss 7
But to a very great extent Laborites .and Minis-
terialists faced Australia together, so they share
the victory. Necessarily it had to be so; they
were allies before the elections, and the Minis-
terial programme was based upon the alliance
continuing. Hence an Oppositionist, but no
Ministerialist, opposed Labor-leader Watson.
No Ministerialist, except Glassey, opposed the
Labor three in Queensland, and Glassey went
upon his own responsibility. It was the same in
South Australia and Westralia. Wherever the vote
would be split and the seat imperilled, no Minis-
terialist was run. This accounts for the some-
what heavy Ministerial losses as compared with
Ministerial gains; it also, to some extent, accounts
for the remarkable Labor gains. The figures of
the two parties can only be sundered, therefore,
by using some violence. Reckoned together, as
they must be, the allied party has gained one
seat from the Foreign-trade and Colored
Labor party in the Representatives and
four seats in the Senate. In'' 'the few
cases where there was no dange. of letting
a Reidite in, and where Laborites and Minis-
terialists therefore met, the verdict in almost
every case was in favor of the Laborites'
more advanced policy. In Victoria the Senate
contest resolved itself into something more even
than this. It was the first chance the State
had to say what it thought of all those forces
which lately gathered round Irvine to squeeze
the railway men, to wrong the ‘civil servants, to
oppress the aged poor, and to dodge land-taxa-
tion ; and the answer was a magnificent triumph
for the enemies of Irvineism. For Syme it was
an even more bitter pill. With all the
strength of the Age, lie strove to force upon
the State the politically decrepit M’Culloch;
and then he had the humiliation to find the hated
Trenwith poll two to M‘Cullooh’s one. The
Argus four fell in even more badly, but then they
were regarded as a cumbrous joke from the first.
Queensland spoke most plainly on the colored
labor question ; and, knowing it best, it thundered
against it. John Murray, who announced that
he had never believed in a White Australia and
never would, was left, among the party candi-
dates, hopelessly at the bottom of the Senate
poll. That was Australia’s answer to his chal-
lenge. In Westralia and S. Australia the position
was almost the same; there, with a White
Australia, was pressed home hard the need
of financial straight-dealing. In S. Aus-
tralia more ought to have been done.
Every contested seat went to Laborites —three
places in the Senate and two in the Represen-
tatives ; and yet three Reidites were permitted to
go back unchallenged. The Laborites here suf-
fered, perhaps, from excessive modesty. They
did not realise how deep an impression, not
only their own Federal men, but their clean,
honest, straight-going, hard-fighting little State
Labor party had made. And it was in this very
respect that the party was handieappedin N.S.W.
Admirable as the record of the Federal Labor
party is, the people,_ many of them but newly
enfranchised, judged its candidates and its calibre
only by the State Labor party. And that was both
unfair and disastrous to the Federal party.
The gross result is that, while no single party has
a majority in either House, Labor has in the
Senate a solid 14 out of 36 members. With
R. C. Baker in the chair, and Trenwith
and Styles incapable of joining a Tot}’
alliance, there would need to be a combination
of every other member in the House to make a
majority against it. In practice, therefore, no
Federal legislation can now be carried against the
Labor party’s will. This is power; and it is also
responsibility. But, on the party’s past record,
The Bulletin isn’t afraid.

The Unchaining' of the Jabberwock.
The bitterest yowl of the election campaign was
the letter of Johnny Haynes, M.L. A., to Sydney
Telegraph, stating that N.S.W. was a betrayed
and flabbergasted State, and that the jabberwock
had broken loose ; and calling a meeting to
arrange for secession. The following few re-
marks represent John at his best :
The worst fears of those who were opposed three years
ago to the Federal scheme have been more than realised.
New South Wales has been voted down by State or colony
compact, and the heel of arrogance, ignorance, and in-
justice is upon us.
In the Senate our last vestige of hope is swept from us,
and we, already racked by political charlatanism, are
faced by demoniacal Socialism, which threatens to
paralyse our commerce and isolate us from kith and kin
across the seas.
It is proposed now that New South Wales, so infam-
ously abused after her magnificent generous surrenders
in the cause of union, shall withdraw from the Federa-
tion and start the work of natural unification under a
Federal Council Bill.
Many believe we should move at once, strike for a
restoration of our political rights and a re-establishment
of our former union with the mother country.
That represents the bursting forth of the jabber-
wock in his most violent form. But it is pai ifully
vague, and the “heel of arrogance ” is left in a
most undefined state. Presumably N.S.W. knew,
when it called on the other States to come and
join it, that the majority would rule, and Johnny
Haynes and his friends have persuaded most of
N.S.W. to return a lone Freetrade minority.
Premier Deakin would probably be pleased to
ask Pumpkin Smith to join the Ministry if
Pumpkin would fall in with the Ministry’s views,
but as he ivon’t the only thing is to change the
views—which Australia won’t allow—or do
without Pumpkin, and wait till N.S.W. sends
in a different kind of Pumpkin. As for
the “magnificent generous” surrenders which
N.S.W. made in the cause of union, they
are as undefined as the heel of arrogance and
the rest of the boojums and snarks of John’s
jolted .dream. It would be better, on the whole,
if the secessionists would begin by drawing up an
exact statement of their grievances, leaving out
the heel of arrogance, and the boots of oppression,
and other undefined articles of “clobber,” and
sticking to dull, wooden information. It should
set forth: —
The amount of commerce N.S.W. has lost.
The date when the Commonwealth broke “ the former
union with the mother country,” together with a diagram
of the breakage.
The number of its subjects who have been hanged
unjustly by Premier Deakin, or cast into a noisome,
toadful dungeon by George Turner, and how P. Frs:i
has gibbered at them through the bars.
The amount of land or money or goods stolen from
N.S.W. by the Commonwealth contrary to the consti-
tution. , , , ,
The extent to which N.S.W. has been paralysed and
had its communication cut off with its kin across the sea.
(The recent foreign criminal at Newcastle doesn’t come in
Any miscellaneous information that may be available
about the demoniacal aspect of Chris. Watson and the
cannibalistic and blood-drinking tendencies of William
Trenwith.


Sample of the State’s lost ‘‘political rights” on a
shovel O' some other handy implement.
There is nothing like getting your facts in a, hard,
precise form, like ironmongery, before letting the
kraken of your wild eloquence loose and unchain-
ing the promiscuous fantod or Juggernaut of
imagination. A plain list of the State’s lost pro-
perty may not be so picturesque as the wail of the
banshee of loose denunciation and the clattering
of its chains up and down the corridors of John’s
mind, but it is much more valuable as a clue. One
of the finest things in literature is Clarence’s
dream of the horrors of the bottom of the sea, but
no sensible policeman ever took it out with him
as a guide when he went looking for the em-
bezzled goods, or the person who did the assault.

Cheap liahor.
“ Bushman ” from Tasmania :
I am sending you a few facts—facts, mind you, from
the way-back farming districts of Tasmania.
First re wages. Most of my life has been spent on the
west, coast of Tassy ; the wild West, where men are free
and independent; good wages, unionism, and all that.
(Things are getting altered now.) Last year fate landed
me among the cookies. I was mesmerised. I had always
thought there was no slavery under the British flag.
I found among these way-back cookies, slavery pure and
simple ; ignorance, black as night; greed and selfishness—
the blind selfishness of the peasant. They are taught
from childhood by the object lesson of the'lives of their
elders to cheat and rob their neighbors—“ dealing ” they
call it. They have, as a class, no conception of the true
qualities of manhood, honesty, truth, unselfishness. A
few exceptions there are. Here and there a man, but
few—very few. Now for some facts from a farming
district not 100 miles from Hobart.
The rate of wages for single men is—tucker and ss.
(sometimes 65.) a week. I know a dozen young men
between the ages of 21 and 25 —lusty young giants, in the
prime of life, great toilers, fine axemen—working for
their tucker, such as it is, and ss. weekly. Barely tucker
and clothes 1 And, when I say working, I mean working,
before daylight until long after dark, and working the
best part of Sunday Fact. One case in particular:
Young fellow (21) started to work for cocky at grubbing
by the week. Five bob and tucker (tucker mostly rabbit
stew). Cocky told him he could light a big fire to see
'3 chop logs until it was light enough to grub. I
asked him if he was going to take it. Said he, “Yes,
and glad to get it 1 ” He put in three years at five bob
and rabbit stew ; then he got a rise—six bob and rabbit
stew. Here’s another case : Married man (ploughman),
getting rations and Bs. a week. He was frightened of
losing his job, as another married, man had offered to do
the same work for 7s. ! These are facts, mind you. No
hearsay ; what I’ve seen myself; I can give name, date,
and place. Here’s the masterpiece : Young cocky (aged
22), out of work ; looking round for job. Cocky told him
“he didn’t really want a man, but, if he liked, he’d put
him on and give him enough to keep him—a shilling a
day.” Said the cocky, “ It’s better than tramping about
the country.” He took it.
Now, what chance have these young cockies to develop
into men—genuine men—who take an interest in the
welfare of their fellow men? Not a minute to themselves.
Eat, drink, sleep (a little), work—weary, dreary drudgery
- from Christmas to Christmas. What’s the difference
between them and the bullocks they “ punch?”
How the cookies intermarry. What will be the end of
it? Mostly big families. They marry and intermarry,
and keep on intermarrying. Do they know the result?
Is it pure ignorance ?
Marvellous the number of young men and young
women in Tassy who can’t read and write. I’ve dropped
acre#’dozens—aye, scores

The New Labor Programme.
“ P.H. ” on Laborites and the elections:
“ A Pretty Good Democrat” (B. 26/10/’O3) discourses
ineffectively on, perhaps, the most difficult and urgent
problem of the political Labor movement, namely, how to
deal with the class of politicians who are, allegedly, “ as
Food as a Labor man.” Unquestionably, the present rela-
I.ons of the party to unattached advanced Democrats
like Kingston, Turner, Hume Cook, Crouch, Mauger,
Toucher, Trenwith, etc., are unstable. Before any
considerable reforms are possible, a working agree-
ment must be arrived at between these parties. Mean-
while the reactionaries thrive. With intimate knowledge
of the labor leaders, I affirm that the greatest
anxiety exists among them concerning the division
of the Democratic forces; but at present they
see no means of uniting them. The suggestion
that privateers like Miss Goldstein, Mauger, Higgins,
etc., should be exempt from opposition without giving
a pledge is absurd. Every one knows that effective
use of representative assemblies can only be made by
parties, and there cannot be a party without a pledge.
Independents are like so many rounded pebbles rolling
down a rivulet, split up or united by the obstacles in
their course, and not by inherent qualities. If the
Political Labor Council officially recognised one
independent Labor candidate, at least half the
present Labor Ms.P. would claim exemption from
the pledge. And yet while the P.L.C. is constituted as
at present, such men as Kingston, Turner, and Higgins
will never pledge themselves to it. Nor is there any
other organisation to which they can pledge themselves.
The P.L.0., which frames the Labor platform and selects
the Labor candidates through its branches, is not quite
democratic in composition. It is composed of one dele-
gate from each affiliated trade-union and one from each
affiliated branch. The affiliation fee is £1 per annum.
There must be only one branch in each State electorate
Therefore a trade-union with 20 members has power in
the Council equal to that of the largest electorate.
Plainly a new political organisation is wanted. Why not
a national organisation on the English plan with a branch
in each electorate, defining its policy at an annual con-
ference, and exacting a pledge from every candidate?
To-day such an organisation would find a majority in
Australia to support the following programme :
1. Australia First.
2. No more Borrowing (except for Conversion).
3. Protection. '
4. Industrial Arbitration.
5. Old Age Pensions.
6. Citizen Army, and Arms and Ammunition Factories.
7. Control of Monopolies.
8. Closer Settlement.
9. Honest Administration.
TO. An Australian Navy.
To such a platform every democrat could subscribe. To
such an organisation every candidate could pledge
himself with confidence, and without loss of dignity.
United under one banner, the democrats would overawe
the daily press and command its support, instead of, as
now, quailing under its lash. United, they could carry
at least two or three important measures each year. If
such an organisation failed to absorb the Political Labor
Council at once, the confluence of the two bodies would
ensue in a few years, provided the new one truly repre-
sented mass interests as against class interests.

A Name for the Federal City.
“ Australian ” writes
When the Federal capital is decided upon, it behoves
men and women of Australian sentiment to be alert lest,
ere they know it, the Commonwealth is inflicted with a
King Edward’s City or an Alexandraville. It is significant
that every chief city in Australasia save one is named
after some Lord or Lady on the other side of the sea who
probably knew little and cared less about the budding
centre christened in his or her honor than the most
reckless and least reputable of the colonists. Thus
we have Sydney, Hobart, Melbourne, Perth, and
Adelaide in the Commonwealth, and Wellington
and Auckland in Maoriland. Brisbane got its title
from an Australian Governor who happened to be
in office when it was founded. The little influence
that over-sea officers of State or local governors have on
popular sentiment is proved by the fact that it is exceed-
ingly seldom that anything of a territorial character is
named after them when they have vacated their posi-
tions. Every city in Australasia would have a different
designation had its foundation been a few years earlier or
a few years later. There ought to be some more defined
reason for giving the Commonwealth capital its name.
The United States had an easy task, comparatively, in
naming Washington after “the Father of his country.”
Canada was in a less clear position, and so adopted
a pretty native-named centre. What will Australia do ?
If it is impossible for Parliament to select one pioneer
who stands out prominently from his fellows* and who at
the same time has a euphonious patronymic, it should
either adopt an appropriate native title, or some abstract
designation suggestive of Federal aspirations or achieve-
ments. It cannot be pretended that any British person-
ae® n°w in office had anything to do with the foundation
of the Australian nation, so they can all be wiped off the
slate without compunction. It would be absurd to honor
a trans-oceanic potentate, however exalted, simply
because he was on hand when the task of choosing a title
was exercising Australian minds. One would feel in-
clined to suggest Cook, but future historians might think
it referred to Representative Joe instead of the great
explorer. The exact name, however, is not of so great
importance as the principle that it should have regard
solely to someone to whom the cause of Australian unity
is under a debt of gratitude, or should in some other
definite manner reflect Australian sentiment, pure and
undefiled. Even Tumut, concerning which the London
papers made so merry when the likelihood of its selection
was first mentioned, would be better than Hopetoun,
Tennyson, Northcote, Edwardville, Balfour, Chamber-
lain, or Lyttelton

Bullying from the Bench.
Judge Rogers appears to have used the whip in
a remarkable fashion at Wyalong (N.S.W )
Quarter Sessions lately. Two young men were
charged on four counts. The jury went out at
three in the afternoon and at 10 next morning was
still not agreed. If a jury couldn’t agree after 19
hours it would seem a fair thing to try a second
one; the judge, however, sent it back for another
iP e l * ■A-* 11.10 the 12 returned, and announced
that they had got so far as to agree to convict on
two counts. Apparently, under the whip of the
judge they had compromised—some of them bad
agreed to find that the prisoners weren’t as guilty
as they really believed them, while others
who presumably didn’t believe them guilty at all
were prepared to find them guilty of some small
thing m order to get the business over. How-
ever unsatisfactory such a result, it was quite in
form ; the Crown had charged the men on four
counts, and it was open to the jury to find them
guilty on any one of them. The Judge, however,
evidently had his own opinions; wherefore,'
according to the local newspaper report, he told
the jurymen who had been locked up for now
close upon 24 hours—
It was one of the most extraordinary things he had
ever seen. It seamed doubtful to him whether Wyalomr
should be continued as a Criminal Court. It was a
disgrace to the place. If he had to make a recommenda-
tion in this respect he knew what he would say.
And he sent them back again, practically with
what they must have regarded as a threat, that if
they didn t find the men guilty there should be no
more Criminal Courts at Wyalong, if he could
stop them. It was no longer, therefore, a question
with this jury, which had already come to a
decision, whether the prisoners were guilty or
not; the Judge had imported quite a new con-
sideration into the case. And, thus harried, they
came back with a verdict of guilty. The Judge
may have been quite right; the men may have
been guilty. But that doesn’t justify the Judge’s
threats. If the jury happened to be the local
publicans, and they didn’t like the Judge’s law
and told him that if he didn’t alter it they
wouldn t supply him with a meal or a bed or a
drink again in Wyalong, and would compel him
to camp out in the scrub, there would probably be
a row. Yet what the Judge did to the jury was
just, the same as that. And there isn’t any row
Junes are very meek animals.

Irvine’s Miscalculation.
There is a great art in knowing when to sell out,
to get in out of the wet, to finish or leave off, to
retire with dignity, to get from under, and to do
all the other things that tend to the passing on
of the responsibility. The quality of judicious
absence is one of the best that almost any man
can have; next to the quality of not being born at
xu’j.'V s the best. The amount of misery
that hasn t happened to the person who wasn’t
born has only been equalled by the number of
bricks which haven’t dropped on the head of the
man who was away. Premier Irvine, of Victoria
who has decided not to resign after all, has evi-
dently failed to assimilate these great lessons
He doesn t know when to cultivate a well-selected
absence. Six months ago he could have got
away with some kind of a reputation. To-day he
could get away uninjured and with most of his
clothes. Twelve months hence, on present ap-
pearances, he will be glad to get away anyhow,
bo far as he has got, he has managed to make the
railway deficit about £200,000 a year worse than
it was before. He started with a promise to
reduce the number of railway employees and
declared they were far too numerous, and he has
actually increased them. He contrived, in an
idiotic and wanton quarrel, to get rid of a huge
proportion of the trained drivers, and millions of
pounds worth of fcho fot&to’s valuable property is
now bemg driven to destruction by ignorant louts
of the. worst description. The biggest harvest
Victoria ever had is in prospect, and also the
biggest block and breakdown, and the biggest
shriek of rage from the farming classes.
1b has already been found necessary to
increase freights to make up for the losses created
by sheer muddle. Turner’s sinking fund has
been stolen and spent. The revenue is still
falling. Bent’s policy of giving free passes, good
for nine years, to suburban jerry-builders, has
become a joke. The £5,000,000 loan was an
appalling failure. The closer settlement policy
has apparently collapsed. Irvine’s one-time
strongest supporter, the Age, now calls him a
failure and almost calls him a lunatic. And
Irvine, who had a chance to get away and leave
someone else to face the railway block and the
enraged farmers, and the looming deficit and all
the other consequences, has decided to 'stay and
let the country see what a complete and monu-
meritai failure he is—to stay till the fowl, already
visible in the distance, comes home to roost
Certainly the calm person doesn’t know the art of
leaving off.

assurance is loaded with; and to follow the
standT a°nar a t SS T ment ° ffic ? S \ Life assurance
stands apart from practically everv other
sort of investment. It is a provision made
deliberately and specifically for one’s old age or
™ S ':' do ; or children, and is often made bymen
who can afford no other provision. And it 'is not
an investment which a man can make whenever
he pleases; he may not be able to satisfy next
year the tests that he could satisfy last year. If

maturing claims, such a man’s opportunity of mak-

ever, and any other provision may then be beyond
him. . The failure of a life assurance office, there-
fore, involves such disaster that it is doubtful
whether the business ought not to be made a State
monopoly—in other words, it is doubtful whether
private societies and companies should be trusted
to take the public’s money on the pretence of
offering assurance. Certainly there are mutual
societies and public companies which are doing
the business just as well, or at any rate almost as
well, as the State could do it; but it is an intricate
business, and the public (especially that part of
the public which most needs life assurance, being
unable to make any other provision) cannot readily
distinguish the sound institution from the rotten
one. It cannot even tell the sound principle
of assurance from the rotten one. Irvine ought
to have helped in the latter respect at least.
The future widow and orphan cried to him to do
S °" course he didn’t hear the widow and
orphan. He only heard the loud-voiced politicians
calling to him to clear their good name—the loud-
voiced politicians who can look after themselves
pretty well, while the future widow and- orphan
can’t. It is all very like Irvine,

The Dirty Kyabrammer.
A extracts from the reports of the Victorian
inspector of dairies give an excellent idea of the
stamp of men who are just now engaged in making
such a howl against socialistic legislation. They
show a disgusting state of affairs and a criminal
disregard of the health and lives of fellow citizens.
These extracts, which were handed to the daily '
press by the Victorian Director for Agriculture
(Mr. Wallace), are as follows :
In the first dairy named, the cows are milked in a shed
adjoining the piggery, with logged floor, through which
drainage passes and collects in a stagnant pool close to
the cow-shed. Dairy a few yards distant, constructed of
wood, and iron roof, walls and floor fouled with milk and
grease, and the odor from piggery is perceptible in the
dairy. The ground outside the dairy is fouled with milk,
and flies are in swarms in and outside dairy. Cream is
stored for a week before being sent to factory under
above conditions.
In the second dairy mentioned the bails are brick,
and covered to a depth of several inches with
manure. Milkers, chiefly boys, dirty in dress, and dirtier
in habits—each one smoking a pipe and expectorating
during milking. Milk stored at night under roof of shed',
and at time of visit pigs were camping under miltabench.
In the third case reported upon, the woodwork of the
separating and churning-room has rotted away, leaving
large openings where the washings from the floor find
their way under the building, causing that terrible
stench which is plainly perceptible throughout the
factory. Butter made under these conditions cannot be
of good quality, and all the efforts of the manager are
nullified by this undesirable state of affairs.
In the fourth case reported upon, the supervisor says
that he overhauled the whole of the milk suppliers’ cans
at the factory, a number of which were rusty and unfit
for use, whilst others were imperfectly cleaned, and in
the bottoms of some there were undoubted signs of the
presence of dirt in the milk.
It is scarcely wondered at that men whose bar-
barous and criminal instincts lead them to keep
their dairies in such filthy conditions should be
opposed to all legislation for the protection and
improvement of mankind. Indeed it is doubtful
whether they can be classified as men at all.
Judged by their habits they might be something
in the pig line.
+
One of the hardest knocks that the S.M.
Herald has received for a long time was the in-
gratitude shown by the Six Hatters in pledging
themselves to aid the Labor candidate for East
Sydney. No Freetrade organ barracked more for
the “Six” than the S’mkrald, nobody howled
more about the injustice and scandalous persecu-
tion of the Labor party, and the shameful and
disgraceful conduct of Ministers in preventing the
Hatters from landing (which they didn’t), and
the disloyalty of it all to the beloved Etnpiah.
The famous half-dozen were represented as being
madder than their proverbial ancestor, and to
have condemned labor conditions and legislation
here as tyrannical and worthy of Russia or Sheol.
So when the downfall came Granny whimpered :
The presence of these gentlemen on the Labor candi-
date s committee has ho political significance. They were
unionists when they left England, unionists when they
were received so fraternally by the unionists in Mel-
bourne, unionists when they were prevented from landing
in consequence of the representations of these brother
unionists of theirs. They are unionists still, it seems
and they think that the interests of labor would be con-
served by the return of a caucus representative. Their
opinion is not of more value than that of any six other
gentlemen imperfectly acquainted with the needs of
Australia.
When they landed, their opinion was of so much
value that the Herald’s representative chased
them all over the city for it. It may still be of
value. The Six Hatters weren’t soured as the
Herald was ; the Deakin Government didn’t
stop their gratis postage and prevent them living
on the plunder of the poor taxpayer, and when
they approved of the Deakin Government thev
probably did it honestly. The Hekald, with its
postage steal torn from it, can’t be expected to
approve of anything the Deakin Government
does.
