Backed up by subservient or interested ’Pootras
Bent rushed his Closed Roads Bill through
Victorian Assembly. Briefly, the measure pro-
vides for the leasing of such roads and of water
frontages; but, in spite of the strenuous oppo-
sition of Prendergast, Duffy and others, the
House declined to compel a landowner to put
swing-gates at entrances to such closed roads, or
to provide for public access thereto, or to compel
adjacent owners to fence the roads where they
don’t lease them. Half the rents of these State
properties are to go to the local councils, but, in
the absence of the provision for fencing, the
rents aren’t likely to amount to much. The
average landowner isn’t the kind of animal to pay
rent for a strip of land unless he is compelled
to do so, and it is no use offering a closed road
or water frontage to anybody but the adjoining
landowner—it wouldn’t pay anyone to fence it
off—so the landowner will neither fence nor lease
the roads, but will just let his sheep wander
around as before. The landholder will only
puy rent for the closed road or water frontage
where someone else is likely to step in if he
doesn’t. The democrats in the House vainly tried
to insert in Bent’s Bill provisions absolutely
prohibiting alienation of such lands; but the
-Kyabramit.es decided to leave all to the honesty
0( ? °f the Minister. And supposing
the Minister hasn’t any ? b
It looks as if the animosity of the big dailies is a
positive advantage to a candidate. Victorian
Labontes, except for one paltry first notice, were
studiously ignored, yet every one polled well up
to expectations, many far beyond. The party
handled its men well—kept them away from the
city, where they were well known and the vote was
sure and rushed them all round the country to tell
the electors what Age and Argus refused to do.
White, a North Melbournite, works the Western
district sheds, shearing; and is, of course, well
known ; still, he polled a heavy tally for a Labor
PFW ln the heart of a squatting country.
M Grath, a Collingwood baker, and an indifferent
speaker, beaten hollow a few years back by
O Connor, for Fitzroy, went out to where he was
almost unknown and gave Government and
Opposition men a great shaking-up. With
adequate press backing, Labor in five of the
States would have an absolute majority of both
Senate and Reps.,, only N.S.W. persistently
returns stodgy Foreign-traders, heavy mercantile
men from the city. Why, Heaven only knows.
A Melbourne jury lately expressed its contempt
for an iniquitous Act of Parliament and the
makers thereof. The case was the first under
f® c - t * ie c ‘ Employers and Employees Act
? r EJ years no one had been mean enough
to call in the aid of the criminal law to enforce a
civil contract—it was left to a Gippsland farmer
to do that. Said farmer charged his “servant”
(a milker) with “ wilfully, unlawfully, and
maliciously breaking his contract by leav-
ing his employment without notice, there-
by exposing valuable property (cows, to wit)
to injury.” Accused,. a dairy-farmer’s em-
ployee, had given his employer a day’s
notice, and had told him to keep the four days’
wages due to him, but had, as a matter of fact,
broken his contract to milk cows and not to leave
without giving a week’s notice. Judge Holroyd
summed up against accused, as he was forced to
do on the facts and the law ; nevertheless, after
two hours’ retirement, the jury acquitted.
j- he acquittal is noteworthy solely from the
fact that it . constitutes a public protest
against an iniquitous and one-sided law.
under which an employer who breaks hi's contract
(by discharging without notice) can be forced to
pay wages in lieu of notice, and that is all ; while
the employee who disregards his agreement can
not only be sued for damages, but can also be
gaoled like . a thief or murderer. At the
very time this case was being heard a jury in an
adjacent court was awarding damages to a woman
who was the victim of a breach of contract to marry.
The defendant in this case had not only broken
his agreement, but had also caused material injury
to a human being—the plaintiff woman—in that
he had, under promise of marriage, seduced her,
and had damaged her future prospects by leaving
her with an illegitimate infant; but the Law had
made no provision for charging him criminally.
The other defendant had done no visible injury
whatever, but his breach of contract might pos-
sibly have resulted in damage to “valuable pro-
perty”—a cow. A woman isn’t valuable pro-
perty—a cow is.
About Protection in Britain:—
Lord Masham (nee Sam Lister) is said to be the insti
gator of Chamberlain’s attack on Freetrade. For the
last 35 years the name of Lister, of Manningham Mills,
Bradford (he was converted into Lord Masham about 10
years ago), has stood for Enterprise in the British textile
manufacturing trade. The arguments advanced by Free-
traders to explain the failure of other manufacturers are
of no avail in Lister’s case. Whilst the slow-going men
were losing their markets to the up-to-date foreigner,
Lister was establishing a reputation as an improver on
foreign ideas. He speculated in attempts to produce
fabrics which were being imported into Britain from Ger-
many, and the general success of his speculations is stated
in the fact that the hard-headed mill owner was captured
by the Tory party and lowered to the peerage. What
Lister doesn’t know about British and foreign manufac-
tures and markets in all kinds of soft goods is not worth
knowing. He has been there, with an inventive mind, a
persistent energy, and an enormous amount of capital.
When he says, as he said the other day, that “ during the
last 15 years British trade with the colonies and British
possessions has increased at the rate of £2,000,000 a year,
otherwise the country would have been in distress owing
to hostile tariffs and free imports ” —when Lister makes
that pronouncement it is time for theoretical Freetraders
to hold their cackle. In such commercial matters the
voice of Lister, the unceasing advocate of Protection, is
the voice of Britain’s own particular god.
Either the muddled attempts at retrenchment on
the Victorian railways have got to the length of
pure insanity or else some of the “authorities”
are deliberately trying to force on a smash. Lately
the dailies, loaded up by the aforesaid authorities,
announced a “somewhat extensive injury to
.the permanent way at Essendon, through the
coupling-rod of a dead engine breaking. Two
rails were broken, but the interference with
traffic was inconsiderable, as repairs were
carried out expeditiously.” (Argus, 30/11/’03.)
A departmental inquiry was duly held and the
painful subject buried. But it leaked out that,
presumably to save two men’s wages, a big engine
needing repairs was attached “dead” to an
ordinary goods train from Seymour, stripped of
all movable parts to save weight, and sent down
to Melbourne. The persons responsible for
this actually removed the connecting, eccentric
and coupling rods and balance .weights; con-
sequently, the dreaded “hammer-blow” got
into full operation, the rails were spread and bent
at different points between Tallarook and Essen-
don (50 miles), and at Oliver’s Bank, just outside
Essendon, the speed was so great that, not two,
but a dozen, rails were broken. The metals had
to be immediately replaced for a distance
of 2| miles while traffic was worked on the
down line, the repairers working night and day
and all through the heavy rain on Sunday to get
the road in order again. And about 10 miles of
new metals will have to be put in between
Melbourne and Seymour. But the department
pcoh-poohs all inquiries and asserts there was
merely a trifling accident.
The demand of Mr. Sandford, who ran Pumpkin
Smith very closely in Bathurst (N.S.W.) district,
for a bonus to help to establish a new iron industry*
evoked a number of letters, purporting to be from
indignant agriculturists, asking why they should
be taxed to provide the iron industry with
assistance. Probably enough these letters were
real. Though everybody is more or less of a
politician in N.S.W. yet there is a very large
ignorance of politics, and the agriculturist, as a
rule, doesn’t in the least realise that for many
many years he has been the most aided, assisted’
petted and bonused man in the State—in fact that
he is a good deal of a parasite, living on the hard-
driven city worker, and yet calling himself the
backbone of the country. He gets his bonus
principally in the shape of railway rebates, and
what very extensive concessions these are is shown
by the latest N.S.W. railway report:

4.
It is hereby proposed that Sydney and
Melbourne should' do something really large and
handsome in the way of a benefit for Edward
Farley. Time was when Earley was a consider-
able god in Australia, and he sang better than any
real god that this paper ever met, and acted better
also. Somewhere about 15 or 20 years ago there
was no better voice hereabouts than his,
and it is a fine voice yet. But Farley, without
being exactly old, and without being worn out,
has dropped out. He left the stage for a while,
and, when he came back, a new order of things
had arisen, and opera was mostly musical comedy,
and musical comedy was mostly girls’ legs, and
what wasn’t girls’ legs was a succession of young
imported actors, who danced and did silly variety
turns. So Farley, who isn’t in the variety busi-
ness, wants to get away from Australia, and a
benefit or a subscription would help him to go.
Australia owes him that much.
## # *
The Musgrove Grand Comic, Opera Co.
said farewell to Sydney, at the Royal, on Satur-
day, inDonizetti’s charming opera, “The Daughter
of the Regiment.” The bright, vivacious music of
the little work and its genuine fun, which ripples
through the score as well as through the libretto,
h as adequately represented by the co., especially
by the orchestra and chorus, but, so far as the
dress circle of the house was concerned, continuous
chatter by a section of the audience smothered
a good deal of the lighter and more deli-
cate effects. The orchestra was, throughout,
well-balanced, and gave the music all its sparkle.
Miss Beattie was vocally not equal to the part of
the Daughter, but acted vivaciously, and in
one or two songs scored something more than
moderate success. Lempriere Pringle adapted a
heavy voice to the light and breezy work of
Sulpioe’s part with remarkable success, and was
. responsible for the best performance of the even-
ing. As Tonio, Henry Hallam was rather tired
and colorless, but he sang correctly and with
sweetness. Encores were frequently demanded
hut seldom given. A big evening’s entertainment
had been arranged by adding the second act of
“ The Serenade ” on to the bill, and the demand
for extras under such circumstances showed the
grasping tendencies of an alleged Freetrade
community.

The Royal re-opens on Boxing Night with
a Xmas pantomime which will have some relation
to “Beauty and the Beast.” “Mother Goose,”
and other well-known properties of the festive
season. It hasn’t yet struck a manager to intro-
ducea Dry Dog pantomime, with a grandpas dc
seul by Dill Macky’s calves. Originality is not
c irriecl to excess in Xmas pantos.
## * *
Julius Knight, he whose Roman legs are
a dream, and whose toga, in the days when he
used to go and eat the lion in CsesaFs amphi-
theatre, was too beautiful for words, will be the
star of J. C. Williamson’s Christmas attraction at
Her Majesty’s, Sydney, opening on the 26th.
“Monsieur Beaucaire” is the name of the
drama, hut that is merely a thin disguise,
put up in order that Julius’ legs may not burst on
the public too suddenly. Miss Maud Jeffries and
a powerful company will assist, and no effort will
be spared to make the show worthy of the occa-
sion. Christmas is a religious festival, and
monarchy is supposed to he one of the props of
the church, and “ Monsieur Beaucaire ” is a drama
with a powerful monarchical grovel in it, bo things
are strikingly appropriate all round.
** * *
In the large crop of holiday shows which,
at time of going to press, were making a commo-
tion at Sydney theatres, Mel. B. Spurr s head
could be seen waving proudly aloft. On the
high testimony of The Bulletin’s Melbourne
showman, M.B.S. is the very best monologue
entertainer who has struck Australia in this
generation. This part of the crop will bloom
on Boxing night at the Queen’s Hall, Pitt-st.,
which has been renovated for the occasion.
Further down the street,. at the Palace, an
American comedy co., which has been touring
M.L. with success, will play “Mama’s New
Husband ” —a piece which, on the word of the
advt., has made all the United States laugh.
This also opens Boxing night.
** * *
William Anderson has shipped off a com-
pany to Maofilarid, and “The World Against
Her” is now being played at Sydney Lyceum
with Miss Conroy as leading lady, instead of
Miss Duggan, who heads the Maoriland con-
tingent. Numerous other changes have also been
made in the cast. About the time these few re-
marks get into circulation the piece will, however,
be changed for the Lyceum’s great Christmas
attraction, “The Great World of London,” for
which a large quantity of horse and other impres-
sive effects are being collected. The Bulletin
doesn’t know for certain whether there’s a pie-
stall in this production or not; but as it will
include several London-street night scenes of
great magnificence, this paper will risk it and
prophesy at least one pie-stall. Somehow, there
is nothing that seems to make a Lyceum audience
happier tnan a pie-stall.
*****
At Sydney Criterion, Charles Holloway
has taken on the large contract of telling “Why
Woman Sins.” How he proposes to cram the
whole list of whys into one night this paper
doesn’t know, and will have to wait till Boxing
Night to find out. Meantime Holloway has shut
up the theatre for a few nights so that he may
attack the problem with the new energy and
clothes and scenery and extras which the subject
and the season call for.
** * *
Sydney aldermen aren’t taking any
chances over the engagement of their hall by
the-shortly-to-advent Profit Dowie for a shekel-
hunting campaign in February next. After con-
sideration, they decided that the purple wings
may flap therein for three days for £215, pay-
able in advance, and a deposit of £lOO to make
good any damage which may possibly result
from any argument Dowie may have with his
audience. Considering that the Profit|s Zion
City has been seized for a small liability of
£145,000 of Dowie’s, and the more important fact
that the Profit himself is reputed to have wings,
the pay-in-advance idea is wise.
** * *
The Christmas extras thrown into the
Tivoli bill consist chiefly of the Cirnocs, who are
put into handcuffs and leg-irons and neck-chains
on the stage, and slip out of them behind a screen.
They are interesting as far as they go even now,
but for practical purposes they come to Sydney
60 or 70 years too late. In Melbourne the boss
Cirnoc, a slim young gentleman named William,
was put into the jewellery prepared specially for
“ the Kellies,” and got out of it all just as easily
as he does out of the collection he carries round
with him. If he could also get out of the habit of
doing so much of his business behind the screen,
the turn would probably seem even more remark-
able than it does now. The rest of the show is
much as it was when it was last favorably noticed
here, and includes Whitburn, Winton, a micro-
biosoope which pictures two toads fighting, and
a number of people who sing and dance, and a
few who sing and don’t dance.
** * *
Melbourne resembles all other professedly
Christian cities insofar that Christmas falls due
in its midst this week. And Christmas is the time
when theatrical managers always produce a new
show and call it seasonable. Bland Holt’s forth-
coming melodrama, at the Royal, is to introduce
(as this journal remarked in last issue) a seasonable
scenario, which presumably includes roast goose
and trimmings. Anyway, the new Australian
narrative will be born on Boxing Night, and its
situations promise to be “rollicking.” The last
two nights of “The Great Millionaire” will
transpire in the meantime. Melbourne waves a
respectful adieu to the “Millionaire,” although
Bland Holt didn’t make it all that it might have
been.
** * *
At Her Majesty’s the Comic Opera Co.
will return to favor on Saturday. There has
been a lapse of several months, which seemed like
years, since Florence Young sang and Lauri
painted his nose red at the big house in Exhibi-
tion-st. Also there has been a lack of frivolous-
ness at other theatres for the past eversolong ;
consequently Melbourne is hungry and thirsty for
opera. Its tongue is hanging out. Respectable
citizens have been observed to stand in front of
J.C.W.’s advt. and feast their bloodshot eyes and
sniff the details like dead-beats outside a cook
shop. The first delicacy of the season will be
“ A Country Girl,” fresh from Sydney.
** * *
The programme appointed for the Prin-
cess’s on December 26th (Boxing Night disguised
under another name) is “Midsummer Night’s
Dream.” In its Shakspearian way the “ Dream ”
will be more seasonable this time than when the
Musgrove Co. made its first appearance; more-
over the management announces some additional
flying fairies, and elfin ballets. A beauteous show
in April and May, this highly embellished pro-
duction of the Bard’s extravaganza should get a
grateful greeting at Xmas. Parents and guardians
who have not yet sampled the “ Dream ” on their
own account need have no scruples in recom-
mending it to the children as a high-class panto-
mime, entitled “ Big Babes in the Wood, or Har-
lequin Oberon and The Sleeping Beauty and the
Beast.”
** * •
Wirth Bros, have graciously consented to
visit Melbourne this week and remain for a fort-
night in the sheltered vale near Princes Bridge
where they pitched their tents last Easter. The
Wirths are returning with new tents—green and
cool to the eye—and the Circus show will include
sundry latest items, in addition to the Howard
Bros/the educated tigers, Page!, and other old
acquaintances. One of the fresh turns is PP
by a professor who has taught the lion and the,
lamb to lie down together in peace and goodwill.
The Circus starts its flying farewell season witn a.
matinee on Boxing Day.
** * *
The Bijou, which was last opened at Cup
time to admit of a ghastly reflection on “ Charley s.
Aunt.” will re-open again in a more promising
manner on Boxing Night. A pantomime, real
and indisputable, is about to break the silence at,
the Bijou, under the management of lom Ger-
man. The story—for even a panto, must have a.
story of some sort or other—will be a. White Aus-
tralian version of “Little Red Riding Hood,
alleviated by numerous local allusions ana
mounted regardless of expense. It was originally
intended to stage this pastoral fiction at the
Gaiety Theatre, but the scheme has grown too big
for the Gaiety, and the theatre overhead stands;
higher in the world, anyhow.
*# * *
Touching the more or less serious-minded
entertainments that will be offered to an mver-fed
public on Xmas Night. At the Town Hall the
Philharmonic Society performs ‘ The Messiah,
with Ada Crossley and Peterkin as principal',
soloists. At the Opera House a musical Festival,
is announced ; it is promoted by Nap. Boffard,
and supported by a numerous concert co. The,
Festival will deal in a mixed manner with the
adventures of Rebekah, a Jewish lady who went
to the well. The Orpheus Society is to jubilate,
religiously at the Bijou, and the only Boffard will
break out again at the Town Hall on Boxing
Night in charge of a popular conoert, free from au.
suspicion of sacred music.
** * *
At the Opera House, the Xmas specialties
are Mdlle. Vulcana and Mr. Atlas, who came to
hand last Saturday. Clark and Hamilton, the
long-deferred pair of comedy sketchers, will make
their first appearance this week. Atlas is a strong’
man in the weight-lifting line of business, and
Vulcana is a graceful young amazon of 18 or there-
abouts, with a lovely arm for dumb-bell practice,
and a back that any male connoisseur would go
miles to see. The damsel gave a private exhibi-
tion of her muscular charms on the Opera House
stage the other afternoon, when her baok was
deeply admired.
** * *
Adelaide’s present blank in the matter of
theatres makes even Anderson’s late “ Greed of
Gold ” regretted. It was a particularly impro-
bable and unusually bloodthirsty show, but Harry
Diver worked hard as a struck-dumb person to
whom the simple idea of writing never seemed to
occur. Also Edmund Duggan was funny, and
Miss Helen Burdette good to look upon ; and
hard work was the motto of them all. After'
Christmas, Beatrice Esmond and co. are announced
to descend from various up-country wanderings
with one “Little Lord Fauntleroy ’’—whosename
seems not wholly unfamiliar. Meanwhile the police
band has been concert-giving vigorously, and the
GleeClubhasdone the same, andsohas the Orpheus
Society. And in many suburban schools the
small girl has made known what Curfew will not,
be allowed to do this evening, while the Univer-
sity had the excellent idea, of making all its Con-
servatorium prize-takers show what they were
capable of, after they had been • handed their’
diplomas. Once again Adelaide prides itself on
being the Home of Music (and elocution), and
really its record at Ballarat and elsewhere in open
competition is an excellent one, so far as that
goes. _

THE BENEFICIAL BAN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Re Trenwith’s triumph over David Syme in getting to
the Victorian top of the Senate poll, the Argus was as
a veteran courtesan at a christening. “ The place
attained by Mr. Trenwith is most creditable to him, ’ said
the Organ of the Rejected Four. “ His election is a
wholesome evidence that the public will not allow the
great power which a public journal possesses to be used
unfairly against individuals. The journal which sets out
to persistently write a worthy man down will always find
that it has succeeded only in writing him up." This
would be entirely humorous and pleasing if the expression
“ worthy man” didn’t threaten danger to Trenwith. If
he has at last secured the support of the Argus, he can,
count himself dead at next election.
For years and years the Argus old
Has uttered its abortive ban
’Gainst all who fought the power of Gold,
And barracked for the Working Man.
Each Labor candidate was “ Mud,"
An enemy to State and Grown ;
The Argus bellowed for his blood,
Or, anyway, “ it wrote him down."
The bunkum hurled at Trenwith’s head
In former times was Trenwith’s food ;
It served the same as meat and bread
To fatten him, and do him good.
Anon the Trades Hall nominee
Became a bloke of some renown ;
“ He’s right,” said the Democracy—
“ The Argus always writes him down.”
The Providence that rules aloft
Directs the Argus in its course ;
All Labor men at whom it scofft
Respect it as a local force.
Delighting in its bitter word,
Rejoicing in its awful frown,
They pray : “ Advance the Cause, O Lord 1
And bid the Argus write us down."

Fruitgrowers round Melbourne are at last doing
what they should have tried years ago-starting a
genuine co-operative company to deal with their pro-
duce. Practically all growers round the city are share-
holders ; none may hold less than five nor more than -00
shares, and there are already over 2000 distinct holder-
idea is to sell wholesale from the co. s own store . to shiy
direct to buyers ; to pulp, can, and make jam. Already
•the pulp and jam factory—a defunct brewery in Rich
mond-is in full swing. Profits go in this order ■.-(1) 5
■per cent, to shareholders on paid-up capital, (2) 10 per
cent, for depreciation, etc.; (3) 20 per cent, to a reserve
fund • (4) balance as a bonus to shareholders on business
done with the co. Excepting one—a city nan-aU
directors are genuine growers, and they have got an
expert in the exporting business, a grower himself, and a
hustler, as manager. If they keep their brand up to the
mark these fruit-growers should presently realise for
themselves a good profit, and that will suit them better
than 20 years of listening to jam-maker Derham s talk of
41 Socialistic legislation.” The principal danger is that
there may he too many bosses.

1903: “ The year’s business of the Colonial Mutual Life

• • • *
The McDermott Sizer now on the market is
rapidly coming into favor with mining men. Useful as
this medicine is, there is no intention of claiming that
by the use of it, it becomes possible to save hy concentra-
tion all of the mineral values in ore. Some loss is bound
to occur, no matter what mechanical methods are
employed, but what is claimed is that by the use of the
Sizer, the pulp can be prepared in a manner more suitable
and in such a way as to materially increase the efficiency
of the concentrating plant. Nor must it be conjectured
that the McDermott Sizer is only for use in conjunction
with Wilfiey Tables; although it acts admirably with
those machines, it is of such general application as to
enable it to be used with equal advantage with whatever
type of dressing machine happens to be employed.
Those wishing for more information in regard to this
useful machine can obtain catalogues and full particulars
from the Austral Otis Engineering Company of South
Melbourne, who are the Agents and Sole Manufacturers
of the machine.*

British manufacturers have been beaten in an
open competition by an Australian engineering firm. We
have ascertained that the Mond Gas Co. of Staffordshire
have accepted the tender of Mephan Ferguson, of Mel*
bourne, for the supply of about 13 miles of Ferguson’s
Patent Lockingbar Steel Pipes, from 18 inches to 36 inches
diameter. The machinery for manufacturing these pipes
has all been made at Ferguson’s General Engineering
Works, Footscray, and shipped to Tipton, in Staffordshire,
where the branch works are being erected. The same
firm have also erected engineering and pipe works at
Wanganui, New Zealand, and have contracted to supply
the Wanganui Borough Council with about 20 miles of
Spiral Riveted Steel Pipes for the town water supply.*

“ Prudence Prude ” has an idea:—“Much has
been said about mining managers, their status, &c. ; let
me say something about directors. If a person holds a
big interest, no matter whether he be a squatter, lawyer,
or business person, without the slightest practical know-
ledge of mining, he is elected on the board, and the
manager is hampered, and often controlled, by'a set of
mining ignoramuses, who, as soon as adverse circum-
stances set in, or the manager will not work the mine to
suit the market, make him the scapegoat, and he is
asked to resign ; and so the shareholders lose a
thoroughly competent man. Why not compel companies
to have a certificated managing director, to assist the
manager, and lay a report before the other directors at
every board meeting ? This would protect the investing
public against incompetent boards, and that, with sub-
stantial reserve funds, would put mining on a firmer
basis.” • \

• • * •
Droving is particularly brisk just now in nearly
all parts of Queensland. Thousands of cattle are con-
tinually pouring into N.S.W. and S.A., especially from
the Gulf country. Up there feed was plentiful through
the drought, but the cattle were isolated by the barren
and waterless stretch between them and any market.
A. J. Cotton, a N.S.W. buyer, has 26,000 head starting on
the roads for the east, and is buying up every hoof he can
get hold of from Winton and Hughenden districts to the
Peninsula. Sid Kidman is also buying up all available
stock in the Northern Territoiy and W.Q. It is esti-
mated that between 38,000 and 40,000 head of store cattle
(priced at £6 10s. per head on the runs) are now on the
way to Bourke, Muswellbrook, Wodonga and other places
from the northern stations.

About six months ago an innocent money-
’ lender fell among the borrowing members of the Victorian
i Public Service. According to his counsel’s statement in
i court he was a very simple person, who was prepared to
i lend the public servant money on a promissory note
( by a brother public servant. The word was
j passed around, and the new money-lender was rushed
' with business. In a few days he had lent £3OO, in lots of
a£1(); to some of the worst marks in the service. Instead
<ofi his cash he now holds a pretty bundle of judgment
c eiiders against the borrowers. His counsel said that the
1 public servants backed each other’s bills in a wonderfully
vready manner.
* * * •
The latest innovation of the Postal Department
'is the application of the coupon system to the telephone.
Any subscriber willing to allow the public to use his
instrument must notify the department, whereupon his
name will be put on the “coupon telephone list,” and he
will be given a book of coupons which he must sell at
threepence each to anyone desiring to use his ’phone for
five minutes. To allow anyone the use of his instrument
without first selling him a coupon is to run the risk of
having it cut off without notice. Out of each threepence
the subscriber gets a penny and the department two-
pence. The system will probably put a healthy stop to
the frivolous use of somebody elSe’s wire, help the
harassed subscriber to pay for his machine, and bring a
little grist to the Federal mill.

“Tubal Cain” : Wanted, and wanted badly, an
/ Australian Savings Bank in connection with the Common-
wealth Post Office. Miners, shearers, and other nomads
1 lose interest and are put to unnecessary and great ex-
ipense in exchanging their savings from one State to
:another. Trades unions (with benefit funds) and friendly
'societies are heavily penalised in the same way. I have
Iknown the transfer of £2OO of Engineers’ Society money
from Broken Hill to Melbourne to cost about £5. One
deposit book should serve for the whole Commonwealth ;
any post-office should pay or receive on it; balances
should go on yielding interest. The balance available
with the Postmaster-General for investment might be
used to buy depreciated State bonds from Bull-Cohen.
Anyway, The Bulletin has given reasons enough for
removing trust money out of the reach of indigent and
prodigal State Treasurers.
» * * *
For years Melbourne only had one “dead-horse
merchant ” who advertised that he removed defunct
equines free of charge. Now there are five whose adver-
tisements adorn the columns of the daily press. The
first merchant has retired very comfortably off, but his
business is still in family hands. It pays handsomely to
be the first in at the death of a horse down South, and
trade in horseflesh is very keen. A dead horse is an
incubus the owner is anxious to get rid of, but he is
spared all anxiety nowadays. Before the late lamented’s
hoofs are cold', someone has telephoned to the nearest
dead-horse merchant, who drives round the corner with
his tackle, and has whipped his prize off before the
astounded owner can wipe away the third tear. These
present-day dealers in unconsidered trifles also remove
dead cows, but draw the line at dogs and cats, likewise
poultry ! The hides, hoofs, tallow, horns, bones, teeth,
entrails, hair, and sinews are all saleable commodities.
This fact is often quite overlooked by cookies who
invariably cremate the family slave when bot-fly gets it
down 1

Investment Society (Melbourne):

Under the society’s original rearrangement it paid 2J per
cent, on all its debentures. Later they were divided into
two equal parts, “ A ” debentures to receive 5 per cent,
interest, and “ B” 4 per cent, when there was enough to
pay it with. Last year the 4 per cent, was paid for the
first time ; this required £1146. This year, some “B ”
debentures having apparently been bought up in. the
meantime, £lOB7 is required. On the year’s transactions
enough business profit was made to pay the full interest
on both lots of debentures and leave £450 over : but the
losses on the sales of property amounted to £5lO, leaving
the net loss of £6O shown above.
The Universal proposes now to have a cleaning
up. Its assets stand in the balance-sheet at £181,023 ;on
realising these it is estimated that a loss of £54,300 will
be made. The losses on realisations already amount to
£22,709 ; total loss to be provided for, £77,009. The paid-
up capital is £128,310. To provide for the losses, actual
and probable, it will be necessary to write off three-fifths
of this ; this will give £76,986. For the £5 shares two of
£1 each will then be issued. This done, the society will
have a capital of £51,324, and liabilities to the public,
£74,590; against that it will have loans on mortgage,
freeholds, &c., £126,745, and £25 cash. The society has,
so far, made excellent progress in reducing its liabilities
to the public ; in 1894 they amounted to £172,662 ; they
are now, as shown above, £74,596. On the reduced
capital the business profit for the past 12 months (£450)
was equal to about 17s. 6d. per cent.
* * * *
S.A. Parliament last session, at the instance of
Labor member Archibald, passed an Act, of which the
principal provisions are these :
“ When proceedings are taken in any court hy a money-
lender for the recovery of any amount lent after the
commencement of this Act, and for enforcement of any
agreement or security made or taken after the com-
mencement of this Act in respect of money lent either
before or after the commencement of this Act and there
is evidence which satisfies the court that the interest
charged in respect of the sum actually lent is excessive,
or that the amounts charged for expenses, inquiries,
fines, bonus, premium, renewals, or any other charges are
excessive, and that, in either case, the transaction is
harsh and unconscionable, or is otherwise such that a
court of equity would give relief, the court may re-open
the transaction and take an account between the money-
lender and the person sued, and may, notwithstanding
any statement or settlement of account or any agree-
ment purporting to close previous dealings and create a
new obligation, re-open any account already taken
between them and relieve the person sued for payment
of any sum in excess of the sum adjudged by the court
to be fairly due in respect of such principal, interest, and
charges as the court, having regard to the risk and all
the circumstances, may adjudge to be reasonable ; and if
any such excess has been paid or allowed in account by
the debtor, order the creditor to repay it, and may set
aside, either wholly or in part, or revise or alter any
security given or agreement made in respect of money
lent by the money-lender ; and if the money-lender has
parted with the security, may order him to indemnify the
borrower or other person sued.
“ The foregoing provisions shall apply to any transac-
tion which, whatever its form may be, is substantially
one of money-lending by a money-lender.”
It will take a very long thin usurer to crawl through
that hole. The strength of the Act lies in its uncertainty-
A cast-iron law against charging more than so much per
cent, interest may be evaded by calling the thing
“ bonus,” or by some such wile, but a law which simply
gives the court power to review, re-open, and upset
everything, and which can’t be provided against by any
document whatever, and which takes in every dodge
that is of the nature of money-lending, whether it is
ostensible money-lending or not, is likely to do good

* * * *
Westralia has decided at last that there is no
hope for the district served by its Midland Railway unless
it gets rid of the mouldy English co. which owns the
line, and borrows money from the State to keep itself
afloat, and runs a miserable delayed hen-coop on wheels
along its two rusted rails, and leaves the farmers*
produce lying two weeks in the rain because it has
not sufficient trucks to carry it away, and no sheds
to store it in, and no tarpaulins to cover it. A Bill
dealing with this and other matters was introduced
last session. Wherefore, the arrogant Bull in London
threatens, as usual, that the supply of loans will be cut
off unless W.A. puts up with any service the English
co. likes to give it. This will never be a free country
till it is in a position to tell Bull that it doesn’t care
a hang for his ioans.
* * * •
So far as it haa gone the Perth (W.A.) motor-
bus service has not panned out as well as the promoters
expected. It is all right where the roads are kept
smooth ; where they aren’t—and that is most places—the
wear-and-tear on the tyres is so great that the calculations
of what would be payable fares have been knocked clean
out.

"Brumby”: A callow youth of my acquaint-
ance, wishing to raise a fiver, replied to a “ Christian
Lady ” ad., and was in due course introduced to a Sydney
solicitor. This person fixed the youth with an eagle eye,
questioned him sharply, made him sign a formal declara-
tion that he was not indebted to any other shark,
obtained his signature to a P.N. for £lO, and then
handed him £4 14s. 6d., casually explaining that he
deducted 6d. for the P.N. form and ss. for the “ Chris-
tian Lady’s ” fee. Wanted badly—A Bill regulating the
exact size of a shark’s bite.

• * * •
The Broken Hill mines are working under the
new conditions set by the Arbitration Court, and nobody
notices any difference. In fact, the companies adopted
the new rules weeks before they were obliged to do so
under the award —pretty conclusive proof that the
miners don’t get any benefit out of the new conditions at
the expense of the owners. During the hearing of the
case some of the men growled about losing their Saturday
afternoons, owing to the day-shift ending at 4 p.m. The
Court had an idea that this could very easily be got
over; without fixing the matter, it recommended that
shifts should be changed at 6 a.m., 2 p.m., and 10 p.m.,
instead of at 8 a.m., 4 p.m., and midnight. But when a
vote was taken on the Arbitration Court’s suggestion,
the men decided by an enormous majority against any
change. The fact is that to come “ off shift ”at 2 o’clock
would give the miner his Saturday afternoons all right,
but to have to go on at 10 p.m-. instead of midnight all
the week would break into many an evening’s fun —and
that was the other small thing which the Court hadn’t
considered.

“L.W.” writes : "Hill End (N.S.W.) scene of
the glittering glory of Holterman, still has a certain
belief, not vigorous but melancholy, in the great tunnel
scheme to prospect at a depth, and also drain, Hawkins’
Hill. Considering that said tunnel would be about six
miles long, and that nothing of recent date has warranted
a 6ft. tunnel at Hill End, hopeS are likely to remain
melancholy. Just now there is a faint stirring of dry
bones in the locality ; besides the operations at King’s
Reef it is rumored that Specimen Gully, at the Hill, the
Canton, near Tambaroora, and the Root Hog, on the
Macquarie, are to be revived again. Whatever their re-
sults they are at least more hopeful than the shadowy
and gigantic tunnel scheme.”
* * * *
“Redoubt”: Be alleged special difficulty of
treating Pinnacles (N.S.W.) ore (8., Nov. 19). The
metallurgical snag of all the Broken Hill stuff is zinc,
whereas Pinnacles ore has practically none of that. Even
in the concentrates of Pinnacles ore but little zinc is
found, and as no effort is necessary to eliminate that
troublesome metal (as is the case with Broken Hill ore) a
much higher silver extraction is obtained. Writer
knows Broken Hill ores fairly well, and has seen the
Pinnacles ores treated at Aldershot (Q.) by both wet and
fire methods, and he is prepared to say that if the plant
to be erected at the Pinnacles will do work equal to that
of the plant employed at Aldershot, then the Queensland
Smelting Co., as long as the large ore bodies of the
Pinnacles last and maintain their value, will have a valu-
able asset. Time will show whether the new manager
of the Q.S. Co. (Watson) is right. At present it looks
very much in his favor. Past troubles of Pinnacles,
writer thinks, can be ascribed to defective methods of
mechanical concentration and not to innate metallurgical
difficulty.
* * * *
That mostly-Adelaide-owned N.Q. tin venture,
the Stannary Hills, is trying to get out of its financial
troubles by issuing £30,000 more debentures, mainly for
the purpose of relieving the directors and others of their
personal responsibility for a heavy overdraft. Even this
new issue, however, will not free the co., which will still be
something over £lO,OOO in debt. The whole concern
was based on a series of shocking miscalculations as to
the cost of the necessary works, the value of the ore, and
the cost of mining, and other things. Developments
have shown more ore than there seemed to be a while
back; but the property isn’t anything like good enough
so far as can be seen, to pay on the capitalisation. There
will be a cold time for all but the debenture-holders
anyway.

The success of the River Dee slug-hunters has
put life into various dredging and sluicing syndicates
formed to test the Palmer River, N.Q. If the results on
a big scale equal those with dish and cradle some of the
adventurers have a good thing ahead.
* * « *
Queensland Minister of Mines Browne is shak-
ing up the Northern mining sharks. His instructions to
wardens re exemptions are to refuse unless applications
are made in good faith. A recent O. Towers case elicited
evidence, in the case of a mine that had been hung up
for six months, that tributors had offered 20 to 25 per
cent, royalty to be allowed to work certain blocks left.
Also alleged that sooner than let tributors work aban-
doned blocks a shaft was let go to rack and ruin. Yet
this co. had paid a quarter of a million in divs. Another
case is that of the School Reserve ; after being hung up
for several years, it was let to a tribute party, which has
just paid a 6d. div. on its 20,000 scrip. Other instances
could be quoted where the old style of granting exemp-
tion for the asking stood between bona fide prospectors
and good wages.

Queen Cross Reef, which holds the Charters
Towers record for 1903, holds also now the record of
being the only O.T. co. which has ever paid £lOO,OOO in
divs. out of mining profits in six months. The actual
working expenses were £120,631 less than the revenue •
but part of the difference between that and £lOO,OOO went
in plant additions. In 12 months 2 tons 161 b. of gold has
been turned out, 1 ton 4cwt. 3gr. of it in the second half
when the profit was equal to £Bl6s. 4d. per ton of stone
treated. The last month’s profit was nearly £21,000.
A far-up South Australian correspondent ex-
plains why all the mica-mining' enterprises die early. The
spar which carries the mica very rarely goes down ;
nearly always quartz takes its place, and quartz doesn’t
carry mica. Then, of the mica that there is, only a very
small proportion is marketable at a price which pays for
export from Australia, London and American markets
being glutted with “small mica” (anything sin. by 6in.
or less is small mica). Of eight tons of mica mined at
one place in the Far North, only one ton of mica could be
cut; and of that lot only 1501 b. was large mica, the rest
being either classed as small or discarded altogether,

The persistency with which Maorilanders
are reminded of the royal visit grows monoton-
ous. The Government was asked in a torrid tone,
the other day, when it was going to pay the
butcher’s bill for meat supplied to the troops at the
magnificent Rotorua royal shivoo. It replied, in
a tired, perfunctory way, that further inquiries
were being made, or words to that effect. The
unpaid-bill disease seems to have followed royalty
everywhere.

M.L. Lawn Tennis Association has been
angling for a titled person to represent it on the
English Tennis Association, and it bagged
Visconde de Horncastle. This gent’s handle was
made in Portugal, but it is reckoned quite good
enough. Cheerfully printed that, besides being
able to play “love all,” the visconde is a great
sport, can handle a cue, ride a bike to some
extent, and float in calm water.
The ways of the Brisbane Charity Organi-
sation Society are interesting—and typical. The
other day Q. Minister Browne wanted to know
how it was that out of a total annual expenditure
of £325 (£lBO of which was State endowment) the
society only spent in relief the sum of £74. The
other £251 was expended (according to the
society’s report) in “supervision and making in-
quiries.” This meant, as Browne remarked,
3s. sd. spent in the supervision-cum-inquiry busi-
ness for every shilling used for relief purposes.
Browne further informed the philanthropists that,
at this rate, it would pay the country far better to
spend the money directly itself, even if 50 per cent,
of those relieved were impostors. Just fancy
spending 3s sd. to find out if a derelict were de-
serving of relief to the extent of a shilling’s worth
of bread. The destitute can’t eat inquiry.
* * * *
As a matter of solemn historical truth it
may be mentioned that the first day’s sitting of
the Federal High Court, on October 6th, has not
yet been brought to a lawful termination. It was
formally opened in the Melbourne Banco Court,
and should have been closed in the same building
when the three judges left the bench. But, in
the excitement of the novel occasion, poor old
Booth, the court crier (since retired), forgot to
perpetrate the proper form of closure, terminating
m “ God Save the King.” Next day, the judges
met in the 3rd Civil Court, which was duly
opened and closed in the ordinary way, without
regard to the fact that the previous court was left
open all night, so that stray hens might have
come in and roosted on the precedents, and their
Honors had (in an official sense) gone to bed
sitting Inasmuch as the court, on the second
day, was not closed in its original domicile there
remains the practically unanswerable question
whether the first day’s court can ever be closed,
except by special Act of Parliament. Moreover,
there are no precedents to prove that a court
which wasn’t formally closed in one place can be
legally re-opened in another ; therefore, the
actuality of all proceedings up to date is open to
grave doubt, and the spirits of Griffith, Barton
and O’Connor are probably a trio of Banco ghosts
doomed to sit evermore on the bench wherefrom
their bodies were not formally released on Octo-
ber 6th.

before him but his eyes on the prowl. His wife saj

girl—:flitted round the table. He was in festive spirits,
The P revious evening he had slipped a half-sovereign into
the housemaid’s hand, and her fingers had closed
' vi *lingly over it. He swept a proud territorial eye round

what spirit of adventure his wife had chosen such a

of his domestics, dating from the period when his wife

T be housemaid had vanished. He drained his tea-cup
nervously, and, staring foolishly at something in it that
struck bis bps and jingled as it fell back into the cup,
discovered—his half-sovereign.

There was a lot of human interest in a
maintenance case heard at Christchurch (M.L.)
recently. A middle-aged individual wearing a
scarred jaw and a suit of unpawnable garments,
who proceeded against two of his family for
support, admitted that he had had an allowance
of two “ wives,” but got through it. When num-
ber one ran away with the other fellow he tried a
second ; but he did not have an idyllic time,
because they were always wrangling about
religion. The humor of it ! Eventually she left
him and sank to respectability again. The court
made no order on discovering that the children
were illegitimate.
Maoriland prisoners cost their country
much less than the Australian average. At
Auckland the net expense per head of the average
number in confinement has been got down to
£9 45., which is probably the lowest point reached
in any. Australasian gaol. At Wilcanniaand some
other western N.S.W. gaols the average is over
£125. The most interesting current Maoriland
experiment in penology is in connection with tree-
planting at Waiotapu ; unfortunatelv the number
of prisoners suitable for this camp-life is small.
Results, however, are encouraging enough to
justify the setting up of a similar camp at Hanmer
to absorb the suitable prisoners from South Island
gaols. The net cost per prisoner at Waiotapu is
something under £l5; less, that is, than it is at
either Darlinghurst or Parramatta, in N.S.W.
“E.”: Justice Way rules the Adelaide*
Art Gallery just as George Verdon used to rula
the Melbourne Gallery. He has good intern
tions, and believes in spending money on
Australian pictures; but, unfortunately, his
aesthetic sense has remained undeveloped, so he
poses as a connoisseur, and selects the most
howling indiscretions. If he would only “in-
quire, ” and allow somebody who knew to buy for
the Gallery, Adelaide would soon have a repre-
sentative collection of Australian work. Art is
cheap in Australia. Sydney does something,
Adelaide does the wrong thing, and Melbourne
does nothing at all. It’s a pity some place doesn’t
do the right thing—and lots of it.

ITS PROPER PLACE!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
VIC.
One day I wrote a poem—
“ These Bards,” said I, “ I’ll show 'em !
Some poems are but part felt,
Each line of mine was heart-felt.
I even saw a tear-drop
Upon my writing-gear drop
When only half way through it—
I couldn’t help but do it;
When one’s best girl rejects one
Verse-writing so affects one ;
My very soul I poured out,
And several lines I scored out
As being too poetic
For times so apathetic.
At last my pen I laid down
With less of sorrow weighed down ;
It’s really very rum how
A change came o’er me somehow.
The exercise of writing—
One’s inmost thoughts inditing—
The feelings does relieve so,
At least I quite believe so.
I all at once reflected,
What if I am rejected ?
What if my loved, lost Alice
Has filled my bitter chalice ?
Yet did I really love her?
Why, no ! For high above her
I’ve always placed, I fancy,
That dear, sweet girl named Nancy ;
She knows of my affection.
Her eyes in my direction
Have glanced, not once but often,
And always seemed to soften ;
She would not turn her nose up
At my suit.
Here I rose up,
Somewhat abruptly flitting
From where I had been sitting;
In doing so I brushed off
My desk the lines I’d rushed off
Quite recently, when feeling
For heart-ache there’s no healing.
Oh, was it fate? I ask it,
For my waste-paper basket
Received that M.S., yawning
For more—it seemed a warning.
And when I saw it drop there,
Well, yes, I let it stop there.
EARDLET TURNER.
TOLL FOR THE SLAVE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“This month (November) the last recruiting -essel
leaves Queensland for the South Seas.” —Newspaper item.
Toll for the slave !
The slave ships go no more
To gather human freight
On sunny island shore.
That wealth might wax apace
They spoiled the island creed,
They brought the islands’ best,
To grow the crops of greed 1
The traffic’s evil smell
Was borne on every breeze ;
Its fame was foul as hell
Through all our border seas.
It sprang—an evil tree —
From roots in rotten soil ;
Its branches drew their strength
From unrequited toil ;
It gave us guilty place
On black Oppression’s roll;
The blood of half a race
Is red upon our soul.
But now —our vessels go
On errand dark no more
No more we sell for slavea
The honor of o.ur shore.
Q.

In the S’African war, some of the British
soldiers discovered a new “drink” in cordite.
British Medical Journal byexperimentsfound—
Dissolved in tea, cordite produces an almost imme-
diately exhilarating effect “ inciting to almost demoniacal
actions.” This condition is followed by heavy sleep and
stupor, lasting five to twelve hours, according to the
quantity taken. To awaken the subject it is often
necessary to slap his face, punch or shake him, and
awakening is accompanied by severe, dull, boring head-
ache, muscular twitchings, and protrusion of the eyes.
It is as an addition to beer that cordi'.e appears to pro-
duce its worst effects. It then excites a quarrelsome,
destructive mania in an otherwise peacefully disposed
individual, and produces immediate intoxication in a
man who can commonly consume as much as four or five
pints of beer without exhibiting a trace of having done
so. If taken in quantity insufficient to produce sleep it
makes him not only quarrelsome, but brings out the
worst traits in his character.
* * *
At Woodville (M.L.), tne other day, a
man was charged, before the local S.M., with
breach of a prohibition order, he having been
found in the bar of the railway refreshment
rooms. The stipendiary, however, dismissed the
case, holding that refreshment rooms are not
licensed “within the meaning of the Act,” their
licenses being issued by the Minister of Railways
and not by the licensing committee. Now an
ordinary railway traveller can hardly get to the
refreshment room counter by reason of the pro-
hibited citizens being there four deep calling for
rum.
* * * *
The old houses in Macquarie-streeb, Syd-
ney, opposite Parliament House, which were
built in the thirties by John Dailey, father of
William Bede Dailey, are to be demolished to
make way for a pile of fashionable flats. The
house in which John Dailey conducted an out-
fitting business in Lower George-street, opposite
the Commissariat Stores, still stands, as do his
early private residences in Princes-street and
Upper Fort-street. Another house which is
threatened is the pretty cottage in Bent-street
adjoining the. Australian Club. This was built,
also in the thirties, by Mrs. Frances Cox, mother
of Mrs. W. C. Wentworth and Mrs. Richard Hill.
Francis Cox was an early colonist who carried on
a whitesmithy on Circular Quay, where Harrison,
Jones, and Devlin now have a big warehouse.
Cox died in 1831; his wife in 1847, after which
Richard Hill, M.L.C., occupied the house. He
died there, aged 86.
* * * *
At Broadford (Vic.) a boy of 15 has con-
fessed to the malicious wrecking of the local R.C.
church, and has been charged with “sacrilege.”
Any other kind of wreckage would have been
called plain, ordinary house-breaking and destruc-
tion of property. The extra penalties attached to
sacrilege seem to be imposed on the supposition
that the Deity is less able to defend His
property than an ordinary person, and requires
special police protection. To be strictly logical,
the charge of sacrilege should be dealt with
separately. The church-breaker should serve the
ordinary sentence for burglary and damage. Then
when his sentence expires he should be placed for
an hour in an exposed spot in the presence of an
immense multitude, so that a Higher Power can
conveniently make an example of him by lightning
if it so desires. If it shows no such desire then
the subject might as well drop.

“Petramos”: Adelaide rotunda on a
night. A drunk wends his way through the scores
of couples seated upon the sward. He pauses
before a lady and gentleman, and assumes a digni-
fied demeanor. He addresses the male canood-
ler: “Me dear man (hie) wh —why dontcher
marry the girl ? ” Some laughter from all round,
a rush by the man addressed, and the next morn-
ing the lawn-mower gathered up several stray
pieces of delirium tremens.

A parson’s wife in a Victorian country
town has a great business capacity. She calls
upon each new arrival,
and cautiously inquires
by which particular
road to Heaven they are
travelling. If it is that
attended to by her
husband she congratu-
lates them, invites
them to join her social
set, and persuades them
to belong to the various
clubs, etc. If not of
her kind, sympathy is
expressed, “as no other
ohurch has the means
of getting its members
into society. But you
may become a member
of our tennis club.”
They generally accepb
the proffered kindness.
After more talk, dur-
ing which the stranger
is inveigled into fur-
ther sundry societies,
“ Good-bye ! Oh ! by-
the-way, you may nob
play on our tennis
court unless you are a
memberof ourchurch.”
Occasionally the privi-
lege of playing tennis
is too good to lose for
such a small matter
as that. Hence the
success of that parson’s church. The other
churches are now searching wildly for
temptations, and some startlingly wicked
ones are suggested.

On the Point Nepean road, at Chel-
tenham (Vic.), is a heap of road metal, and
alongside is a notice on a post—“ On this
spot will be erected the Moorabbin Soldiers’
Memorial in honor of those who fell in the
late war. Donations will be received by ”
etc., etc. That notice has been up a long
time now, but the memorial hangs fire.
Occasionally a wild-eyed man with turnij s
in his hair hunts the secretary up, but nob
to bestow a donation. He is generally
some suburban cocky who had
been taking a stroll late on
Saturday night, and fell over
the obstruction, and broke the
bottle, and spilled the Sunday’s
beer. So far only one of Chelten-
ham’s heroes has received any tang-
ible recognition of his valor: a captain
has had a suburban racehorse named after him.

“ We are all socialists now ” —in connec-
tion with things that there’s no boodle in. Even
£ £ Bevan, the Melb. Freetrade and anti-socialist
parson, got on to a platform the other night to
advocate that the State should take over that part
of his own business which doesn’t pay. The
Bishop of Melbourne had moved in favor of “ the
introduction of Bible lessons in the State schools
as part of the school curriculum and Bevan,
who seconded it, said that, though hitherto he
had approved of free, secular, and compulsory
education, he was now satisfied that the churches
wouldn’t “fulfil their duty of providing religious
instruction,” and therefore he was driven to
advocate State action. Besides being an anti-
socialist’s tribute to socialhm, this statement of
£ £’s is about the nastiest thing said of the
churches for a good while. In short, it means
that the churches hang off religious instruction
where the instruction can’t be followed up with a
collection.

Bishop Langley’s expressed opinion, at
Bendigo (Vic.), that the “great social up-
heavals” of th epresent day are an unmistakable
sign that the end of the world is close up now, is
probably the last and worst calamity for which
the Labor party and socialistic legislation have
been held responsible. It only wanted the
bringing about of the end of the world to com-
plete the list of disasters due to the Labor
party. Still, if social upheavals can briig
about the end of the world, surely the bishop
ought to heave all he knows. To go to heaven is
a. great thing, but to be of that privileged genera-
tion which winds up the earth’s accounts, and
sees the match applied to the universe, and
departs amid the yell of comets and the howl and
crash of stars, apd the falling overboard of suns
and the general Resurrection, and departs
along with the ghosts of dead Carthagenians and
Assyrians, and Charles I. without his head, and
the Upper House and the Wandering Jew, and
the Irripenitent Thief, and that sees the debris of
the Cosmos being sold for what it will fetch, and
three moons going for a penny—that would be
something worth dying for.
About this time of year a column appears
in the daily papers headed “Christmas Appeals.”
The column consists of letters to the editor from
the secretaries and presidents of so-called charit-
able institutions, begging funds or goods from the
public. Reading these letters, one is struck with
the fine, haughty, patronising tone of the writers.
Almost without variation the objects of charity
are referred to as “our poor people.” It sounds
as if the bosses of these concerns had bought some
droves of poor people at auction. Asa rule, the
full name, title, pomp, and tremendous designa-
tion of each writer are printed in capital letters at
the foot. There is much advt. in this kind of
business, apart from the half worn shirt which
goes to “ our poor.”
The eternal hunt for an easy billet. A
Maoriland corporation advertised for a married
custodian for the municipal baths. An unmarried
man applied, assuring the council on his honor that
he would marry right away if he got the job. It
fell to him on ballot. He wasn’t engaged to any
girl, so it was necessary for him to hustle. The
unattached girls hustled also. Two evenings
running he was called out after dusk to meet a
blushing voice at the gate which said, shyly, that
the partially-invisible owner would gladly help
him out of his predicament, and to have a small
hand that he was wholly unacquainted with put
confidingly in his.
* * ♦ «
Benevolent institutions are too much in
the habit of tight-lacing themselves with rules
and regulations. ; A girl, charged with murdering
her baby, testifies:
She first went to the Board of Health, then to a lying-
in-home, thent.o the Valentine-lane Institution, and then
to Mr. Green’s office in the Domain. She had tried those
places in order to get her child taken care of.
Each of these Sydney institutions had a rule or
regulation the careful observance of which con-
tributed to the murder. There is great scope for
a charity which will take in just one kind of girl
—the one that the other charities don’t provide
for. It might be called the Omitted Girl Society.
* * • *
Adelaide University Board of Discipline
spends most of its time struggling to keep the
students sufficiently quiet once a year so that
Chancellor Sammy Way may hear himself speak.
It has now got down, to trying to persuade some
of the students to suppress or strangle the others
if they won’t be silent. But, after all, the best
way would be for Samuel to be less dreary, or for
there to be no Samuel at all, or for the student
who makes a row to be locked up by a policeman
like any other rowdy, and fined £5 or one month.
* * * *
It looks as though the new Melb. daily-
paper venture will go the way of the others
through the awful disproportion of heavy
comment to crisp news. The defunct papers
of Australia nearly all started with an idea
that a newspaper can be written up. Macaulay
and Carlyle in partnership could not keep
a newspaper afloat. Yet a little daily news-
paper might forge ahead, or at all events keep
alive, in Melbourne, as a purveyor of gossip, on
the Parisian model. Paris is four times the size
of Melbourne, and would only have eight dailies
in the same proportion. But it has twice as
many. The daily paper is often started there
without any capital, and entirely depends on the
writing genius it can put in.

The Chard divorce case still drags on in
N.S.W. Law Courts, and the last decision is
another victory for the wife. The Chards were
married in 1876, but did not air their troubles in
public until early in 1903, when the husband pro-
ceeded for a decree of nullity, because of an
alleged informality in the marriage ceremony 26
years previously. He won his case after a great
digging up of the bones of ancient biography,
but the decision was upset on appeal to the Full
Court. Counter actions for divorce were then
brought by each party, and the wife won her case,
with costs. The divorce case wasn’t interesting—
just ordinary ; but the first suit, which turned on
the lavy’s neglect to properly dot its “i,” or some-
thing like that, 26 years ago, was a striking proof
that the oldest hair can be split if only there are
enough costs. Probably the reason why hair
grows after death is that the law may split it,
with costs.
WHERE THE LIVE MEN LIE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The poet has sung of the brazen sky,
And the sun-baked plains where the Dead Men lie ;
The thumper of pulpits with beaming “ chiv ”
Has droned of a Land where the Dead Men live,
Where bones that are bleached and rotted again
Draw round them the shapes and spirits of men.
And he tells of a golden land on high
Where on wings of the Morning the Dead Men fly.
It may be truth or but hazy dream
Where the Live Men lie is a worthier theme.
Where shoddy in piles in the mart is sold
On tawdry puffs for the jingling gold ;
Where wild cat shares on a mystical rise
Go shooting aloft on a bubble of lies ;
Where preachers whose God was a homeless Jew
Lull Dives to sleep in his cushioned pew ;
Where tile-hatted swells of great renown
With invoices faked take the Customs down.
There the Live Men lie, there they crucify
The Truth ; and the Dollar is God on high !
Q.

The alien-loving press, when the exclusion’
of Asiatics was proposed by the Barton Govern-
ment, centred its huge organ of evil prophecy on'
the pearl-shellers of Thursday Island, and pic-
tured the pearl trade as passing thence to the'
Dutoh settlement of Merouke. John Douglas,
Thursday Island magistrate, visited Brisbane the- 1
other day, and here is what he says after a year
and a-half of anti-alien law:—
The interest at Thursday Island in the Dutch port of
Merouke appeared to have subsided, and the threatened
exodus of pearl-shellers from Thursday Island to that
port has not taken place.
That should encourage timid legislators to bear
up against similar evil prophecies when asserting
white rights.

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, December 22, 1903.
Mt Dear Arini,—
A fresh lot of naval men are with us. One foot
on sea and one on land—to one girl constant
never. The cruiser Scylla took away the old lot,
a cargo of lieutenants, doctors, paymasters and
clerks, men who were more or less popular and
who accepted a large quantity of entertainment.
In some instances they also accepted wives. The
seaman is the landswoman’s prime favorite,
either for a husband or for a flirtation. He
rarely stops long enough on land to allow
her to find out his limitations, or to
bore her. And he returns from each journey
over the blown wet face of the sea like a
bridegroom to a bride if he be wedded, or if un-
attached, to look for fresh summer pilots to guide
his. empty head unto the shore of nothing.
(Think that’s Tennyson, but -believe it was
“heart”—not “head.’’) The naval men who
have left us carry poetic memories of their war-
ships’ summer anchorages in Tasmanian waters.
There are some very eligible ones amongst the
new arrivals. Australia is no place for the “ well-
born ” but poor young man who wants a rich wife.
We have no heiresses (save one) worth men-
tioning.
“Hop.” returned from putting a girdle round
the earth in six months, is still like that portrait
of himself (in Ashby’s street-case), and signed by
himself, “ I recognise it.” He saw most of our
Australian artists and writers who are in London,
and he says that, judging by their prosperous
appearance, they are at least not adding to the
natural gloom of that city. He was in New York,
but there aren’t many of us coruscating in that
old city, In San Francisco “Hop.” saw Marion
Drewe, a Sydney girl black-and-white artist. She
thinks of giving up that kind of art, and is
studying for the stage. Of his Japanese memories,
about the strongest is that of seeing women doing
navvies’ work. At Nagasaki, his liner was coaled
by women. They stood in line passing the baskets,
laughing and chattering as if they enjoyed the
labor. Coming back here in a German boat, he,
in common with other male passengers, felt at
least a kind of comic indignation at the way the
women passengers inhabited the smoking-room-
leaving their drawing-room a desert. Smokers
were driven on deck, where, in wind or rain, or
both,, they might be seen trying to smoke
under the lee of something or other. If
one continued to pipe in his lawful quarters a
woman would sniff an aesthetic sniff and say, “ I
smell smoke.” Not a man had the sand to assert
himself, and to settle stolidly down to puff with a
pound of strong plug tobacco as his ally. On other
liners there is a notice over the smoke-room door
warning off ladies. I’ve heard that little Emily
Soldene was the only woman who could success-
fully defy the notice without scar ing out- the men
occupants of the room.
Percy Grainger’s 56 mile walk (mostly through
the night) in 16 hours in Maoriland was the cause
of his getting some curious letters. A professional
walker wrote offering to walk him “ for a purse.”
A lily-maid, wrote asking him if he had walked for
the purpose of composing a nocturne. She knew
it could not be for a new moonlight sonata,
because there was no moon that night. One
serious wight took it for granted that Percy would
not d.o long walks if he did, not suffer from
“nerves* and want of sleep,.’’ and went on to
advise a course of thought-power and reading.”
The book that helped him most (to sleep probably)
was In Tune With the Infinite.” Some persons
evidently think that no vigorous person walks
from choice.,
A valuable heirloom left Maoriland when Miss
Ada Crossley brought away a certain tiki—or god
—which Mr. Seddon gave the prima donna. It is
the same kind of grotesque deity which Maggie
Papakura, the guide, used to wear on her neck in
Sydney, only the material of it is different from
Maggie’s greenstone. The Crossley tiki seems to
be very old smoky ivory with opal eyes. Miss
Crossley wore it on her breast at her matinee for
the charities last week.. The image has been the
cause of tribal wars and family feuds, and women
have married! and been married for it. Also the
contralto, was given some of the sacred huia
feathers and divers green-stone charms. She
deserved a few gifts, for M.L. certainly thinned
her down grievously. Manager Tallis has deve-
loped bulging eyes through wondering at the
ready-money condition of its people. Every one-
horse township yielded a fine audience, and
shambling old fellows who didn’t look as if they
had a copper would book five-shilling seats.
Last Wednesday was an epoch-making day.
There should have been signs and wonders, and
failing these, many did not believe. Atmo-
spherically, there was nothing to distinguish it
from an ordinary day of sun, clouds, and dust.
Last Wednesday enfranchised woman voted for the
first time in N.S. W. She was there with a baby in
her arms, too ! Opponents of the franchise used
always to clinch their arguments with a word pic-
ture of a woman with a baby in her arms jostling
her way to the ballot box through crowds of men
to an accompaniment of coarse jests and ribald
laughter. There wasn’t as much jostle as Molly
the Marchioness used in getting to the throne on a
presentation day. There was no laughter or jests
—not even a faint show of curiosity on the part
of a male voter. The she-voter wasn’t half as
interesting as the cricket scores, and she was no
more concerned about the ballot paper than if she
were buying bootees for baby.
i ? ne P rotea * Ol >ly I heard, and it oame from a
lady of 80 year* or so, who arrived in a|bath-chair
to see her grand-daughters vote. “If Queen
Victoria had lived this wouldn’t have happened’’
she said. The republication of a letter written
hy *he late Queen in 1870 reads curiously at
this juncture. “The Queen is most anxious to
enlist everyone who can speak or write to
join m checking this mad, wicked folly of
Womans Rights, with all its attendant horrors,
on which her poor feeble sex is bent, forgetting
every sense of womanly feeling and propriety.”
And yet but for the assertion of woman’s right to
succeed to the throne, where would Victoria have
been?

A well-known Sydney merchant, who recently
divorced his wife, had as his principal witness a
lady friend of the respondent—one who seemed to
have accompanied her everywhere, and shared
her room, and received her tender confidences.
Now a marriage is in the air, and witness, 'tis
said, will soon be a wife.

Patti carries her sixty years lightly. Her face is lined,
and the most generous make-up will not hide the ravages
of tune. But the figure, that exquisite figure, which wa®
always a wonder, is still in the prime of life and bids fair
to outlive the face. In her stage demeanor the great
prinni donna has not changed. She is apparently as
Bu r Pr^ a k h er bouquets and her applause as ever,
and she loves to play her pranks on the audience. She,
whose memory is proverbial, came forward with a sheet
of music from which she pretended to sing “The Last
Hose of Summer,” which every child knows by heart and
which she has sung in public 3679 times. She also cast a
glance at Signor Sapio’g “ Home Sweet Home” sheet to
make quite sure of her part. This song she has sung 798 L
• v!L and when she Ban £ it for the 7985th time last
night there were some in the audience who quite agreed
with her that there is no place like home, and wished,
they had remained there.
Let this be a warning to Melba and Crossley to

.PX Xmas hegira has begun, but the vacuum
is being rapidly filled from inland, and our pave-
ments resound with the boots of country people
buying watches, new clothes, and pictorial post
cards, and talking about the gigantic wheat yield
of 28,000,000 bushels.
From Pater’s Printery, Melbourne, comes an
uncommon fine set of post cards—comic, classic,
Australian, and figurative. You know the way
ships that pass in the day speak each other in
passing with flags ? One set of these picture cards
carries the different flag signals in colors with the
written message. Very suitable for the outward-
bound to send to the left-behind from different
ports.

Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ Ada Crossley certainly has thinned consider-
ably. At the Thursday, concert glasses went up
for an astonished inspection of that grey
figure, and the conclusion was that Ada’s
enviable curves were less marked and she was
beginning to look lean. A sinuous, grey garb
pretty well minus a corsage, accentuated* the fact.

specimen of the art that conceals- art. A deep
frill of grey-toned cob-web lace came from the*
skirt silk—no sign of the joinev’a handicraft—it
simply came. Then a faint illumination, like
dawn in winter, of dull steel; and silver foliage*
blossoming into, steel flowers a-down the front-
panel. The audience, too, held some fine frockery
—notably Mrs. Claude Macdonald, in a wonderful',

cheerily about town in a heel-tipping grey frock;
with bright pink decorations. A horseshoe of
cornflowers was placed, in a poetic spirit and an,,
unhappily, not-justified optimism, over the heart
locality. * Nervous ? ’ she answered anxious*
females; ‘ Not a bit of it. Yes, I think I’ll poll
well. As yet, it’s a mix-up. Prophecy is useless..
But, however it goes, I’m satisfied at my work..
The cause has gained ground, and I’ve broken*,
down no end of silly prejudices throughout town.
and country.’ Miss Goldstein’s spirit is admir-
able. The next night, when the returns had
settled no end of hopes and fears, and the Gold-
stein score was found to be quite inconsiderable,
Vida, in decorative black and pink, enjoyed the
Crossley concert and didn’t blink an eyelash. Few
women are such good losers.
“Nothing untoward happened to women at the
polling booths. They went in droves, and got to
work with automatic steadiness. Very occa-
sionally a perfect lady came with vine leaves in
her hair, but a handy Robert passed her out, and
dullness reigned again. The baby who was so
great a bugbear in tne speeches of anti-suffragists
turned out to be a very rare occurrence. Just
nowand then a womanly voter.swung her off-
spring dexterously under her left arm as she got
to business with the recording right. But not a
wail was heard, and in no instance was the blue
pencil used as a teething-comforter for the
young ’un.
“ The squattocraoy is wearying of its home-
stead in this beastly country. The George Chirn-
sides, like the Rupert Clarkes, have taken a
fashionable perch in the Big Smoke, and are
settled there when not gadding on the small patch
which calls itself the Continent. Two other
merino families contemplate a similar removal to
Mayfair. The children, don’t you know, have to
be put to school, and their twang repaired
in the English colleges ere it is too late, and
the fond, maternal eye must supervise the
polishing process. No wonder an Australian
Tea Room flourishes in the Big Smoke. And
what a fine harvest there will be when the
absentee-tax comes along, and these people who
find Australia a good enough place to keep them,
but not a good enough place to live in, have to
pay for their superfine delicacy !
