“Talk of wedding outfits, ’tis agreed among
local Smartest that the luckiest of recent brides in
4l*e matter of valuable presents from her husband’s
people, was the handsome daughter of a Supreme
Court Judge, who met her Fate abroad, in the
shape of an army man of wealthy parentage and
very large private means of his own. When the
wedding was celebrated here, not so very long
ago, the showy bride sparkled with presentation
diamonds, gifts from hubby and his relatives,
while her white train was edged all round with
valuable ostrich-plumes, brought to his liege-lady
from Africa by the generous warrior. Said
moneyed match caused quite a simmer of excite-
ment among the bride’s intimates, several of whom
'(among them the daughters of another Supreme
Court Personage) still persevere with the pre-
scription, so successful in her case—foreign travel.
“ Aide Browning set a croquet tournament roll-
ing before he sailed away, and it has been kept
a-roll with more or less success ever since. The
most swagger players started the season with
•competitive scorings at the Kooyong, but have
•come along to the Warehousemen’s Sports Ground
for the final. But a grand assortment of trophies
failed to give the game the necessary spice of
■excitement. Croquet will always be the recrea-
tion of pallid curates and listless young women.
It doesn’t even kill time—only bruises it a little.
“ The English papers have hinted at the coming
fashion of Diana astride, so the she-sports of the
Western District have been trying it on. I hear
•of a golfing champion who takes to the notion
strongly, and canters astride her hack along the
roads of Colac to the amazement of Daddy Hay-
'seed and his neddy. But by next winter the
horsewomen of the whole country will have learnt
the trick. The comfort is great, but, unfor-
tunately, the garb that goes with the new, light,
masculine saddle is cut with a quite painful regard
for propriety.
“Melbourne people unanimously curse the
City Council’s system of watering the streets
with sand, in order to ‘lay’ the stickiness of their
tarred surfaces. This horrible innovation has
had more than a year’s trial. Half a million
garments, on a rough calculation, have been
smudged by tar; the dust nuisance is perceptibly
worse this year than it ever was before; and a
mew sort of sandy blight has been introduced into
the community. The discredited ‘ cure ’ for city
•dust is understood to come from America, where
it was probably invented by civic boodlers who
•did business in sand and tar.
“Either the demand for petticoats in politics
is stronger in Victoria than in N.S.W., or
the great difference between the polling of the
two States is due to Vida Goldstein’s superior
■energy and business management. Vida’s enthu-
siasm was appalling. In order to be the first
person to poll for Melbourne electorate, she got to
the Athenaeum Hall at 7.30 a.m., half an hour
before the doors opened, and waited in wet boots
for the historical event. The average married
woman doesn’t take her first baby more seriously
than Vida took her first vote.
“Adelaide University Commemoration was this
year a spectacle not undignified—for the first time
for many years. This was due partly to the
determination of the Council to stop the old cater-
wauling, but largely to a feeling among the
undergrads, themselves that the tiling had gone
too far; also to the influence of Governor Le
Hunte, who, when they apologised to him per-
sonally on a previous occasion, talked to them,
like a big brother, and then made them go and!
apologise also to their somewhat unpopular Chan-
cellor. Under the new arrangement, the students ;
keep their printed programme, and are allowed,
time to sing a highly personal verse as each can-
didate for a degree goes up ; and in return they
refrain from interrupting the speakers. In a word,,
they are now ‘ funny without being vulgar.’
“Melbourne householders are becoming more-
imbued every day with the idea of batching in
flats. A big Flinders-street garter merchant
broke up his happy mansion the other day, and
scattered the accumulated home treasures of
many years for the purpose of going to live in a-
flat, which, after all, seems to be merely the joy
of crowding into a very small space, and having;
no garden.

“One of the most attractive of Collins-street
charmers has divorced parents, with whom
she takes her turn at the promenade. One
day she pairs off with her petite mother. . Another
day she trots about with papa. There is another
instance in Melbourne of this sort of thing, where
the daughter is allowed her hitched-off father’s
company one day in the week, and it is made a
time of great festivity, in order, perhaps, that the
other six days spent with the permanent she-
parent may seem dull by contrast.
“ There is an outcry among the migratory souls
of this city against the paying-guest style of
lodging. An American matron, who has been in
rotatory residence here a few years, tells how cruel
is the treatment of the paying guest. The gentility
that the name implies is catered for by a meagre
board and a lot of show. The finer the surround-
ings, the skimpier the menu. The paying guest .
grows thin and anaemic. She has her silk cushions .
to recline on, her flowers to perfume the air,
but not . enough victuals to eat; and to complain t
is a vulgarity that would kill your lady hostess.
The dame says: ‘l’m sick of the paying-guest ,
business. I want to be a boarder and have enough «
to eat. A smart establishment by all means, but,
one that will feed you.’ ’ ’
“One young woman who might have been a*i
typewriter goes one better by clerking it to a city
lawyer. She’s as slick as any Yank speculator,..
and whisks in and out of the public offices, getting
through the red tape business with a neatness,and
despatch no man could beat. No larks, either.
She wastes no time on giggling youths of the
Lands and Registration departments. She just
goes right in and gets through her work, her
mind bent on business, and cogitation shadowing
her brow.
“The evolution of the safety pin is something
to wonder at. From secret service through many
generations, it now does its work in the light of
day and unashamed. Designed on a gigantic
scale, the mammoth safety pin is used to catch
the whole back brim of the hat on to the head !
upholstering. There’s no missing it this time.
Think of the advance, from the secret sanctuary
of baby’s wardrobe to the topmost and loudest*
position in the smart grown-up’s toilette.
“Most women find their men relatives ai
nuisance in flower-gardens. They always want to'
cut down one’s floral pets for the sake of neatness;-
and She never knows when He may run amok
with garden-shears or lawn-mower, and clip all the
individuality out of the scene in a trice. Man
should devote himself to vegetables. There’s
money in even a small plot of ground if planted
with the right sort of legume. Three shillings'
expenditure will return nine shillings’ worth of
potatoes. Cabbages and cauliflowers can be bought
too cheaply to make them a profitable crop for an
amateur, but broad-beans are well worth growing.
Use them when they’re tender, little things before
they develop husks. Britishers let all their vege-
tables get too old before gathering. German
cooks serve up dishes of baby-carrots, ever so
much more delicate than the full-grown root.
Yours affectionately,
AKENEHI.


YOUR CHILD.
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills are the best medicine in the world for making sickly children grow up strong
and healthy. What children need in the growing time is plenty of good rich red blood—and Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills actually make new blood. They are the surest cure for all troubles of childhood, like rickets,
peevishness, diarrhoea, stomach complaint, general weakness and St. Vitus’ dance. They, too, are just the
medicine that the little ones like—for they are perfectly harmless, sweet, pretty, and easy to take. They do not
contain the smallest grain of mercury, arsenic, or any other poisonous drug. There is nothing in them that
could harm a babe on the breast. They contain, in fact, just the elements that go to make pure, strong, rich,
red blood. For young children the dose is one-third of a pill, while those over ten should take half a pill, after
meals. What Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills did for little Katie Williams they will do for any other child in Australia.
“ Katie began to sicken with her teeth,” says Mrs. Jane Williams, 48 Greek-street, Glebe, Sydney. “ Then at last
Eickets began to set in. Her stomach got right out of order till she had hardly the strength to move. Her whole body
was sore and tender, and the pain across her little back was terrible. It was pitiful to hear her crying' even when the doctor
touched her. I had special food for her, hut the poor mite was far too sick to touch it. She just lay like one dead. Her legs
had no life in them, and were so twisted that at last they had to be put in sp’ints. Sea baths and every other sort of treat-
ment did not do her a bit of good. Then I heard how good Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People were for little children,
and I started Katie with them. After the first few boxes she seemed better. In a few weeks new life came into her legs, and
she began to walk a few steps. The pains and soreness left her body and she picked up in every way. I kept giving her the
pills until she was plump, healthy, and free from every trace of Rickets. Now Katie is full of life and energy—and, for saving
her life, I can never give enough praise to Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People.”
2s. 9d. a box. Dr. Williams’ Medicine Co., Queen’s Place, Sydney.
Six boxes, 15s. 3d.

PERSONAL ITEMS.
Peter McShane, erstwhile cricketer and
footballer of Victoria, is dead at the age of 43
years.
A nigger-loving candidate for Senatorial
honors at the recent election once wrote on a big
official report, “Minite: Mr. Blank must wait
until I here further as to this matter.”
Judge Boucaut (S.A.) was so ill a little
while ago that it was not expected that he would
over sit again. But he has perked up wonder-
fully, and may hang several people yet.
Ex-schoolmasters who carried the Labor
flag, had no luck. Griffith, in N.S. W. ; Rheinold
in Q. ; McMasterin Tas. all put up a splendid
fight against great odds, but were beaten.
A. 0. Barrett, the Laborite, who tried to
knock out the pugnacious Conroy in Werriwa,
served in the Soudan campaign, and a few years
ago raised a company of Australian Rifles.
Adelaide’s best-known democratic priest,
“Father John,” of the Preceptorists, whose ring-
ing sermons often filled a good part of the local
Labor paper, died the other week, aged 35.
A veteran of 82 contested the mayoralty
at Port Adelaide this month, and ran a bad last
in a field of three. At the official declaration of
the poll he remarked that he would have another
try in 1904.
The growing opinion is that Rev. Bill
Macky is not only Jesuitical in methods, but
really is a Jesuit. His moustache and calves are
the exact counterpart of those worn by Jesuits on
the eve of the French Revolution.
H. W. Lee, who downed Frank Clarke at
the Cowper, has lived amongst butter all his days.
Butter is almost the sole subject of his conversa-
tion. He claims to have originated the creamery
system of manufacturing the commodity.
The S.M. Herald had one frank line,
anyway, on the morning after the elections. In
its biggest type it described one of the Argus
anti-Labor four in Victoria as “ Colonel Sim-
pleton”—“Colonel Simpleton (anti-socialist),
13,273.”
N.S.W. Attorney-General B. R. Wise
sickened of his legal studies in England once, and
bolted to America with the intention of going on
a cotton plantation in Georgia. The sickness
passed, however, and he returned to England
and law.
South Australia’s Deputy Postmaster-
General Todd has finished his 62nd year in the
public service, counting in the time he spent in
Greenwich Observatory. In this period he is
alleged to have punned on every word in the
language.

Dr. Liddell, Federal member for the
Hunter (N.S.W.), adds one more to the athletic
politician class. He belongs to numerous sport-
ing clubs, and talks horse, oricket, and football as
an expert. He is a native of Maitland district,
and a year or so over 40.
A well-known resident of Bathurst
(N.S.W.) was, some little time ago, appointed a
J.P. A customer waited upon him on business, but
had to interview his wife, who stated that “his
worship was over the road at the police court dis-
tributing justice to the people.”
The only student at Adelaide University,
since its establishment 26 years ago, who has won
first-class honors in both mathematics and physics
in the final examinations for the arts and sciences
courses, is John Raymond Wilton, aged 19, the
son of a well-known Adelaide journalist.
Anthony Mills, who died atTimaru (M.L.)
lately, aged 63, was, as a young man, armorer in
the British navy, and served in the Baltic and
Mediterranean fleets. He commanded a company
of British volunteers organised to assist Garibaldi,
got wounded twice on entering Naples, and was
operated on and nursed in Garibaldi’s own house
Another dead miser has transpired in
Victoria. The remains of William M'Mahon,
85, were found in his hut at Oastlemaine. On
searching his clothes deposit receipts for £4500
were found. A large number of M‘Mahons who
wouldn’t speak to William a month ago have now
recognised him as a beloved and long-lost relative.
The Vic. Assembly redistribution of seats
has cut the Jolimontand West Richmond seat to
pieces. It has belonged to Theodore Fink since
Bosisto, the eucalyptus man, died. Fink is now
politically like the Wandering Jew, and says he
will retire and devote himself to his business. He
is an able man—a man with a brain and a wobble.
The trouble is that the wobble is bigger than the
brain.
H. C. L. Anderson’s model of the Burns
statue presents a very commonplace, tide-waiter
sort of person, without any suggestion of “Scots
Wba Hae” or “Tam o’ Shanter.” It is quite
likely to be a truer likeness than the majority,
but truth is seldom satisfactory where likenesses
are concerned. Not one great man in 100 looks as
he ought to.
Grandpa Hegarty, of Surry Hills, Sydney,
attempted suicide, the other day, because he
believed there was to be no old-time pantomime
in Sydney this Christmas. He had taken all his
children to the show, and, in late years, his grand-
children, and he broke down at the threatened
loss of his annual treat.
John Norton’s action in fining himself at
Mackay for letting off artillery in a public place
followed in the steps of early N.S.W. precedent.
Judges who kept the court waiting used to pass
coins into the court poorbox. Barristers, for
similar infractions, not only paid over coins, but
at the close took the jury out for a drink.
Astronomer Russell, who retires from
N.S.W. service at the age of 68, has led a placid
life at Sydney Observatory for over 30 years. A
couple of times only was the surface of his exist-
ence ruffled—once when a bumptious assistant
fought him for his place of power, and once when
someone sent him a comet or some similar explo-
sive, and nearly scattered his remains over his
own telescope.
“ Min ” : Tom Mann, like his predecessor,
Ben Tillett, has become the model of rising
Victorian Labor orators. His most successful
mimic, up to date, is Jack Lemmon, whose
representation is exact in every detail, even
Mann’s lingual defects being copied. His sing-
song elongations, nasal drawl, provincial accent
and too rapid utterance are most faithfully repro-
duced, when not slightly exaggerated.
“ Burglar Bill ” : Rather pleased to hear
of Steele Rudd being retrenched. To think of our
greatest humorist being an under-sheriff always-
made me feel mad. I once saw an under-sheriff
walking third in a hanging expedition. Bob
Howard walked first, the governor of the gaol
second and then the sheriff. I watched them
knock at the door of the condemned cell, and the
sheriff made his demand for the body, which sat
alive inside. It isn’t the billet for a humorist.
Inspector-General Fosbery has just com-
pleted 30 years’ headship of N.S.W. police. He
has been half a century in the public service.
The old house in Phillip-street, where he began
his police work, is gone, and the men who held
dens in the building when Fosbery clerked there
are almost all in the cemetery. It was the first
Sydney residence of the late Alfred Stephen, and
some members of his family were born there.
The present acting-Chief Justice, when a very
young boy, slid cheerfully down its narrow stairs
and whooped in its scanty precincts.
The Rev. Colin Campbell, who died at
Hawthorn (Vic.), the other day, aged 86, had an
unusual career for a cleric. A squatter in Vic.
from '39 to ’5l, he then, for a time, succeeded the
late H. C. E. Childers as secretary to denomina-
tional schools. Between ’54 and ’59 he sat in the
old Leg. Council and the Leg. Assembly. After
another spell of station life, he returned to the
Assembly in ’73, and in ’77 unsuccessfully opposed
Bent at Brighton. He then took holy orders in
the Ballarat diocese, having been a volunteer for
church work for 25 years. Superannuated in ’B9,
he occupied himself with the promotion of reli-
gious instruction in State schools.
Mahutu te Wherowhero, “King” of the
Maoris, who became a satrap of King Seddon,
and received as reward a call to the bedroom
chamber of the Maoriland Parliament, made his
first speech two days before the session closed.
He had for five months been wrapped in deep
thought, or else absent, but when he did speak he
made strenuous objection to a measure brought
down by the Government of which he is supposed
to be a member. A horrified Attorney-General
tried to explain to him the doctrine of Ministerial
responsibility, but Mahutu objected that the Bill
had been brought down too suddenly, and
moved that it be postponed until next session.
As the Bill had been on the stocks for 15 months,
the instance was an illustration of the Maori’s
inclination to think one year and act next century.
Marcus Clarke, by one who knew him :
Saw a good deal of Marcus Olarke in the late SO’s and
early 70’s. I sum him up in this way: Good-looking,
and possessed of personal charm. Always well dressed.
He needed to be so, because he could not be impressive
like some greater men in shabby or ill-fitting clothes
An excellent conversationalist. Of a light, Frenehy
character, and devoid of the sense of rectitude. Great
literary talent, largely artificial in its methods. He nevf r
put any real feeling into anything, having none to put
in. Neither a beautiful landscape nor a matchless
musical effort could move him, but he always admired a
fine literary performance, and would praise generously. A
literary artist, he worked on his subject like an impartial
artificer with a single eye to the effect. Outside of that
no story of suffering could touch him. Without a subject
and time to work it up, he was non-productive. To
write a novel without material within the four walls of a
prison cell, as did Cervantes, would have been a hopeless
task for him. Of distinguished literary ability (he made
a deep study of the style of Ohas. Reade), but no genius
in the sense of capacity for spontaneous creation.
Clarke was always cynically indifferent to criticism of
his own work.

“Unit ” writes :
The favorable attitude of Rev. Dr. Marshall (Melbourne)
towards a famous relic of the Roman Church, “ The Holy
Shroud of Turin,” suggests that he is making a desperate
effort to stem the tide of backsliders Unitai lanwards.
Half the Presbyterian ministers of the present day are
unconfessed Unitarians. When Rev. Charles Strong, of
Scots Church, Melbourne, surrendered his ppsition in
consequence of his unorthodox views on the subject of
the Atonement, he returned to Scotland, thinking to
found a great new church there. But on interviewing
churchwardens and officers of leading kirks, he was met
by this fatal reply : “ We agree with your views and
have held them for years, but we consider it in the public
interest that the Church should continue on the old
orthodox lines.” So Strong re-packed his portmanteau,
returned to Melbourne, and opened the Australian
Church.
A letter from Yandemonia :
Dr. Elkington, the man whose courage and modernity
of method so promptly stamped out the small-pox
epidemic that Launceston had been gently remonstrating
with for months, has been appointed Chief Health Officer
of Tasmania, with bisr powers and an almost limitless
discretion in case of epidemic or other serious emergency.
Elkington—in face of the difficulties which always
beset a new-comer to Tasmania —has distinctly caught
on. If he continues to take his office strenuously,
he has any amount of work to do. The adminis-
tration of the health laws in Tasmania has always
been a very squalid sort of farce. The marvel is,
not that Tasmanian centres have been so healthy, but
that there has been any health in them at all. The
ports have been practically unprotected against
contagion from the deep sea. The inspection of
dairies and cow-yards has been, at best, patchy
and perfunctory. Sanitary conditions—outside of the
actual city area of Launceston —have been injurious. If
Elkington goes to work to the full of his powers he’ll
raise the deuce of a dust to begin with, and a good many
ancient fossils of great repute and grave stupidity will
squeal most damnably ; but the place will begin to get
clean and many prehistoric stinks will vanish.
Recently deceased Shirley W. Baker began
life as a Wesleyan school-teacher at Chewton
(Vic.). In ’6O he went to Tonga as a Wesleyan
missionary. After a number of years he resigned
to become Premier to King George. He practi-
cally governed the island, and established an
independent Methodist body under the title of
the “Free Church of Tonga.” A discontented
faction made an attempt to assassinate him, and
wounded his daughter, who received gunshots
after throwing herself in front of her father. In
’9O Sir John Thurston visited Tonga, and not
appreciating the Premier’s ruse in contriving that
King George should be intoxicated at the official
interview, and also being dissatisfied with the
Free Church and with things generally, deported
Baker, directing that he should not revisit Tonga
for a period of two years. After remaining in
Auckland for some time, Baker returned to the
Islands, and occupied himself mainly with religious
work. Baker’s Free Church was a vigorous affair
in its best days, and non-adherents were flogged
with an energy which gained many converts.
Hence a great deal of the discontent above alluded
to. Baker, in short, was a horrible example of
how the church behaves when it gets a free hand.
“J.A.C.” writes
_ The American multi-millionaire is, from figures pub-
lished by London Tatler, really a person of comparatively
moderate income after all. J. Pierpont Morgan has
assured the New York Commissioners that his taxable
property, all his just debts being satisfied, amounts to a
negligible quantity. He pays, this year, tax on £BO,OOO,
and that only because he does not want to be looked
upon as a “ tax-dodder.” Poor but honest! Jacob Schiff
and George W. Vanderbilt are equally conscientious.
They are assessed on £40,000 and £lO,OOO respectively.
John R. Drexel, who has no such scruples, has “ sworn
off” altogether and professes to be a millionaire with no
money at all.
The late Monsignor O’Hea, of Melbourne,
when*he had charge of old St. Paul’s, at Coburg,
often read a sermon from a book of religious dis-
courses instead of preaching an original one.
When he was remonstrated with he used to say,
“Preach myself? Surely the people’d rather
hear Bossuet or Bourdaloue in English than me !’’
But the collections were bad. Besides not getting
value for their money in sermons, some of the
Coburgers thought “the Dane’’was rich enough
and didn’t want a collection. Then a smart young
curate, named Dowling, who could talk well, was
sent to Coburg. “I’ll do the preaching every
Sunday, Mr. Dean,” said he, “if you’ll let me
keep all I get above the average collection now.”
The “Dane” consented, and young Dowling
carried a mug of silver coin up to his bedroom
every Sunday.
“Johnny” Gordon, who has moved up
from the S. Australian Attorney-Generalship
to the Supreme Court Bench, is pretty commonly
thought of as a promising young fellow of 30 or
so—perhaps, by this time, getting on for 40—
whereas he is really 53, just the same age as
Kingston. , “Johnny,” who has in S. Australia
something of the same sort of reputation that
George Reid has in Sydney, was, like George,
born in a Seotchbyterian manse. Gordon studied
theology for a time, got into the law, set up in a
southern township as a solicitor, and passed into
politics in 1888. He was never reckoned a top-
notcher as a'lawyer, but be is as pretty an after-
dinner and literary-society speaker as Adelaide
has. As, at the time, the only available lawyer
in the Leg. Council, he was Kingston’s represen-
tative there when the famous row over the Ade-
laide Hospital started. The row, in fact, started
over a close relative of Gordon’s Gordon sided
with the relative, and left the Ministry. Still, in
the last 10 years he has had seven yenrs of office,
so he hasn’t done so badly. Yon Doussa, who
succeeds him as Attorney-General, is a hard-shell
Tory. Gordon is a Tory, too —but not a hard-
shell. Bundey, his predecessor on the bench,
has nearly killed himself with work. Justice
“Johnny” isn’t likely to do anything of that sort..

“ Marat,” on an old player :
This poor old George the Showman is about played out.
I hadn’t seen him perform for about ten years ; so the loss'
of the old-time fire and energy, and the slovenly way he
did his juggling business, surprised me. Last time I saw
him he was doing a balancing trick called “ Tearing Down
the Upper House,” from a hotel window in Park-street*
and it was good ; so, hearing that he was to appear in Red-
fern the other night, I went. lam sorry now. I wouldi
have liked to remember him as he appeared that night
ten years ago, when he threatened to tear down the
Salomons, Browns, and Jacobs who were blocking his.
path to fame. When I arrived the crowd was in a playful
humor. Someone up on the platform was craving a hear-
ing on the ground that he was connected with M’Gowen
& Co. for a number of years, “ Surely they were not going
to desert him.” I rubbed my spectacles and tried to make
out who was talking, but the light was bad. “ Who is
it?” I asked a bystander. “Old Dry Dog,” he said*
promptly ; and I lost my spectacles in my astonishment.
I wish someone would form a society to take actors*
clowns and stars of all classes, and care for them before
they start on the down grade. The spectacle of an elder'y,
stout old juggler trying to go through a performance
which the small boys ridiculed, and craving their indul-
gence on account of his one-time cunning, is depressing.
It reminded me of an old confidence-sharper asking pity
from a judge because he wouldn’t have been caught
picking pockets if it hadn’t been that he now pic-kecf
clumsily’ as the result of old age. When a youngster
threw in a witty interjection some pitying bystander told
him to “ pull oil.” “ He’s getting old now, and he’s not as
nimble with his tongue as he used to he,” the bystander
explained. Granny, in Hunter-street, gave him a good
notice next day, though. But, then, Granny is getting
1.0 o hack number herself

CHRISTMAS EVE.
JONES (who has been celebrating it): “Great
Scotsh! — hie — wha'ra awful fate! I mush be
losht in the bush !”
“ Minaritchee,” on an alleged article of
food:—
“ Why don’t your bush liars tell us how to make a good
damper ?” asks “Polstan” (8., 3/12/’O3). Because they
can’t. True dampers (made of flour, salt, and water only)
are of the past. A vast number of bushmen and women
cook their own bread daily, yet it is doubtful if one good
damper has been made for twenty years. I spent my
third and fourth decades along the western fringe of the
artesian belt, where every man is more or less a cook,
and the conditions are favorable to damper produc-
tion. Many thousands of flour and water effects
were observed without affording one fairly digestible
specimen. Previously, however, in the '7o’s, during a three
years’ peregrination of Riverina, true damper, palatable,
nutritious and satisfying, was tested daily ; was, in fact,
as common as cursing. But the quality declined rapidly
in the late 70’s. I don’t think the decadence was due to
loss of skill. The men who perpetrated sodden outrages
in the 80’s and 90’s in Queensland were generally identical
with the artists who triumphed in Riverina in the 70’s.
The change I attribute either to difference in the wheat
used in flour-making, or to difference in flour-milling
methods. But the bush pioneers are not without compen-
sation, for while the damper has declined, the evolution
of the johnny-cake has reached a high point. The latest
triumph can be produced in 25 minutes from the moment
you unshoulder the bluey. Method: Make a fire of
sound, dry sticks about an inch thick. Mulga, box,
coolibah, or bloodwood are best. Gidya and desert gum
are too hot and ashy ; lancewood and stringybark too
dead. Good coals will result in ten minutes. Meanwhile
mix dry flour and baking-powder thoroughly. Make
dough quickly and roll with jam-tin into circular cakes
4in. wide by jin. thick. Place on coals. They begin to
rise at once, and within two minutes each will have
assumed hollow globular form. When the underside is
browned roll the globe over with a stick. If the fire is
well planned as to area and strength the baking of a
batch will not occupy more than five minutes. The
result is an ambrosial food. If not thin enough the cakes
will not puff up, but become mere leather-jackets.

“Ed.”: The mutton-bird, or, properly,
the short-tailed petrel, comes in September, and
disappears from April to August. It nests in a
tunnel about 3ft. long, made in tussocky sand
hummocks, in the islands of Bass Straits and the
neighboring seas. The mutton-bird lays only one
egg, which, like all eggs laid in hollows, is white.
Coming in September, they clean out the old
holes, then go to sea for some weeks, and return
in the third week in November. Flinders men-
tions that when in the Straits, in 1798, he passed
through a flight; the stream of birds lasted one hour
and 35 minutes. He reckoned the numbers were
over a hundred million. The mutton-bird is not,
as “ Penguin ” (B. 26/11/’O3) says, too nauseous to
eat. Let “ Penguin ” scald a scarcely-fledged
mutton-bird to remove the feathers, split the body
down, lay it in salt for a week, then parboil it,
and afterwards fry it in its own fat to a delicate
brown, and he will decide that the sweetest rasher
of bacon is gall compared with young mutton-
bird. Have seen a young mutton-bird’s body
dried and a wick drawn through the body for use
in place of a candle. Egging has its risks;
goannas and snakes abound in the rookeries,
living on eggs and young birds.
“ A.A.” : For dare-
devil riding never saw
anything to equal that
of Monaro men, and
have had a wide ex-
perience from the
Gulf of Carpenta-
ria to Twofold
Bay. Best thing
I ever saw was
many years
back at a
cattle muster
on a station
on one of the
Kosciusko
Ranges, when
a now well*to-
d o resident
o f Bombala
named Jim
jumped
on the back
of a wild bull
from the top
of the stock-
yard gate. It
can be more
easily imag-
ined than des-
cribed how
the bull per-
formed, buck-
ingand bellow-
ing around the
yard for half-an-
hour. The gate
was thrown open,
and away over the
range went Jim and
the bull. All attempts
to yard them again were
hopeless, bull charginghorse-
men and everything that came
in his way. For two days and
nights we followed over hills and
down gullies and gorges in a vain
attempt to release our mate from his
perilous ride. Early the second day both
were showing signs of fatigue, and our only
hope was to supply Jim with some tucker and
whisky to keep him fit until his mount would
collapse. Bags of tucker were thrown at him, and
when they caught on the bull’s horns, Jim took
the provisions off and ate them. He was going
well on the evening of the second day when bull
fell from exhaustion. A friendly bullet from a
rifle put him out of his misery. His bones, I
believe, still bleach near the Soda Water Springs
at Rock Elat. Distance travelled was over 80
miles. If you don’t believe me, ask Pope Dill X.

“Ku-Klux” : He recent Bulletin par
concerning the color of the gills as a test. There
is one part of a fish which no man can fake—the
eyes. The eyes which look clayey and dull
denote that the consumer had better “pass fish.”
[But the dull appearance is merely a sinking-in of
the eyes. The old fisherman in Blackmore’s
“ Maid of Sker ” used to put that right by insert-
ing a splinter of wood to make them bulge out
again. Then he sold the fish to Hezekiah, the
horologist. This device might be worth trying.
—Ed. B.]
AUSTRALIAN INDUSTRIES ILLUSTRATED. — CABINET-MAKING, AS IT IS
DONE IN LILLY BUCK-STLEET.
“ Dogspike”: Saw during the late drought
what to me was a new method of driving pigs to
market. These ordinarily obstinate brutes did
their twenty or so miles per day along the
Goondiwindi road into Warwick at the tail of a
cart containing hunks of dead sheep, cattle, etc.,
collected in surrounding paddocks. Every few
yards a morsel was dropped overboard, and, as it
was good, healthy, loud-smelling carrion, there
was always a scramble for the tit-bit, and a
cheerful readiness to travel at the tail of the cart
in hope of more.
“ Thurla ” sends this :
«« Old Oove ” (B. 3/12/’O3) can’t stand “ H.S.B’s’'state-
ment re pastoralists and farmers (B. 12/11/03) and says
let “II S R.” localise his growl. Allow me to localise in
support of “ H.S.R.” I knew of a farmer in the Bingara
fN/S.W.) district who had two youths working for him—
ploughing and wheat sowing—for clothes and rations
only ; they were about 18 or 19 years of age. Another
farmer in same locality had a man ploughing and wheat
sowing for 12s. per week and rations, working six full
days a week, and acting as rouseabout at the house on
Sundays. So much for Bingara farmers; now for the
pastoralists : One station on the Gwydir pays its hands
from 12s. to 15s. per week, and the station drovers hi per
week, but these last only do droving from station to
station and nothing else. Then in the Manilla (N.S.W.)
district the ploughmen only receive from 12s. 6d. to 15s.
per week. Probably “ Old Cove” is a good sort of em-
ployer, and does banquet his campers on potatoes, sauce,
pickles, etc. ; but he is only one in a hundred.

“ J.L.” : Be the luck of the drunk. Some
time ago was standing on the rear platform of a
train doing about 25 miles an hour. Boozed
swagman sitting on the step asked where it ™
bound for. On being told swaggie mumbled
something about “the wrong train.” Before 1
realised what his intentions were the party with
the swag stepped off the carriage. He lobbed on
his feet, turned three and a half somersaults, and
then slid down the embankment on his head, just
reaching the bottom before his swag, which had
stopped to do a series of extraordinary bounds on
the permanent way, and to finish a kangaroo-
dance on the grass. A relief party from the next
station found the ruin busy hunting for his pipe.
Beyond about a square foot of gravel rash dis-
tributed over various parts of a very useless citizen
he appeared none the worse for the performance,
and said he would do it again for a pint of beer.

“ Myall ” in reply :
The fact that 70 per cent, of stock in Walgett district
perished in five years proves nothing against the utility
of artesian water. The first crop in the district irrigated
with artesian water was grown in 1900. In 1901 a few
landholders who enjoyed the benefit of proximity to a
bore followed the lead, but it was not till 1902 that the
bore water was generally availed of. The crops in 1902
averaged over two tons to the acre, but the aggregate
supply of fodder produced was useless for stock-raising
purposes, as only a few bones existed. Could mention a
couple of far-seeing landholders, however, who saved 80
per cent, of their flocks by the cultivation of sorghum.
Irrigation with artesian water is but in its infancy ; it is
when the next drought happens along that the benefits
of artesian water will be fully appreciated.
* * • *
“ Belmont ” about a horse and a doe :
I am sure there never was another such horse to kick
and bite. He did it so unexpectedly, too. He would be
looking a stranger square in the face, apparently about
to communicate some information of value, and then
suddenly lift his hind foot, and fetch the unsophisticated
man a rap on the head that would make him see seventy-
five dollars’ worth of fireworks in a minute. We had a
dog, a black and white, and if ever there was a dog who
thought he had a head full of knowledge it was that dog.
How plainly I can see him approach that black horse to
sniff his heels. He ought to have got a longer sniff than
he did, considering that he lost the greater part of one
ear in the attempt. It was done so quickly that we would
not have known anything about it had the dog not
spoken of it himself. He never sniffed that black horse
again. The flavor wasn’t what he had been used to,
I think. Three days later, when he was turning round
to speak to a flea near his tail, as is customary with dogs,
the black horse unexpectedly reached down and took a
mouthful of spinal joints out of the dog’s back, and
mortification from being thus caught preyed so heavily
upon the dog’s mind that he died in a minute or two.
The Bulletin has doubts about the truth
of this yarn, which comes from Westralia :
Two ringers from the Speewah, while looking for graft
in W.A., struck a cookie who wanted 861 sheep shorn.
It was just after breakfast. Cockie said, “ Put your
nags in paddock, tucker with us in kitchen, and shake-
down inside to-night if it rains.” They tied their horses
to fence, didn’t even unsaddle, shore the lot, got cheque,
and were 10 miles along the road before Gertrude
Josephine Amelia, the cookie’s daughter, hollered out
“ Dinner’s ready.”
RILEY’S GOAT—A LAY OF ELECTION TIME.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ There are many complaints of the lack of interest
taken in the elections. — Daily paper.
Have you heard of Gidya-Gidyee ? It’s a little backblox
town
Where the waters of the Barcoo and the Bunyip Creek
come down,
With a weary population, mostly idling round the pub.—
You might find it, but the chances are you’d miss it in
the scrub.
Now the men of Gidya-Gidyee all had votes, as all men
should,
And they reckoned to record them for their bleeding
country’s good ;
So they spouted in the shanty and debated heatedly—
Half the town was for M'Clusky ; t’other half was for
M'Gee.
And the town grew animated and was filled with wild
alarm ;
Everywhere you chanced to ramble you would see a
whirling arm ;
And the Tree of Friendship withered, and a worm
gnawed at its root,
As each candidate’s supporters dodged the other fellow’s
boot 1
Then the day came on for voting, and they gathered
round the booth ;
The M‘Geeites had been drinking and had nearly slain a
youth
Who had rashly classed their idol as a Yellow Irishman !
Saints above ! Their roar of anger blew the wheels off
Simpson’s van!
“Look ye here!” yelled old M'Ginty, pointing to a
william-goat
That was out upon the common, cramming sundries
down its throat,
“ See that billy-goat of Riley’s feeding there upon the
thrack 1
Well, our candidate’s a Green man—green as that old
goat is black ! ”
“ Black be domned l ” said old man Watson ; “ mon, the
sainted goat is broon ! ’’
" Ye’r a liar!” roared M'Ginty, “an’ a crazy Scottish
loon! ”
“ Fenian ! ” gasped the angry Scotchman, off came hat
and off came coat,
And like tigers they went at it o’er the color of the
goat.
Sandy fought for bonnie Scotland, in his eye the battle
light;
Pat had visions of ould Ireland as he landed with his
right.
“ But,” remarked a long Australian (all his words I can-
not quote),
“ What’s the blanky use of fightin’ 1 Why not catch the
blanky goat ? ”
As a sensible suggestion it was greeted with a cheer ;
Both the foes agreed, and Patrick’s teeth left Alexander’s
ear;
As a graceful recognition of this kindness, from Pat’s
throat
Sandy took his bony fingers, then the crowd went
towards the goat.
But that wise old goat of Riley’s had been often caught
before,
When the township kiddies rode him, and a knowing
look he wore,
As he cantered past the leaders. Then the men who’d
come to vote
Circled wildly round the township in the wake of Riley’s
goat.
Then in haste the goat departed and the aggravated
crowd,
Pantingly, like blood-hounds followed ; frequent were
the oaths and loud 1
Till two miles or three were covered and the town was
out of sight,
Then O’Grady at the goat threw heavy clods with all his
might.
Sad to say one clod when falling struck a man upon the
head
Who was solid for M'Clusky, and he fell like one struck
dead ;
And another hit O’Grady, and he raised a wailful note ;
Then those voters scrimmaged fiercely and forgot about
the goat.
Gory were the deeds of battle, loud the yells of Jim {and
'Bill
While the sunlight made a halo round the goat upon a
hill.
This and that way raged the struggle, many lay upon the
loam,
Till night fell upon the carnage; then those warriors
went home.
Not a single person voted save a solitary drunk ;
For the rest, completely wearied, straightway tumbled
into bunk.
And the unit, as to methods being somewhat at a loss,
Scratched out both the names before him and then wrote
his own across;
No ; I can’t say if M'Clusky or M'Gee it was got in ;
But though years and years have drifted o’er these thirsty
sons of Sin,
You can always raise a battle in that dreamy sun-baked
town
If you shout in through the pub-door : “Patrick Riley’s
goat was brown 1 ”
Then you’ll hear a shriek of anger, and you’ll hear a rush
of feet,
And it’s wise to run your hardest down the lone and
dusty street ;
For some men in Gidya-Gidyee would refuse a heavenly
crown
If the giver of the diadem said Riley’s goat was brown.
N.S.W.

“Polstan”: A difference between fresh
and salt water mussels lies in the faofc that while
the latter are
quite helpless
once they are
detached from
the clump or
pile to which
they belong, the
former can walk
anywhere they
please. A fresh
water mussel,
when it wants
to go to see its
girl, or attend a
Y. M. C. A.
meeting, mere-
ly gets on its
edge, opens out
its shell a little,
and then, pro-
truding a por-
tion of its body,
makes the pace,
leaving a deep
track behind it
in the mud. But a fresh water mussel doesn’t
taste as good as its salt water cousin. I cooked
and tasted one of it once, and I regret to say 1
still taste it. I’d rather eat the shell than its
occupant any day.
* * * *
“ W.W.” : In the Tumberumba (N.S.W.)
district, many people are at a loss to know what
has become of the hares. Before rabbits became
so numerous the district was overrun with hares;
now you might drop across a dozen in along day’s
ride. Some think the rabbits have driven
them out. It’s my opinion fluke has thinned
them. Noticed hundreds of dead ones a few
seasons ago. At the time they were dying so
fast, shot an old buck, poor as wood, and he was
about the flukiest animal on this earth. Rabbits
in this district are also fluky; 90 per cent, of old
rabbits are affected. Dams, tanks, and waterholes
from which water for domestic use is drawn are
seldom wire-netted, and in dry times, when thou-
sands of rabbits drink out of one small hole, water
should make good hydatid soup if well boiled.
• • * *
“ War E.” : Wonder if it is the ruthless
destruction of birds by phosphorus baits for
rabbits that is responsible for the present-day
plagues of grasshoppers, butterflies, caterpillars,
etc. ? Nature intended every bird for some pur-
Eose, and when man steps in and destroys these
irds wholesale, something is bound to happen.
That’s my theory at any rate!
“Bunny”: If the Umber-
umberka (Broken Hill) water
supply scheme had only been
taken up in earnest at the proper
time, some shortmonths ago, what
a magnificent and almost inex-
haustible supply of rain water
would have been conserved ! The
creek with the rumbling name
ran almost a banker for days after
the late storm burst on the Bar-
rir. The total Broken Hill rain-
fall for the year to date has
been 1104 points. Only once
since 1894 has so much rain fallen
in any 12 months. At Poola-
macca 205 points fell during the
recent storm. Broken Hill had
three thunderstorms in 48 hours
in a recent week. Usually the
thunderstorms are followed by
gales from the south that dry up
the land in a few hours, and
restore the dust to its damnable
dominion. But the climatic con-
ditions of the Barrier have been
much modified since the break-
up-o’-the-drought. The thunder-
storm was accompanied by the
heaviest rain that has fallen here
this year. _ Thirty-two points fell
m ten minutes. Two minutes
after the rain started the street
channels ran bankers. Had this
rain continued a little longer the
situation in some parts of the
city would have become serious.
Broken Hill is drunk on rain
water now.

what?
“Monowai” (who is also in “the kid-
whacking business ”) protests :
We have it, on the authority of Aristotle, that a man
can lie, and yet not be a liar —that, in fact, an occasional
sin does not necessarily constitute a habit. I have been
ten years a schoolmaster and only had occasion to “kid-
whack” once, and my case is only a type of many. I
humbly protest, therefore, against the nick-name. You,
I doubt not, may have had occasion in the course of your
career to dismiss an office-boy. Are you, therefore, -iustlv
to be called a “ kid-sacker” ?

THE HOLY COW.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Talk of church bazaars and religious raffles—Anglican
and Presbyterian, and Wesleyan—they are poor little
side shows compared with what the R.O.’s can run when
they choose. At Lismore (N.S.W.), the other day, a sale
of gift cattle to St. Carthage’s Cathedral brought in a
total of £2050. It is stated that, before the sale was held.
Bishop Doyle sent a sagacious cattle expert to visit the
R.C. farms and stations in the diocese, and select the
beasts that would be most suitable as offerings to the
Lord. The expert seems to have performed his duty
well and truly, judging by results.
Where the Richmond River runs
Sure the Holy Church has sons
Who will not see her want while they can spare ;
Mick Quinn, up there of late,
Put a cow upon the plate,
But Patsy Hogan beat him with a pair.
Bishop Doyle, with judgment sound,
Sent a cattle expert round
To the stations and the farms to put a cross
On the bullocks and the cows,
So that not for lack of nous
Or foresight should religion suffer loss.
Then the stately, chosen beasts,
That were sleek and fat as priests,
Came pacing in their pride of flesh and bone ;
And their joint melodious low,
At that pious cattle show,
Was an anthem swelling upward to the Throne.
Where the Richmond River runs,
Sure the Holy Church has sons
Who have hearts as big as bullocks’ hearts. ’Tis great
To see Murphy and M'Gee
Handing pigs in cheerfully,
When Bishop Doyle is sending round the plate.
CREEVE ROE.
ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile character) will he taken of
communications insufficiently stamped. Name and
address must appear on MS. No liability is under-
taken in any case re contributions voluntarily sub-
mitted, whether sent by post or handed in ; and in
no case will MS. be returned unless stamps (of any
State) accompany.
W. Watson: You say “No country has ever
yet become great in the manufacturing line [simply by
manufacturing for home consumption.” Substitute
“world” for “country "and you say, just as logically,
that this world can never be worth anything in manufac-
tures unless it exports its goods to Mars and Venus. As
to your idea that “ we must not have too many factories
until the time when we are in a position to send our
manufactured articles into the markets of the world,”
that isn’t original. Long ago a woman told her son not
to go near the water till he could swim Nada : Some
good points, but death and burial poetry is a cheerless
thing. Couldn’t you do a cremation poem—something
beginning “Do not weep, friends, while I crackle?"

off... .H.H. : “ Wisdom for Wives” is calculated to make
any wife foolish... .Towrai: “Always" merely an assur-
ance that you will stick to your best girl, which, after
all, is only a matter between yourself and the girl

Narine: (1) Again, The Bulletin isn’t opposed to
the carrying out of public works. It is only opposed to
carrying them out with borrowed money. (2) The
Commonwealth isn’t restricted to taxation through the
Customs. It can levy a land tax and use it all. (3) The
liombala site, even if it is largely private lands, could be
got cheaply. It would be the expenditure of the public
money that would make the area valuable. And, any-
how, there is more public land around Bombala than in
almost any other site so far mentioned. Also, as The
Bulletin has pointed out a hundred times, the money can
be provided by the adoption of the Canadian banking
system. Considering how often The Bulletin has ex-
plained how the money to construct a Federal capital and
acquire a Federal territory can be obtained without
borrowing, you are a very disheartening correspon-
dent J.J.G. : A long way below publication level,.

Mr. CHAS. A. LAWRENCE, Montevideo,
Minn, writes:
“ I had A HARD COUGH that resisted the remedies of
our physicians. My brother, a druggist, seeing that I
was fast drifting into consumption, recommended Javne’s
Expectorant, and I feel sure that IT SAVED ME PROM
AN EARLY GRAVE.” *
For a present? The selection of a Xmas
present is not always a simple matter: utility and
suitability do not always go together. Many gifts are
mere ornaments, and often not much at that. Without
doubt a neat camera outfit is an ideal present, and
combines at once pleasure, usefulness, and instruction ;
not merely that of an evanescent nature, but supplies a
lasting and interesting pastime. The establishment of
Messrs. Harrington and Co. Ltd., 66 King-street, is a
dep6t for the supply of cameras and photographic
materials of every conceivable kind, and in the matter of
cameras they have a huge stock to select from ; the
prices range from a few shillings to many pounds. The
cameras and outfits are so arranged that no previous
knowledge is required, complete and simple instructions
are suppled with every camera. Harrington’s illustrated
catalogue is free for the asking.*
Curious case of public forgiveness from
Windsor Gazette (N.S.W.):
AS I am innocent of any wrong to Mrs. , of
Riverstone, I freely forgive the malice which
prompted her action last week. My enemies are safe
in God’s hands.
Nov. 3,1903. JANE .
The last sentence suggests a satisfying conviction
that a Higher Power will fall on Mrs. and
deal it out to her more thoroughly than the
advertiser could hope to do.
W. N. Willis, M.L.A., as a land agent
needs no black-tracker to guide him through the
devious paths of the Land Acts. He fights all
his cases, and the harder the task the better he
likes it, and wins all the time. 21 Bligh-street.*
REFORM WITHOUT REVOLUTION. -
The great and particular merit of Hunyadi Janos
is that it relieves without discomfort, in other
words, it effects. reform without revolution. This
is because it is the best and safest natural
aperient.*
MAGIC.
In ancient days the remarkable curative properties of a medicine which possessed the power to cure
complaints so, seemingly, different in their origin as
Rheumatism
Gout
Neuralgia
Backache
Sciatica
Indigestion
Anaemia
Blood Disorders
Biliousness
Jaundice
Gravel
Stone
Bladder Troubles
General Debility
Sick Headache
would have been regarded as magical, but Magic is dead and Science reigns supreme. No effect is
now allowed to be attributed to a supernatural cause. We demand in each case a scientific explana-
tion, definite, clear, and comprehensive. In order that it may be fully understood why
Warner’s Safe Cure
cures, as it does, all of the above-mentioned disorders completely and permanently, it must be realised
that each of them is but the manifestation of a diseased or inactive condition of the
KIDNEYS AND LIVER
If these vital organs are working freely and perfectly, none of the disorders in the list given can
afflict us. This important fact will be fully realised by reading the following description of the won-
derful functions performed by the kidneys and liver.
The Kidneys filter and extract from the blood about three pints of urine every day. In this
quantity of urine are dissolved about an ounce of urea, ten to twelve grains in weight of uric acid,
together with other animal and mineral matter varying from a third of an ounce to nearly an ounce!
When the kidneys are in health, all this solid matter is in solution and is invisible. Directly the
kidneys, through either weakness or disease, become unfit to do their duty properly, a proportion of
the solid matter remains in the blood, becomes actively poisonous and causes us to suffer from uric
disorders such as Rheumatism, Gout, Neuralgia, Lumbago, Backache, Sciatica, Gravel
Stone, Bladder Troubles, and Bright’s Disease. A simple test ,to make as to the’condition
of the kidneys is to place some urine, passed the first thing in the morning, in a covered glass and
let it stand until next morning. If it is then cloudy, or there is a brick-dust like sediment/’or if
particles float about in it, or it is of an unnatural color, the kidneys are not healthy, and no time must
be lost in adopting remedial measures, or Bright’s Disease, Diabetes, or some less serious but more
painful illness will result.
The Liver.— ln the liver various substances are actually made from the blood. Two or three
pounds of bile are thus made from the blood every day. The liver takes sugar from the blood,
converts it into another form, and stores it up so as to be able to again supply it to the blood'
gradually, as the latter requires enrichment. The liver changes uric acid, which is insoluble, into
urea, which is completely soluble, and the liver also deals with blood corpuscles which have lived’their
life and are useful no longer. When the liver is inactive or diseased we suffer from Indigestion
Biliousness, Anaemia, Sick Headache and Blood Disorders.
The health of the liver and of the kidneys is so closely connected that it is almost impossible for
the kidneys to be affected, and the liver to remain healthy, or vice versa.
It is nearly thirty years since scientific research, directed specially to diseases of the Kidneys
and Liver, was rewarded by the discovery of the medicine now known throughout the world as
Warner’s Safe Cure. It was realised, at the outset of the investigation, that it was necessary
to find a, curative agent which would act equally upon the kidneys and upon the liver these
organs being so immediately associated in the work of dealing with the body’s waste material and
after many disappointments, the medicine which possessed the required action in the fullest
degree was at length discovered. Warner’s Safe Cure cures all diseases of the kidneys
and liver, and, by restoring their activity, these vital organs are enabled to rid the body through
the natural channels, of the urinary and biliary poisons, the presence of which in the system are the
cause of Rheumatism, Gout, Neuralgia, Lumbago, Backache, Sciatica, Blood Disorders Ansemia
Indigestion, Biliousness, Jaundice, Sick Headache, Gravel, Stone, Bladder Troubles, and General
Debility. Warner’s Safe Cure cures all these disorders simply by removing the cause of the disorder
This is the reason why cures effected by Warner’s Safe Cure are permanent cures. [24

Business, Robbery, etc.
“ E.E.” : The expert ring-finder dived his hand
to the footpath a yard or two in front of me, and picked
up the ring that wasn’t there. I was walking at a smart
“ bat,” and my knees—they are bony—came into violent
collision with his ribs, fairly taking his wind away. He
stood up as straight as his bruised wind-bag would allow
him, apologised humbly, and showed me an imitation
diamond ring, which “he had just picked up.” By way
of encouragement he ventured—“ Passable find?” His
eyes sparkled as he offered the trinket for sale. I
walked on smartly. He was not to be shaken off, so I
had the pleasure of his company for 100 yards or so,
whilst he explained that as a flash ring was of no use to
him, he would chance its being a genuine stone, and let
me have it for 7s. 6d. “ How’th bithnith ?” I queried,
growing tired of his company. The ring-finder’s jaws
dropped, and taking a look at my face —I pride myself on
the length of my nose—concluded I was a Jew, spat
vigorously, and turned away. You can always close a
cronk jewellery bargain by talking as if you don’t eat
ham. No sane man would try to take down one of the
Chosen People with a fake trinket.
* » * *
A novel case chronicled in M.L. disclosed a
queer state of the law. A native in a good official position
gave a publican a cheque, which was dishonored, and
latter sought to recover. The S. M., who must have been
burrowing into forgotten legal ruts in his spare time,
pointed out. that a bill given bv a Maori must have
attached to it a translation in Maori, and as a cheque was
defined as a bill of exchange, and it wasn’t translated, the
plaintiff couldn’t get judgment. Yet that native speaks
English thoroughly, and, were translations of all his paper
transactions demanded, he would feel greatly insulted.

A strange story of a cash-register. The appli-
ance indicated £27, but when the cash in the till-drawer
was counted up it was only £22. The apparently respon-
sible clerk was dismissed. On the following day the shop
cat was found climbing on the machine. It was hanging
on to the most expensive end of the keyboard, and every
time it made a fresh struggle to get up it registered
10s. 6d.
* * » *
“Angowrie”: The unreasoning, unpatriotic
prejudice of the people of some parts of Australia against
the products of their own country, and particularly of
their own district, has become proverbial, and greatly
retards manufacturing progress. On the Clarence River
several makers are turning out an unsurpassed quality of
millet brooms on a small scale ; but they and the store-
keepers selling for them find great difficulty in disposing
of the goods. One large storekeeper advised his broom-
maker not to label his brooms. The maker rather prided
himself upon the superiority of his wares and his elaborate
labels, which he hoped would advertise him and extend
his trade, and it was very much against his will that he
acted on the storekeeper’s advice. The unlabelled brooms
sold readily, and the purchasers were unanimous in
declaring their great superiority as compared with
“ local rubbish.”
* * * *
“Potchie”: Some time ago a White Cliffs
gouger dug out a unique and beautiful specimen. He
was advised to show it to some British or Australian
buyers, because it was such an extraordinary speci-
men. He declined. Said that the “Too plue” push
would give him a fair deal. He shewed his find to his
foreign friend, who valued it at £4O. The buyer paid
£4O for the specimen ; went to Europe with his wife and
family for a trip, had a good time, returned to White
Cliffs, and then calmly announced that he had cleared
something over £lOOO and the trip out of the deal. The
gouger can and does co-operate for cheap beer ; but he
will not co-operate so as to control the disposal of opal.
The united gougers could pretty well control the world’s
market. The disunited gouger lives in a bag humpy and
doesn’t control anything—not even his dog
* * * *
“Bobrid ” : The run on “fertilisers” for wheat-
crops this season is enormous, and the supply is not
equal to the demand. The writer, however, ventures the
prediction that there’ll be a slump in the fertiliser biz.
before long. The usual fertiliser gives nothing to the
soil ; its action is entirely on the grain, in rushing it up
quickly and giving it a “good start,” and this means a
further drain on the soil, “running it out” even quicker
than before. The land will become in time impoverished,
and the fertiliser will fail. It’s just like a man on a
burst taking a pick-me-up to give him a chance to go on
a further burst —it doesn’t minimise in the least the
effects of the spree. The only fertiliser is one that gives
back to the soil what the wheat-crop takes from it—not
sand and a stimulant.
* * * *
A. E. Morgana, who was Westralian Premier
for 15 hours or so, back in the company-promoting days,
has an interesting curiosity—a leather bag with several
small punctures in it, on which hangs a tale. The man
who made the holes used to own a mine, which wasn’t
quite so good a mine as he wanted some prospective
buyers to think. All the old mine-salting tricks had been
played out, including the trick of shooting small gold
slugs out of a gun into loose ore, and the other
trick of letting gold-dust fall out of the finger-
nails into the samples as they were broken.
“ No,” said this man with the mine to sell—
“ No, I don’t want to go into the workings with
you. Go yourself; take your own men ; break out your
own samples, seal them, and if it doesn’t come up to
what I say, then don’t buy.” So the samples were broken
and the bags sealed —and then the honest mine-owner
jumped at his chance to pump in some chloride of gold
with a hypodermic syringe through the leather. The
punctures are still there to tell the tale.
* * * *
To read the statistics of Victoria’s swindling
irrigation and water trusts, as set forth in the Municipal
Year Book, is enough to make one’s gorge rise. The
lovely Rodney Trust got £2J2,699 of public money, owed
£52,726 of accumulated interest; passed on the whole of
the interest and £147,699 of its debt. In the 13 years it
did business before the repudiation it paid just £l6Ol
15s. 3d. in redemption. And to-day, after all these thou-
sands have been spent on improving its lands, Rodney
shire has the gall to value itself at barely ss. per acre, and
then strike a shilling rate thereon. Tragowel Plains Trust,
in Gordon and Castle Donnington shires, which gathers
in the rich flat lands between Kerang and the Murray,
paid a whole £443 15s. Bd. off its indebtedness before it
began to default. It borrowed £158,418, accumulated
£80,141 of interest, then shuffled out of all its interest
and £123,418 of its debt. To-day it values its land at an
average of Is. 3d. an acre and strikes, one shire a
shilling, the other a ninepenny rate thereon. Numurkah
borrowed £19,757 for its waterworks and finally shuffled
out of £6,076 of capital debt and £835 interest. It now
values itself at 3s. 7d. per acre on an average and strikes
the usual shilling rate. That is the “ poor farmer” side
of the case. The city worker’s side is shown by the case
of Footscray which strikes a rate of 2s. 6d. in the £, and
Brunswick, which strikes a 2s. 4d. rate. The house the
writer occupies is rated at £32 a year, and the rent is 16s. a
week. And on top of all this the Footscray and Bruns-
wick toilers—who finally pay the rates, Whether they own
their houses or not—are sweated to find the muni ipal
subsidy for these blessed shires. And these insolent
robbers have the cheek to talk of Trades Hall domination
and the need for retrenchment!

Reply received by a N.9.W. Government
Department from a man who had received notice to
register his firm under the new Act: “Referring to
registration, I am agent for nothing whatever, or have no
business either, nor never was, so the notice is in no way
useful to me, so I return it with thanks.”

“ Victim ” writes: Time some steps were taken
to reform one grave abuse rampant in the Victorian civil
service. I know of at least 20 civil servants who do a
roaring business as money-lenders. Needless to say they
are not seven or eight shillings a day “ wages ” men, but
men with good salaries. Working by means of dummies,
they advance the orthodox fiver for a pound a month
interest. Receipts are always made out (when given at
all) “ received from Mr. Blank on behalf of Mr. Dummy,”
and so even the victim can’t squeak. They trade largely
with the needy in their own branch, and practically own
the impecunious in the Board of Shirks. Has anything
been heard of Tommy Bent’s money-lending scheme
lately?

“ K.” : Natural wild beer—what dreams of bliss
those words conjure up 1 Maoriland, where the Prohibition-
ist is running amok, has beer oozing out of the very trees.
The matai tree has a sap which is much relished in some
of the bush districts, and is called “matai beer.” Also
people drink the sap of the rimu tree, and it is said to
taste like a first cousin of the real thing. If all the pubs,
and breweries in Maoriland were closed, there would be
bush tours every week-end organised by Cook. The
only danger is that some of the tourists would take to
the woods for good.
“ Elmore ” : Are there any honest people
in Victoria now ? I went to a cemetery, t’other
day, to see the last of a friend who had crossed
the Great Divide. Noted, to my astonishment,
that all the railed graves had rows of barbed wire
on them, while the gates opening into the little
enclosures were securely padlocked. Also, on
several graves, wreaths of artificial forget-me-nots
and such like were chained to the tombstones. I
believe, since Victoria was demoralised by the
bank bursts, the people would even steal the
epitaph from the pious grocer’s monument if they
could get it off. The ghoul in charge told me
that the barbed wire was quite useless unless the
flowers were chained down, owing to people
coming with telescopic fishing rods in their
pockets, and fishing for the vegetables.
“Puebla,” from Wellington, M.L. :
Maoriland is now in the throes of the Tourist Craze, and
if things go on as they are Maorilanders bid fair to be a
nation of boarding-house keepers. No crawl is too large
for the almighty tourist. For the convenience of Rotorua
tourists passing through Auckland, the local post-office
is to be kept open till 6 p.m., as against 5 p.m. in other
parts of the State. Mere Maorilanders might benefit by
the change elsewhere. Of course the extra work entailed
on the post-ofHce officials is a detail. Again, in the new
and abortive Licensing Act, Premier Seddon inserted a
clause providing Government cocktails in no-license dis-
tricts for travelling English and American Johnnies,
while a fine of £2O was imposed on a Maorilander having
drink in his house. In the House of Reps, many mem-
bers are beginning to grumble at the waste of public
money on the tourist traffic when more important indus-
tries might well be started with the aid of a timely bonus.
Nobody seems to benefit by the tourists who do come,
except the co. which runs the 'Frisco mail boats. Even
if the traffic became a success (from the boarding-house
point of view), it would be a dismal failure from a
national standpoint. The country that relies on tourists
is pretty well damned. M.L. wants more factories, less
imports, and less patronising foreign Johnnies. When
the tourists come, let them find a manly breed of people
—not a nation of waiters, rushing around with a serviette
and saying “ Yessir.” The Lord never meant man to be
a waiter.
“ Westward-Ho ” writes from Durban,
S’Africa:
I had made three attempts to work my passage to
S’Africa from N.Q. but steamship companies have no soul,
so I had to part with thirteen guineas—most of my capital
—and sailed from Sydney in the Graochus. Said Gracchus
was a new cattle ship, and was hastily fitted up in Sydney
to carry two-legged carnivorous beasts. She had a
coolie crew, but the chief steward and second cook were
white. The weather got rougher all the time, and we
passed Albany at the rate of four knots against a nasty
sea. Seventy miles further, a leak was discovered. One
of the port dead-eyes got stove in by a floating log, and
there was a foot of water on the 'tween-decks. The
vessel was put about, and we steamed back to Albany as
fast as God would let us. Some of the passengers began
to console us by saying that the ship was unlucky. Al-
though new, she was for sale. She had had smallpox on
board, and a murder. Some cursed heavily and began to
look queer in the eyes. The fear of death, no less. But
there was no panic. I did not feel very uneasy myself,
as she did not seem to be settling and was going 15 knots
before the gale. lam not brave, but it is well to assume
a virtue if you have it not. I played my fiddle desper-
ately as a counter-irritant. We got to Albany all right,
and, after the leak was stopped, resumed our voyage.
We found Lourenco Marques to be no good. The
Portuguese put various obstacles in the way of overland
passengers to the Transvaal, and the town itself is hot
and squalid, rent and living very high, and no chance of
work. However, it is likely to be a much more important
place before long. We are now in Durban, and only two
or three out of the 45 who remained here are working,
and most of us have no money. Durban is an up-to-date
town, with a lot of unfinished buildings going up, but
the building trade is at a standstill, pending the settle-
ment of the labor question. They will probably import
Chinese, which will mean a boom for a time ; but, after
As things are, it would be madness for any mechanic, or
other person without money, to come here. I had good
reasons, or thought I had, for coming; but the outlook is
black, and the population is mostly black also.
“ Cheetah ” complains about old age pen-
sions :
Pity the sorrows of an Old Age Pension clerk ! There
are 380 old age pensioners on the list of our suburban
office, and two of us have to fix the “ old-agers ” up in
batches for two or three days fortnightly, besides our
ordinary routine work. Out of the total, 240 can’t write
—these we manage at the rate of 30 an hour. “Touch
the pen ! ” is all we say, and his or her mark is fixed in
a jiff—and the money paid over without any trouble,
especially to the old “militias,” who always stand at
attention awaiting their turn, and salute on retiring.
Some of the old women get garrulous, and wait to retail
their history of better days agone, hut we hurry them
along inexorably. They are bad, but only gnats in our
ointment to the men who can write. How we curse educa-
tion when the “ old-ager ” comes along who glories in his
1 ‘ fist,” asdisplayed in hieroglyphics on the pay-sheet. First,
he produces his voucher-book wrapped in a handsome nose-
rag. The unwrapping process is slow and shaky. Then he
hunts for his specs.—the fixing process is likewise tedious
and shaky. Then a careful selection of all the available
pens has to be made—the only one with a hair in its nib
is chosen. All effort on the part of the “ old-ager ”to
shift that hair between each letter of his name is in vain.
As he writes he rests his head on his arm, almost level
with the counter, and smudges his laborious masterpiece
with his sleeve. Then his money has to be carefully put
away; the specs, carefully wiped and recased; the
voucher-book re-wrapped in the useful nose-rag ; we have
to tell him where he put his stick—and off he ambles.
Then there is the man who can’t see, and won’t hear, and
has forgotten his name, or has come for the pension
which isn’t his. Again, I say, pity the sorrows of an
Old Age Pension pay-clerk 1

“ Ciro ” : Kyabram’s recent mission to
Bacchus Marsh (Vic.) to open a branch failed
badly. One man planked down the -necessary
shilling and promised support, but no one could
be found to second the motion—“ That a branch
of &c., &c., be formed.” The identity of the
“ lone fisherman ” who favored the establishment
of a tail feather of the ’Pootra is not quite clear;
locally they are inclined to the belief that it was
Sammy Lancaster in disguise. The ’Pootra league
firsb started under the impression that there was
some way of paying debts without money ; of fill-
ing the Treasury without taxes, but merely by
shouting; of reducing the railway shortage with-
out _ increase of rates. It thought cursing the
engine would make it burn less coal, and that
Irvine’s basilisk expression was cash, and that a
strong man could make £1 contain 255. and a
cool, calm man could pay off the deficit by the
mere glare of his eye. And all these things fell
through.
THE BRIDGE—A KIND OF POEM.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
This is the bridge that we built.
This is John Bull who lent us the money to pay for the
bridge that we built.
This is the interest we pay to John Bull who lent us the
money to pay for the bridge that we built.
This is the money that John Bull lent us to pay the inte-
rest we pay to John Bull who lent us the money
to pay for the bridge that we built.
This is the money that John Bull lent us to mend the
bridge and' to pay the interest on the money
John Bull lent us to pay the interest due to John
Bull who lent us the money to pay for the bridge
that we built.
And this is the flood that swept away the bridge but left
the debts with interest still owing to John Bull
on the moneys he lent us to mend the bridge and
to pay the interest on the money John Bull lent
us to pay the interest we pay to John Bull who
lent us the money to pay for the bridge that we
built.
M.L.

ment the Labor party’s policy might be defined
as support in return for transgressions.
Thinning out. In 1901 George Reid’s
majority was 4052. In 1903, with 4000 more votes
polled, it was 3739. From scoring 3 to Ihe has
got down to 2 to 1.
“Yes, gentlemen,” said the candidate,
fervidly, “I’ll go a step further.” He did,

beneath, amid great enthusiasm.

Curtailment of the Gov.-General’s allow-
ance recalls the fact that the first attempt to
reduce an Australian Governor’s salary was made
by N.S.W. Legislative Council, elected under the
Constitution Act of 1842, in Gipps’ time. The
late Richard Windeyer introduced a Bill reducing
the salary of future Governors from £5OOO to
£4OOO. The Bill passed, but was refused the
Royal assent —in fact, the ‘ * Crown ” is said to
have been not only hostile, but also very angry
over the matter.

sary examinations, and for
miscellaneous weeds and
sundries. It is a great
Government!
The number of State
electorates under the new
Victorian redistribution
scheme with mining interests
at one end and farming in-
terests at the other attracts atten-
tion. In some cases it looks as though the radical
mining vote had been thrown into a dull farming
district in order to make it ineffective. Eagle-
hawk, Bairnsdale, Daylesford, Korong, Ovens,
and Waranga are cases in point. Anyhow, the
mixture ought to liven up Vic. provincial politics.
Touching the Electoral Boundaries Bill in
the Vic. Assembly—“Mr, Boyd moved that
Jika Jika be omitted with the view of inserting
the word ‘Northcote.’” Alas! the word “North-
cote” is going to be inserted in a good many
places throughout Australia.
The Irish bunch in last Federal Parlia-
ment is scattered—some, like M‘Millan, having
retired, and others, like Glassey, being rejected.
Hugh Mahon re-appears triumphant. The bunch
consisted of Mahon, Glassey, M‘Millan, M‘Cay,
Higgins, Kirwan, Glynn, and Harney, their
native towns being judiciously distributed over
the tight little island.
For some reason or other the influence of
the R.C. church bosses goes solidly behind the
Protestant Malcolm M’Eacharn. Even at the
recent election, when the Catholic, Dr. Maloney,
was Malcolm’s opponent, the influence of Holy
Church was behind M'Eacharn as usual.
The Labor party in N.S.W. Assembly is,
to put the case shortly, paralysed by cowardly,
easy-going leadership. James M‘Gowen is a
man who should dream away his life on the
sunny side of a haystack ; the leadership of a
party with a big battle to fight isn’t in his line.
The biggest recent effort of the Sup-press :
Trenwith, the night before the election, addressed
a huge, enthusiastic crowd of 8000 in Fitz-
geralds’ circus—the biggest building he could get;
and next morning both Melbourne news-papers
were too busy barracking for their own tinpot
gods to notice the circumstance.
Victoria got its first chanc3 last Wednes-
day to say what it thought of the ’Pootra Parlia-
ment ; and of nine State members who asked for
places in the Federal Parliament it enthusiasti-
cally flung out all but two. And those two —
Trenwith and Wilson—weren’t ’Pootras. No
fowl ever had its neck wrung more thoroughly.
Queensland State Treasurer Kidston is
sticking to his guns. A big deputation from his
own constituency of Rockhampton pursued him
for a little loan, and was told that Rockhampton,
like the rest, mu°t pay as it goes. The Q. press is
so astounded at getting an unsqueezable Treasurer
that it says anything is now liable to happen.
Sydney Evening News had the cheek to
announce that at Hay (N.S.W.) “an attempt
was made by some unscrupulous opponents of
Mr. Blackwood to introduce the sectarian ele-
ment.” Blackwood was the Freetrade candidate
—the representative of the party that ran in the
Dill Macky interest, and put up people like Wilks
to denounce Protection as a “ Popish conspiracy.”
Tinkering to the extent of £25,000 is con-
templated to the old ramshackle N.S.W. Parlia-
mentary building. The excuse is the old story
about the invaluable library attached to the insti-
tution. The yearly amount spent on renovations
and repairs to the huge Macquarie-street hovel is
generally five figures, and it is usually debited to
the anxiety about the books. Very little is said
about anybody’s fears lest the refreshment-room
should fall down.
It would be hard to beat the cynical
impudence of Melbourne Argus in openly telling
the Melbourne electors, the day before polling,
that “ they should not forget that previous to
Malcolm M'Eacharn’s voyage to Japan he had
generously left £2OO to relieve cases of hardship.”
In its anxiety to give Malcolm a lift, his friend the
Argus practically denounced him as an individual
who had paid £2OO for votes, and called his
generosity a bribe.
So far as can be ascertained, the Federal
electoral rolls were, on the whole, a trifle more
accurate than the State ones usually are. The
differences in N.S.W. lay in this fact: At a State
election the Sydney dailies don’t waste space on
the stodgy letters of the person who missed his
vote, and just drop his plaint in the basket. But
it was a question this time of bringing discredit
on the Australian Government, so men, women,
children, infants and dogs were allowed to fill
pages of space with the story of how they hadn’t
been enrolled.
