“ H.” writes ,
Some criticism was levelled (8., 19/11/’O3) at the
various shifts worked on Vic. railways by drivers and
firemen. In consequence, one of the worst of these
shift No. 30—has been changed from North to Port Mel-
bourne, probably to escape your criticism. The driver
must start his train at 6.29 a.m., and ends his last trip at
6.50 p.m.; add on lj hours before 6.26 a.m., getting
engine ready and taking it to Flinders-street, and one
hour to put engine away, and, making allowance for the
spells, the time worked is 13 hours 10 minutes, yet only
8J hours are paid for.
In the Yic. Assembly
Redistribution of Seats
scheme, the stupid old dis-
proportion between coun-
try and metropolitan elec-
torates is maintained. Un-
der this scheme three elec-
tors in the country are
worth four electors in the
metropolitan area. No
reason is given beyond the
fact that it has been cus-,
tomary to give the country
voter a greater value than
the metropolitan voter. So
long as this state of affairs
exists, the country tax-
payers are bound to force
the Treasurer to hand back
to them in grants for roads,
bridges, seed wheat, and
remitted land rents and
water dues, more than their
fair share of the revenue
of the country. It is this
disproportion in the. repre-
sentation of Parliament
that is keeping back the
land tax, which, it is ad-
mitted by Irvine himself,
is the key to successful
financing in Victoria.
Among the obscure
privileges which N.S.W.
Railway Act sanctions, to
the detriment of the public
purse, are the concessions
of a life pass to the Speaker,
President of the Council
and Lieutenant - Governor
after one year’s service,
to the Premier after two
years’, the Chief Justice
after three, and members
of the Cabinet after four.
A President of the United
States, or of France, retires
to his private position
when his official services come to .an end., and is
supposed to have been paid for his public work
while he performed it. In Australia public men
are badly paid while in office, but hang on to the
taxpayer in small unnoticed ways when their
existence has been wholly forgotten.
“Grenadier" writes:
Have seen no newspaper comments upon the crude art-
fulness of that British mission to Thibet. The “ mission
was to be accompanied by an escort of 300 Sikhs, 3000
Sepoys, a few cannon, and “ a few British soldiers, all over
6ft. high." The heathen Lama (or whatever his name may
be) who pursues an anti-British policy in Thibet will pre-
sumably accept the over 6ft. soldiers as fair samples of
the British army, and a fine effect will be achieved. The
secret of England’s greatness is largely Bluff.
No one expected much loyalty to Aus-
tralia from Sydney Sunday Slimes and Daily
Telegraph, but their frantic demand, in the
Stelling case, that Australia should provide for
the free admission of foreign criminals, and their
tremendous denunciations of the Deakin Govern-
ment for not freely admitting foreign criminals,
were something beyond the average in the way of
folly. In the frenzy of their conspiracy against
Australia, those two organs have forgotten that
there is a point at which the unclean business may
become unprofitable.
At the bidding of the servile Peacock, the
Yic. Assembly expelled Labor-member Findley
for reprinting in the Tocsin certain remarks made
by an Irish paper about the King—remarks which
the Age and Argus had already published, or of
which they had given the substance. But Findley
was a pro-Boer, and Victoria was mad just then.
Since then it has repented of its jingoism, its
farm-burning, and its Peacock—and it sends
Findley to the Senate with nearly 90,000 votes.
The miner, taking him all round, proves
himself the solidest anti-Foreign-trader and the
straightest White Australian in the Common-
wealth. Not a single mining district of size is now
represented bv an Oppositionist. Kalgoorlie has
sent in Laborite Frazer in place of Oppositionist
Kirwan; Capricornia (Mt. Morgan), Laborite
Thomson, vice Oppositionist Paterson; Broken
Hill, Cobar, Newcastle, Charters Towers, Gympie,
Croydon, and other Westralian fields are all for
THE MAN WITH TWO SOULS.
Labor; Bendigo, Ballarat, and the West Coast of
Tasmania are Ministerialist. It is a clean sweep.
“ B.” writes :
As anticipated, the recommendations of the Education
Commissioners are to practically sweep away the present
N.S.W. education system, which, they say, is an anti-
quated thing, and nothing like what it was supposed to
be. The costly document will probably go into the dusty
pigeon-hole. The stickers who have been in the front of
the Public Instruction Department for many years would
have almost to start life afresh to carry out the proposed
changes. All their pet ideas are threatened in the
Commissioners’ report, especially the pupil teacher
system. A bolder Minister than Perry, M.L.A., is wanted
before an expensive report, such as that furnished by the
Commissioners, can be of any practical benefit to the ;
State.
When David Syme dies, the word Trenwitk
will be found engraven on his liver. Luckily for
the Age autocrat the stupendous triumph of his,
enemy cast an enormous shadow before, and pre-
pared him for the blow. On the previous night &
crowd of some 8000 people jammed into Fitz-
geralds’ Circus building to hear what Trenny had
to say about the Age and other political matters.
When Syme got a report (not for publication) of
that most impressive meeting he knew what the
ballot had in store for him, and before retiring to
bed he took half a box-full or brown beans for-
baffled boshters.

press of Victoria to sit down heavily on the-

The United States, which is a good deal-
further advanced than we are in the art of eject-
ing the undesirable immigrant, not only shuts out-
contract laborers, diseased persons, criminals,,
paupers, lunatics and Chinese, but it now charges-
two dollars admission fee to all immigrants coming-,
from outside North America. The man who.
won’t pay his two dollars isn’t allowed to land.
A shocked Maorilander lately published the-
receipt for his “head-tax,” and seemed to feel
hurt. Apropos that Maoriland bank clerk, who
was recently ejected from the States as a contract
laborer, was so carefully sent back that, when the--
vessel in which he returned stopped at Honolulu,
he was taken ashore and put in gaol during its-
stay.

27th March, 1901.
To Mr. S. A. PALMER,
Dear Sir,—For the benefit of suffering humanity I make
this statement. Five years ago I was very ill and was
reduced to a shadow, the doctor who was treating me
said I was suffering from Consumption and he could not
cure me. In the beginning of the year 1898 Mr. Thompson,
who was for many years overseer in the Printing Depart-
ment at Pentridge, who had to give up that position on
account of suffering from Consumption, and who had
derived great benefit from Vitadatio, advised me to call
at your Institute 47 Bourke-street and consult you. I did
so and you advised me to give Vitadatio a fair trial, as
it was a sure cure for me, I commenced with the medicine
and in a short time my disease began to pass away from
my system. I persevered with Vitadatio for some time,
gradually getting stronger and eventually I became quite
well and gained (1J) one and a half stone in weight. It
is now three years since I left off taking Vitadatio and it
is a proof that the disease has completely left the system,
as I have not had any return of the old trouble. I shall
be pleased to give any further information about my case
to anyone calling at my address.—l remain,
Yours faithfully,
ELIZABETH J. WILLIAMS.
For further particulars,
S. A. PALMER,
Head Office: Clarendon Street JV. f
South Melbourne.

Jas. Copeland, formerly of the firm of
M'Arthur, Sherrard and Copeland (Vic.), is hale
and hearty at the age of 87 on his South Gippsland
property, “ Drumlarney.” He remembers travel-
ling on the first railway opened in England when
a buyer for his father’s Irish soft-goods firm.
The Carruthers party is steadily gaining
seats in N.S.W. on the strength of its programme
of economy. Yet, strangely enough, nobody
even asks it what it means by economy; how
much it reckons it can run the country on;
whether it is going to borrow nothing, or
£1,000,000 a year, or £5,000,000 a year. Economy
means anything that the alleged economist likes.
Some men call 2d. extravagance, and some call
£lOOO meanness. M’Millan got charge of the
N. S. W. Treasury on the economy device, and he
had a big deficit every year, and ran up the loan
expenditure to the highest figure on record. Will
Carruthers please define his economy ?
Black-labor Cowley, Speaker of Queens-
land Assembly, swears he will carry to the High
Court the question whether the Constitution
allows the Federal Parliament to block a man
from running for a seat because he happens to be
in a State House. It was thought his mouth had
been stopped with the Speakership; but he is
aching for fight, and so lodged a paper with
a Northern Q. returning-officer purely in a
tread-on-my-coat-tail spirit. It looks, anyhow,
as though this will be the first large matter in
which the High Court will appear as guardian of
the Constitution, and superior to even the Par-
liament itself.
N.S.W. Parliamentary season closed
without the threatened keel-hauling of Judge
Docker, and that erratic person still whirls cheer-
fully in his orbit. The Judge simply does not
realise that there is anything bizarre in his Bench
arguments with juries. He lives judicially in the
early N.S.W. world, when the Judge was a mem-
ber of the jury—or rather when five or six officials
were Judge and jury. The early Judge Advocate
and his brethren on the Bench examined the
accused, disoussed his offence, and deliberated
together on the verdict. In Forbes’ day the
Bench was restricted to law and the jury made
supreme on facts; but Docker appears to have
never heard about the change.
“ D’Harum ” : Another military row at
.Adelaide. A well-known Scottish identity with
his kilts, etc., entered an Adelaide restaurant.
At a table sat three well-known citizens and a
colonel. As the Scotchman passed them he
remarked “ Good day, colonel.” The colonel
ignored the salutation, and the ire of the mon
fr’ the Highlands rose. He repeated, in a louder
tone, “Good day, Colonel.” The colonel looked
at him. “Oh, good day,” he said, offhandedly.
The old Scot stood still. “Colonel,” he re-
marked, slowly, “be a gentleman, even if y’ be a
colonel.” And then he passed on, and sat down
to a bowl of Scotch broth.
That weary person Wyatt has been orga-
nising branches of the Navy League in Tasmania.
At Hobart, with the Governor in the chair,
Wyatt told the people—
The very life of Australia depends on a strong fleet.
It will be utterly impossible to have a White Australia
without such a fleet.
And the Black-and-Tan inhabitants are so much
in the habit of applauding when the blessed Fleet
is mentioned that they cheered wildly. Hobart
is so “loyal” that if the British Royal Family
declared for a White Australia, the Labor party
would have to defend the local Chinese with pick-
axes to prevent the Very Best People of the
Island slaying them in cold blood.
Old Nat Levi, the most unconscious joke
in the whole House of Vic. Landlords, is much
concerned about “ the loss of population in the
State since 1892, as shown by a paragraph in the
Argus.” Clothed with an air of grave responsibility
Nathaniel arose the other afternoon to ask whether
the Government was about to do something to
stay emigration. Attorney-General Davies, having
duly exposed the sinful foolishness of the Argus
figures, remarked that whenever the Government
introduced a measure to provide land for the people
oranythinglike that, Nat’s vote was given againstit.
Compared with the older a,nd harder-shelled Tories
even Irvine deserves a kind word. The Iceberg
may have sometimes striven to place people
on the land. A Levi Ministry would put them
mostly under it.
Sydney Evening News was a bitterly
shocked paper when intelligence of the Labor
victories in Westralia and S.A. began to pour in,
and it appealed thus: —
On the issue of Socialism versus Anti-Socialism, Mr.
Reid should, before long, be able to gather a strong fol-
lowing round him ; and, in the interests of the country,
it is to be hoped that he will lose no time in setting
things in motion for the formation of an Anti-Socialist
Party, irrespective of tariff differences.
Exactly so. And if the Freetrade minority would
drop its tariff differences something of the sort
might happen. But if two parties join “ irrespec-
tive of tariff differences” the question always
rises—which one is to irrespect ?
J. J. Wilkinson, the man who was
running for the Senate in Westralia, but retired
because some of the papers called him a pro-Boer,
didn’t have much of a chance, anyway. The
strongest argument one of his press supporters
could find in his favor was that he is about the
best turf-handicapper in Westralia. But a
remarkable man in many ways is “Jim” Wil-
kinson. For years he was the best known coach-
driver in the Western districts of N.S.W. Most
coach-drivers are garrulous about their present
and past teams and little else; Wilkinson knew
Gordon from first to last, and recited him in a
huge voice by the hour. He left off coach-driving
10 or 15 years ago, but has since cultivated horse
and horse-poetry just as hard as ever.
It is not “the same Old George.’ It is
the old player telling of his former greatness:
There was some opposition, but Mr. Reid silenced it bv
a reference To his long and honorable negotiations with
the Labor party. —S.M. Herald (8/12/’O3), on Mr. Reid at
Redfern.
They had stood beside him for 24 years, and he did not
think they were going to support Victorians and desert
him .—George at Redfern.
For 23 years with only one break he had represented
East Sydney, but if they thought that he had ceased to
be of value —well, he was getting a bit old, etc. — Same
George at Woolloomooloo.
All the vanity of the decayed mummer who tells,
in pothouse corners, of the way he played
Hamlet 20 years ago. The man who is not “the
same Old George ” is beginning to note the yawns
and the weary looks, and craves their pity. “T
am growing old, gentlemen, -Don’t bump the
aged. ”
Recall now the brag of George Reid during
the last few months. The valiant George was
going to flatten out some prominent Minister —
Barton not being available, Lyne was going to be
his cold-meat. But when it came to the pinch
George sat tight in safe East Sydney, and sent
poor little M’Lean to be killed in the Hume.
“Every member of the Deakin Government shall
be opposed by strong Opposition candidates, ”
said Reid—and there wasn’t a strong Oppositionist
to face a single one of them 1 Deakin, Turner,
Forrest, Chapman, all of them unopposed; and
Lyne only opposed because the small Reidite who
was sent down there had no show in his old
electorate.
“ Vic.” : I find it necessary to modify my
opinion of policemen. One of him was called on
by the chairman of a political meeting the other
night, and instructed to cast me out (I had asked
the candidate what his principles were). Instead
of obeying with flunkey-like readiness, he protested
audibly, and only “ discharge^'his jooty”ina
reluctant manner when the chairman (a squatter,
J.P., and several other iniquitous substances)
pompously threatened to report him at head-
quarters. Also he apologised to me earnestly all
the way down the aisle. I accepted his apology
and signified the same in beer. Afterwards we
formed ourselves into a Fenian society, and pro-
pose commencing active propaganda work by
blowing-up Irvine’s deficit. Contributions of
dynamite will be gladly received.
“ M.” sends a protest :
J. Denniitoun Wood, the bygone politician who recently
■ecured a seat in Tas. Assembly, is curiously described
by an amateur or outsider when dubbed “ the liveliest
and most brainy in the small crowd of members" (B.
19/11/’O3). Wood, so far as my twelve years or so of
gallery experience goes, is the woodenest politician who
ever achieved any sort of reputation. He’s as lively as a
snail in a swamp. A certain fluency of speech he has, a
wearisome blare of words ; but his brightness consists in
the ordinary intelligence of a fairly shrewd lawyer
blurred by age and a long period of inactivity. I’ve just
seen Wood’s volume of “ poems an arid collection of
forced rhymes and unsuccessful attempts at rhymes—
lines that limp and jerk and stagger in their pitiful
anxiety to score—ineffectual verses right through, ai
musical as a kerosene tin in the hands of a suburban
small boy. This is his “first volume of poems." I hope
there may be no second.

Extract from historical essay by a school-
boy up in the bracing atmosphere of the New
England (N. S. W.) tableland:
Simon De Montfort, otherwise known as Simon the
Brighteous, was the second son ot a man of the same
man was a general, one of the Crusades or Holly words.
On the death of his father young Simon went over to
England to get the earldom of Leicester which he claimed
from his mother, King Edward 111. whose ink was
scarcely dry. However, he treated the people worse than
ever, but one night they was crossing an Inlet called the
wash the next after having a meal of peachers. After the
King made him governor over his estate. This annoyed
the barons worse than ever.

“T.D.”. Is the hollowness of the vice-
regal tuft-hunting business dawning at last upon
the Australian citizen ? Time was when daily
paper reporters had the most detested day’s work
of the year in going to a Governor’s levee, watch-
ing the grovel, and copying several thousand
names for publication, taking care not to duplicate
from the half-dozen visiting cards which most of
the loyal kow-towers handed in to make sure they
were not overlooked. Gov.-General Tennyson’s
levee on King’s Ned’s day was attended by less
than a hundred people outside officialdom, and
most of them failed to go to the imbecile length of
wearing evening dress at 10 a.m. and being con-
siderably guyed by the small boy in the street.
When George Hazeldine, who stands
charged with stealing a bottle of brandy from
the Murtoa (Victoria) railway station, comes up
•n tr u al in February next (if he does), his case
will have been meandering aimlessly around
the Crown Law offices for just 13 months. This
is scandalous, even fora Law Department which
had already put up a pretty steep record for
slowness. It seems that in January last the
accused came before the Court of Petty Sessions
charged with the theft of a bottle of brandy. He
pleaded guilty to the offence, recouped the owners
the value of the brandy, and asked to be retired
from the railway service. A police magistrate
and two J s.P. were on the Bench, and although
Hazeldine asked to be dealt with summarily, he
was sent to trial at the higher court, simply
because the Bench could not agree on the penalty.
Ihe case was several times mentioned in the
higher court, and eventually an application was
made for a mandamus to compel the lower court
to complete its adjudication. There was some
further delay, and after that the Crown Law office
seems to have fallen asleep, for its representative
at the Horsham General Sessions lately admitted
that he was not aware that accused, for lack of
funds, had abandoned the proceedings for a
mandamus. Even Judge Johnston expressed
UnU m. a l y^loud sur P ri se. It is a strange and
muddied Government which, can’t get through
with a charge of stealing one bottle of liquor in
less than a year and a quarter.

They take their football very seriously in Eng-
land. Papers of November 2 tell how, on the previous
day, a man of 62 died of excitement during a match in
Lancashire—a “qualifying” game for the English Gup,
of no importance whatever except to the players apu
their friends. “ The incident occurred towards the
close, when the local team was pressing Southport
severely, and seemed likely to equalise the scores.
Medical aid was summoned, and he regained partial con-
sciousness and murmured, ‘Did they.score? .He was
reassured, and then sank into insensibility again, from
which he never recovered."

Disease makes many a woman prematurely old.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
The great test match lasted six days, and
although the attendance fell off, general interest in the
game was unabated right through. It was a wonderful
struggle, and saw more records wrecked than any
previous contest of any sort that this paper knows of.
In batting Ranji’s 175, Sid. Gregory’s 201, and Murdoch’s
211 were all passed by Foster, and when he had passed
the Australians’first innings all on his little own there
was a mighty cheer. Apparently it disconcerted that mag-
nificent slogger, as he promptly lifted one in the off into
Noble’s hands. The visitors’ accumulation was the highest
ever made by an English team in a test match, and was
also a record score of runs for wickets taken. Previous
best was 1514 for 40 wickets, but the Englishmen this
time made 1541 for 35 wickets. Even the gate money
was a record, beating the previous best, £4006, taken at
Melbourne when Stoddart’s team was here, ’94-5, by £226.
* * * *
It was the fourth day that the Australians
played their great uphill game, and at stumps draw stood
75 to the good, with five wickets still to go. It was also
the day of the great Trumper innings. With the score at
three for 191 Duff shaped schoolboyishly at one from
Rhodes, and Relf held the simplest of catches. Rhodes
had sent down 10 overs for 9 runs and two wickets.
Trumper took the vacant crease, and was 20 minutes
scoring four runs. Then he back-cut Braund’s first and
second balls for four each, the third resulted in four byes,
and four more were gathered off the fourth ball. Then
came the Hill-Crockett episode. Noble joined his whilom
school-chum, and Trumper absolutely thrashed the
bowling, making four after four, till the century appeared
simultaneously with his side’s 332, after he had batted
somewhere about 1£ hours. Two runs later Noble stepped
out to one and was promptly stumped. Armstrong
joined Trumper, and the score was 367 before play closed.
Next morning 11 were scored from Rhodes’ two overs.
Playing forward one grazed the edge of Armstrong’s bat
and went to Bosanquet, who, though falling, held it
securely. Hopkins, Laver, Howell, and Saunders joined
him and were dismissed in turn, and after batting 3hrs.
60min. Trumper carried his bat for 185. He made 25
fours and played a most- brilliant game, placing the ball
where he liked and giving never one chance. Comparison
of his rate of scoring with that of Foster and Noble
reveals that he compiled 133 in exactly half the time
occupied by them over the same number.
* * * *
The only unpleasantness in the match was
really due to the loose, not to say absolutely bad fielding
of the Englishmen on the fourth day. Overthrows were
frequent, and it was one of them that caused the trouble.
Umpire Crockett declared Hill “run out” when he
appeared to be well in. Hill himself said he was “yards
to the good.” Anyhow, the crowd roared condemnation,
taking its cue from the members’ pavilion, and for
several seconds the hooting was infernal. It broke out
spasmodically all through the play, and at the close
Crockett came in for a further demonstration. This sort
of thing is to be regretted. The umpire may be wrong,
but he is more likely to be right than the distant occu-
pant of the members’ stand who is possibly a gentleman
who has plunged on the result.
* * * *
It is quite usual for the English visiting team to
win the first test and lose all the others ; this may be no
exception to the rule. Stoddart’s men, five years ago,
had nine wickets to the good, and M’Laren’s eleven, two
years ago, won by an innings and 124 runs. Twenty-nine
of the 67 tests played have gone to England, 26 to Aus-
tralia, and 12 have been drawn. Of 38 matches played in
Australia, 20 have gone to the home teams and 18 to the
Englishmen. Of 29 played in England, Bull has won 13,
Australia 6, and the drawn games numbered 10.
* * * *
One of the most humorous sights of the recent
test match was the way the Australian fieldsmen swarmed
round Rhodes, the last man to join Foster when that
mighty hitter was making the world’s record in a test
match. They were like crows round a lamb, and
almost grabbed the ball the moment it struck his bat.
But Rhodes was caution itself, and, in spite of the most
assiduous attention, he lasted long enough to make 40
and see 130 added to the score. The English tail is a
wiry appendage. * * *
Foster’s second innings was very different from
his record-breaking first. He was batting 18 minutesbefore
he got a run and had only 19 to his credit when he went
out to off-drive Armstrong, but the ball broke clear of the
bat and Kelly had the bails off in a twinkling. He was
in 59 minutes and uncertain all the time.
* * * *
“0.K.” : Out of respect for a popular cricketer,
the real cause of the Sydney crowd’s demonstration against
Crockett has been carefully ignoied by commentators.
Clem Hill gave the cue for groans and hisses when he
stood his ground and stared at the umpire who had ruled
him “ run out.” Had the batsman accepted the judg-
ment without any dumb demur, the crowd would have
merely mourned his bad luck, instead of surmising that
the decision was a mistake. They couldn’t see any
reason for hooting, except the reason suggested to them
by the batsman’s tactics. As for the absurd imputation
on Crockett’s character—well, it came from the most
excited portion of the onlookers, and no reasonable per-
son expects to find common sense in a cricket crank.
r * * * »
The cricket barracker has his flashes of humor.
When umpire Crockett gave Hill “run out” in the first test
match the crowd exhausted its vocabulary in finding suit-
able expressions to convey its contempt for the umpire,
his family before him, and his children after him. At
last a raucous voice, raised in withering scorn, was heard
above the din : “ Yer ort to stand for the Senate.”
« * * *
Trumble will play in second test match on
M.C.C. ground, New Year’s Day. Likely he’ll supplant
Laver. Also possible that Trott may be resurrected for
the occasion. M.C.C. ground has seen Australia success-
ful in last four matches played there. Australia has won
eight, and England six, of the 16 contested on M.C.C.
ground. Other two were drawn games.
The feature of the N.S.W.-S.A. return match
has been the consistent scoring of the N.S.W. men so far
as they have gone. O. Gregory stepped out of the train
and made 97. Noble also has been in scoring mood.
Mackay, the new inter-State man, made a fine start with
66—six less than Trumper’s. Claxton, though still lame
from an injury received in Sydney, has alone bowled
with much success. The big N.S.W. scoring seems to
put the match outside S.A.'s reach.
• * * *
N.S.W. team chosen to meet Queensland in
Sydney this week is a surprise. Five of the 12 were not
in the 11 or next 13 on University Oval last month. The
object is, apparently, to encourage younger players, but,
in the face of Queenslanders’ good showing against
Britishers, the policy is dangerous.
* * * #
S.A.’s “ Eton and Harrow ” match ended last
weekin the easiest of wins for Prince Alfred College players,
whose moderate score of 205 brought them home with an
innings to spare. St. Peters, however, still have the
longest list of wins to credit. There was no Hill playing
for' P.A.0., the generous supply of Clem’s brothers
having at last run out. The youngest of them was, on
the same day, engaged in winning an electorate match
with an excellent 75 not out, and another is strongly
backed for trial in inter-State cricket for his bowling.
• » * »
A recent Vic. Grammar School match put up
what looks like a record. Geelong College’s first two
batsmen made 353 on the first day—no wickets down.
Brighton Grammar School’s total was 2. The first bats-
man and the last made one each ; nobody else scored.
One bowler took 7 for nil; another did the hat trick.

M.L. Bookmakers’ Association, a show with a
fighting reserve of £6OO, has now adopted a fawning,
conciliatory attitude towards cluhs, and pleads in a pretty
little circular to be licensed to bet on courses. Ikey says
in his characteristic way that he would sooner be con-
trolled than have to bet surreptitiously, as at present.
Legitimate bookmaking isn’t unlawful in M.L.; the sur-
reptitious betting spoken of is the unlawful tote-odds
laying. And if the gentry with the aroma of the
billiard-room clinging to their clothes think clubs are
going to allow them to openly compete with the tote on
their own green swards, they make a fine healthy error.
* * * *
“ 0.K.”: It is not enough that Isaac Mosea has
been prohibited from crying the odds at wheel races. His
very existence calls for suppression. He ought to be writ
down on the statutes as a capital offence. The full infamy
of Ikey’s odds on the Austral Final was exposed in Melb.
Herald t’other Saturday, without a word of reprobation
from that family journal. “ Even money Scharp, 4 to 1
each against Meyer, Scheps, and Kett, 6 to 1 against
Walker, and 10 to 1 against others.” There were 14
runners. For the information of the average Juggins,
who knows nothing whatever about bookmaking as an
exact science, here is an imaginary “ volume.” The sup-
posititious Ike opens his speculation by laying “ a level
’underd agin the favorite,” and then proceeds to bet up
to that first risk :
Risks Receives Pays out on a win

Ikey receives £3OO. Against this his biggest liability is
£2OO, his lowest £llO. If the favorite wins, the man
with the bag comes out of it with £lOO to the good. An
outsider’s victory shows a profit of £l9O. And the
original outlay ( i.e ., risk) was only £lOO. Of course, the
barrackers for Ikey will argue that no member of the
beaky brotherhood could “get round" on such easy
terms as these, several of the runners being unmarket-
able, because they were supposed to be “ stiff." But one
is quite justified in assuming the published odds to have
been the longest odds obtainable at the start. And as
for the “ stiff ’’ starters, there is no denying the fact that
each of them was a possible winner. A bad smash-up
during the race might have shattered all the cronk
arrangements, and converted the surviving competitors
into triers, and the victory of an unbacked outsider
would have meant “ a skinner ” for Moses.

* * ' * *
Alec Taylor, who belonged to the “Ballarat
division” of horse-trainers, didn’t long survive old
Tommy Wilson. Alec died last week, aged 72. A good,
burly, bottle-nosed little humorist, full of wise saws and
ancient instances, and a thorough master of his business
all the time. He believed in giving a nag a hard prepara-
tion for a hard run. “ If you don’t put it into ’em you
can’t get it out of ’em,” he would say ; and although he
just missed his great coup with Wait-a-Bit when Grafter
beat him by a neck for the Melbourne Cup, he managed
to get some big results from a comparatively small
number of flat racers. With Vengeance he won Caulfield
Cup in 1890, and Australian Cup the following
year. He took the long-distanoe handicap again
in 1896 with Idolator—a supposed non-stayer and
a 30 to 1 chance. In the jumping races “Tay-
lor’s selected” was always worth considering. Kestrel,
Pirate, Busaco, Dondi and others down to the time of
My Mistake ran prominently for the veteran trainer.
Sometimes his luck was cruel, notably in the case of
Colonel Shilinski. The Colonel aforesaid was Taylor’s rod
in pickle for the V.R.O. Grand National Hurdle in 1902.
Somehow the good thing got beaten by a head by the
moderate Rawdon, whereupon the old man sold the horse
to Albert Miller, and had the mortification of seeing it
win four. big stakes off the reel for its new owner.
Hardly another trainer of Taylor’s old-fashioned type
remains hereabouts. Some of his quaint sayings will
survive the memory of him. They have passed into
proverbs.
* * • *
The slump of Queensland racing. Q. Turf
Club’s three-days’ meet produced a profit of only £52,
although it was the one to which the club looked for big
returns. Prize-money totalled £2525, of which J. M'Gill,
the owner of Fitzgrafton, secured £660, and Mrs. J.
Tucker £385. Kaesen did best among jockeys with four
wins, and A. Scott and W. Stewart followed with three
each. Tote investments were £lBO4 less than last year
for first two days.
* « * *
In a Maoriland holy conference recently a
simple-minded parson made a sensation, by stating that
he had gone personally into a haunt of sin called
“Tattersall’s,” and asked a “gentleman” there if the
' totalisator was good or bad, and the “ gentleman ” had
stated frankly that it was bad, and led women to gamble.
For the moment it looked as if the tote was squelched.
Then it was whispered round that, in the town the rev.
gentleman came from, “.Tattersall’s” was a barber’s
shop,; and the man who called himself Tattersall made a
little book on the races when he wasn’t cutting hair, and
he was naturally aggravated because the tote spoiled his
business. The simple-minded reverend, when he went
and interviewed the poor little hair-cutter, really thought
he was Invading a great gambling centre and gathering
the unbiassed opinions of a leviathan of the turf.

“H.P.,” from Wellington (M.L.): The 35 “two-
uppers ’’ lassoed in an alleged athletic club, the Olym-
pic, referred to in Bulletin, 13/10/’O3, were each fined £2
and some shillings for being found in a common gaming
house. The alleged keeper was committed for trial, ana
on Nov. 30th the jury, after a retirement of four hours,
failed to agree. A question that has not struck the
public so far is, whether the fines of the 35 will be
refunded if the keeper is found not guilty. If the jury
thinks that he was not the keeper of a common gambling
house, then those found upon the premises were not
commonly gambling. The prosecution, anyhow, is
inconsistent, for the whole of Haining-street not a hun-
dred yards from the Olympio Club, is given over to the
slant-eyed, whose pak-a-poo and fan-tan shops are con-
ducted with glaring openness night and day.
* * * *
Billiard Referee Mannock openly state# “no
ma.n with any sense of self-respect would ever act as
referee twice in matches played between Dawson and
Stevenson." “ The former ’’ he says, “ cannot control his
temper, and the latter is the greatest all-round grumbler
the world holds.” Trouble cropped up over last match
played between Dawson and Stevenson before the latter
left England for Australia. Mannock, as referee, ruled a
certain ball, very near the baulk line, could be played,
and that decision should have been final. Stevenson
required the distance to be measured. Mannock yielded,
and result showed he was correct, but Stevenson kept on
talking even when he got to Australia.
* * * *
London Stock Exchange seems to take its
walking matches seriously. In a recent track com-
petition, Broad, the winner of the Brighton walk (which
first started the craze), was wiped out without mercy
when his pace became unfair. Has anyone ever been dis-
qualified in any of the recent flutters in Australia ? They
certainly formed an exhibition of all styles, up to the
comfortable trot. Again, after Broad had gone, one
Duke was ordered off the track by a judge, and was
ordered on again by the other two judges, who agreed
that he was fair 1 The unfortunate Duke missed
winning by rather less than the 150 yards which he lost
while being ordered off and on again. There is not one
man in a thousand who is competent to judge a walk
(and most of the other 999 will dissent from Ms decisions).
All sensible sports-promoters should steer clear of walk-
ing events.

An agricultural show coincidence. On being

* • • *
The impending arrival of Major Taylor, with a
view to displaying his magnificent calves on Sydney
Cricket Ground cycling track, has started another yell
for the cutting down of local prize-money so that more
Yankee fliers may be imported to give excellent displays.
The Yankee fliers, it is explained, will not come unless
they receive big appearance-money. Then, in the name
of the Holy Kangaroo, let the accursed Yankee flier stay
away. Man cannot live by bread alone, neither will he
bike-race for honor and glory only. Even under existing
arrangements there is little enough in the game for local
riders, and this proposal that the Australian cycling clubs
and leagues should give up fostering local sport and
healthy exercise to become collecting machines to provide
substantial appearance-money for Yankee wheelmen is as
idiotic as it is unpatriotic. What is wanted is a substan-
tial increase in the local prize-money and also an increase
in the number of substantial prizes to be won. If this
were done it would serve both ends —it would encourage
the local wheelist and the Yankee dollar-hunter would
come along just the same. Extra special dollar-hunters
like Taylor might have to be paid to appear, but in view
of the increased possibilities in the shape of prize-money
they would, no doubt, be glad to accept a much smaller
fee.
• • * *
Yankee cyclist Lawson is in Westralia with his
eyes glued upon the Westral Wheel Race, to be run at
Kalgoorlie this week, and which is worth £330. Morgan
and McDonald, two of Australia’s best, are both there,
and Lawson should find it necessary to show the best that
is in him to carry off the gilded plum.
* * * *
By scooping the Austral Wheel Race, Scheps
heads the prize-winning list for the season with £4/5.
Ivor Lawson won £157 10s. . McFarland, £Bl, and
Australia’s fastest flier (Don Walker) only £6O. Altogether
there doesn’t appear to be much in being an Australian
cycling champion. Scheps is only a boy yet, just turned
19 : probably ha will go on winning bike races for a year
or two and then it will dawn on him, if he is a person of
ordinary intelligence, that there is nothing in the game
nothing to justify the premature wearing out of his boiler,
anyhow. , . . .
N S.W. National Coursing Association is much
exercised on the subject of giving stimularits to grey-
hounds to cause them to run one brilliant course for
betting purposes. When the drug has worn off backers
not in the know fall in. Doping is more prevalent in
coursing than in any other branch of sport, and a sub-corn-
mittee has recommended that nothing but beef tea, to be
procured from the secretary of the club in a sealed bottle,
be allowed. Unfortunately the doping is generally done
before the dog goes to the slips, and the only way to
block it is to have the hounds in the slip-steward s charge
for at least half-an-hour before the course is run.
* * * *
N S.W. bowling season proper opened Satur-
day, and 16 clubs engaged in first round of Pennant com-
petition. Play generally was close. Three matches
resulted in victory by one point. Worst defeat was that
of Gladstone Park by Waverley, 128 to 68 Petersham
went under to St. George. Surprise of the round was
Manly’s success over Ashfield.

Sculler Jim Stanbury, 2jst. above his weight
has commenced preparing for possibilities, and will stick
to work till Towns-Tressider match is decided, when he
proposes having a cut at the winner for big money.
Most promising distance swimmer among Aus-
tralian comers is Harold Baker, just turned 15, w ho
figured at Bondi against Dick Cavill. His form was
attradtive, his pluck' and stamina beyond the Usual,
and he kept his head right through. Well looked after,
and not asked to do too much, Baker should, in a year or
two, take the place of the top-notchers of the present
time. „ .

N.S.W. fighting axeman, Bill Heckenberg, is
probably out of the pug. business for ever. _ While fight-
ing Wallsend miner Squires, at Newcastle, his ankle gave
way and he had to capitulate. Referee Ford called all
bets off—which was absurd. It was a wild bumping
scrap, but only lasted three rounds.
After all, Australian missionary work among"
the aborigines has not been in vain. Dargin, who was-
“raised” on a mission station, met “Spider” Kelly at
Sydney Golden Gate last Saturday night, and although
he jarred his right and could only use his left for a con-
siderable portion of the fight, he used it to such purpose-
that half-way through the twelfth round Kelly retired,
more than satisfied. Dargin, who, so far, neither drinks
nor smokes, will now probably break up a few of the
Sydney middle-weights, but is not likely to become a
champion.
* * * *
Australian bantam pug supremacy between
Toohev and Green, at Sydney National Sporting Club last
week, was not over clever. Green, whose shoulder was-
put out in previous meeting, generally did better work,
and scored the greater number of points, but-the referee s
decision was a draw.
* * * *
Negro Ti iddleweight “Starlight,” 11.4 cham-
pion of Victoria, met Ike Stewart at Sydney Gaiety
Saturday last. Starlight pounded like a steam hammer,
but Stewart, though staggering and all but outed again-
and again, kept a bold front and kept going. In the
seventh Ike seemed thoroughly done. A right on the
jaw and a jolt near the left eye, which left him gashed,
seemed to have ended it; but he struggled through the
round, and was comparatively fresh when the gong
sounded again. During and from the 12th Stewart-
evened things up considerably, but “ Starlight ” led all the
time. In the 20th round referee declared it a draw.
Starlight’s vitality is remarkable. Now over 50, he is
infinitely more lively and a better fighter than half the
continent’s not-half-as-old bruisers. Twenty-five years-
ago he was fighting at Foley’s old White Horse
Gymnasium in George-st., Sydney, and could deal
out and avoid stoush in a remarkable manner. The old
man is to-day, in all human probability, the most-
ancient clever pug in the world.
THE OLD ENTHUSIAST.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I hate beyond all other bores,
The Ancient, stuffed with Record Scores,
Who squats beside me at the match
And speaks, perchance, ot Noble’s catch,
Or Foster’s cut, and tempts me thus
In foolish manner to discuss
The length and breadth of Trumper’s fame,
And cries (and makes me say the same)
That “cricket is a funny game.”
I hate the man] yet sit and smile
And babble with him all the while.
He’s fat and short, or tall and lean ;
His name, no doubt, is Brown, or Green—
That crank who notes with joy and glee
The patient play which wearies me.
In vain I pray for strength to fly ;
I cannot move, I dare not sigh—
I’m cricket-bound when he is by.
No other dullard hath the power
To hold me captive hour by hour, r
And draw from my unwilling lips
Inane remarks on drives and slips.
The secret of the Ancient’s spell
I leave to someone else to tell;
Enough for me to blush with shame
And plead the aphorism lame
That “ cricket is a funny game."
F.

manner. If you know you have a liver and suffer
from the many ailments arising out of its defective
action, you cannot do better than take a course
of the world-renowned liver medicine, Bile Beans

on the liver. They put this organ in thorough

aches, debility, and the multitude of ailments a
disordered liver sets up, will be promptly and per-
manently cured. It is only by acting directly on
the liver that these ailments can be permanently
cured. Local treatments only relieve while being

Bent and Taverner are making prepara-
tions to get from under the mess when the Irvine
Government comes down with a crash. To mem-
bers in the corridors these two loyal Ministers
openly criticise the policy and methods of the
Government When the Opposition walked out
of the chamber in a body the other night Bent was
to be seen waddling about the corridors, telling
m rnbjrs that Irvine had made a fool of himself,
and that if he ran the show it would have been
“all right.” Taverner is said to be intriguing
with David Syme to overthrow the present
Government, and create a combination of the
“moderates” to fight the Labor party and the
extreme Tories.
A North of Ireland Association has been
established in Melbourne “to bring together
respectable North of Irelanders.” Things like
that are always happening to Australia. This
tendency to bring somebody together for the pur-
pose of muttering over old world traditions is the
curse of our country. You can’t bring North of
Irelanders together without further separating
them from South of Irelanders, and then, when
the two crowds, have been sufficiently separated
they either break Australian property in their
excitement or they sacrifice Australian political
interests for the sake of their dirty old squabbles.
A Dill Macky, for instance, doesn’t necessarily
believe in Freetrade, but the sacrifice of the Aus-
tralian workman to the foreigner may help to get
into Parliament a man who will howl about the
North of Ireland, and so the Australian must be
sacrificed. This country shelters Dill, and he
sells it as cold-bloodedly as any other traitor ever
sold the land that took him in.
The attempt made at late electoral meet-
ings to introduce the professional claqueur in
Sydney does not encourge the pest to increase
and multiply. An agent submitted to leading
speakers a scheme, whereby he guaranteed to
“generate enthusiasm” in a meeting, the ardor
of the audience being determined by the amount
of the fee. Similarly he proposed to ‘ spoil a
meeting. The hiring of the necessary voices
meant money, and on that account, as well as tor
other reasons, the agent was cold-shouldered.
Candidate’s expenses are limited, and he can t
well put down “ hired applause” in his accounts.
No less than 92 Bills showed their noses
inside N.S.W. Parliament during the past session,
and of 20 that got through only eight were brought
in by Ministers in the Assembly. Sixty -1 wo were
hung up somewhere. Minister for Mines jviaci,
harmless and inoffensive to the last, had charge ot
seven Government measures, and he was not able
to Bteer a single one of them through the flood of
words regarding Bill’s dirt-money, and the
alleged improper engagement of an improver in
the dockyard. The partly-extinguished Owe-
Sullivan was down for only two, and both of them
were drowned. Even Crick lost three out of four.
Colonel Templeton, one of the Argos
candidates for the Senate:
The workmen of to-day are not equal to those of 20
years ago. And why 1 Because they think less of their
work and more of their fees.
Templeton himself thought so much of his fees
that he demanded some £20,000 more than thecourt
would allow when he was liquidator of the Premier
Permanent Building Society. And, looking at
the miserable dividend, some of the creditors
wonder if the liquidators, especially the liquidating
colonels, of to-day are equal to those of 20 years
ago.
Here is a curious combination of circum-
stances : The Chinese unionist, who is ten times
as violent a unionist as the white unionist, has at
last spanked his boss, the aggregate Chinese
cabinetmaker of Melbourne, into submission.
Under threat of being murdered and having his
house burnt, the Chinese employer has recognised
the Chinese union, put the wages back on the
old scale, announced his submission in the papers,
withdrawn all charges of assault and battery
against his employee, and has propitiated Joss.
Now, the most ferocious white unionist never
assaulted Derham and insisted that he should
give up the Christian faith and turn idolator in
addition to raising wages. Yet Derham and his
Tory friends perpetually hold up the murderous
Chinese unionist as a model, and want to import
him in large numbers.

“ C.P.” : In view of the wave of indig-
nation which has swept over Australia as a result
of the treatment of the poor Malay shipwrecked
sailors, I have a suggestion to prevent a repetition
of the disgraceful occurrence. I know it ivas a
disgraceful occurrence, because I saw it in the
Freetrade organs. My proposal is that in future,
when niggers intend being shipwrecked on the
Victorian coast, they should be compelled to give at
least a fortnight’s notice, so that arrangements for
their entertainment might be properly made. A
guard of honor, headed by Major-General Hutton
and the State commandants, could then meet
them at Port Melbourne and conduct them to an
official welcome by the Governor-General, The
roads might be suitably festooned with flags, a
few arches el ected, and the city illuminated in the
evening. Afterwards one of the Malays might be
billeted on each foreign - trade editor and each
cheap-labor parson. All those willing to subscribe
amounts of £lOO and over for this purpose will
kindly forward same to me immediately, and the
money will be paid into the Yana Bank.

“R.A.K.” : Saw a crowd gathered around
a respectably-dressed girl on the Hospital corner
of Russell-street, Melbourne. She was as white as
a sheet and was tottering around in a curious
manner, but it wasn’t drink Asked a bystander
what was the matter. He said casually he sup-
posed she’d just had a chloroform operation at the
hospital and hadn’t got over the effects of the
drug yet. The Kyabram Government economises
so much now that the hospital has to throw its
patients out promptly to save wear and tear of the
premises.
The committee of the Melbourne Benevo-
lent Asylum has finally decided not to commit
the “ illegal ” action of refunding a trifle of the
late Hingston’s money for the purpose of main-
taining a tombstone over his grave in England.
James Hingston bequeathed £27,000 to the “ Bene-
volent,” but forgot to provide a proper endow-
ment for his own damp asylum, where he sleeps
with his fathers. A sentimental solicitor wrote
several letters from England, suggesting that the
Benevolent, as principal legatee, might as well
strain its bye-laws by voting a £lOO donation to
its benefactor’s memory. The far-off attorney
reckoned without regard for the notorious
scrupulousness of Melbourne’s public institutions
in all money matters. The Benevolent consulted
its own solicitor, and was advised to preserve its
conscience pure and undefiled. So Hingston’s
gravestone can rip. The question is settled.

The conservative craze for reducing wages
in Australia began as early as June, 1804, when
Governor Collins, of Tasmania, appointed a board
to sit, one Sunday, after church service, to lower
the rates for day labor. The new prices were
promulgated by General Order.

No Australian takes his politics more
seriously than the bush digger. Up in far-north
Flinders, for instance, the men on Leichhardt
wanted to vote for M‘Donald, but the Philp crowd
wouldn’t declare Leichhardt a polling-place. The
nearest polling-place was over 90 miles away, but
that didn’t atop them —they got horses, rode in,
voted, and not a claim was touched till the job
was finished and M‘Donald was elected.
• It is just as well N.S.W. Parliament shut
its disorderly house for a while :
Mr. Perry : It is not so !
Mr. J. O." L. Fitzpatrick : It is so !
Mr. Perry : It is a lie !
Mr. J. 0. L. Fitzpatrick : It is not a lie !
Mr. Perry : It is a lie !
Mr. J. 0. L. Fitzpatrick : I rise to order.
The Chairman : The hon. member ought not to have
interrupted.
Mr. J. C. L. Fitzpatrick : I object to the Minister saying
“ It is a lie” !
Members should study the evils of ruffianly
language during the recess.
He the difficulty of regulating the bonus on
white-grown sugar. A certain Kanaka in a S.Q.
sugar district is married to a white woman.
Tanna, his wife, and a white employee do all the
work on a small sugar farm. Hence the Poly-
nesian argues that he is entitled to the bonus as
employing only white labor. Bonus, however,
was refused, as the authorities held that Tanna
employed himself also, and consequently had no
right to the bonus. In another case, a white man
had married a white girl who was burdened with
a boy of some 1,3 or 14 years—an illegitimate,
semi-Polynesian son. White cocky aforesaid
employed his wife’s dusky offspring in the cane,
and applied for bonus. The bonus Was, naturally,
refused. Hereupon the sugar farmer hunted the
boy out of the place, and refused to allow him
near his mother. And these things occurred in a
country where respectable journals used to assert
there was no danger of racial contamination.
“ H.” : One more item in the Victor! m
Government’s record of treachery to railway em-
ployees. Some time ago M‘Donald, M.L.A.,
drew attention in the House to the fact that the
railway employees worked 12 hours on Sundav
and did not get paid for it. Of course, Shiels
denied it, but immediately issued an order that
Sunday time was no longer to.be worked without
pay. Now, the men in this overmanned (?) service
have been working overtime and getting time off
without pay for it. In other words, a man does
10 hours extra work without pay, and in com-
pensation is allowed 10 hours off without pay. So
a new order directs that if a man has worked five
days overtime and five, days Sunday time, and has
wor red off the overtime, it is to be reckoned that
he has worked off the Sunday time. He is still
not paid for the Sunday time. The only differ-
ence is that he doesn’t, because he worked a
Sunday without pay, get knocked off duty with-
out pay on a week day., To put it another way,
instead of working, six days and being paid for
five, he works seven days and is paid for six.
“ Harrie N.” writes from Brisbane :
I attended a number of the women’s political meetings
in Brisbane, and enjoyed myself greatly, especially
where the scented Tory dames were gathered together.
They have a very faint knowledge of politics, but they
speak in nicely-chosen language, and never mention by
name any of the ladies who hold opposite views.
“ Socialism” and “socialistic” are words which are
introduced at every meeting. The capitalistic ladies at
first had an idea that a “socialist” was a dangerous
person who held similar theories to Guy Fawkes, and
carried infernal machines about on his person ; but they
were so twitted on their ignorance that they began to try
to find out something about the cult, and this is how one
of their leading orators explained it the other
day. “If I work hard on one of my nicely-kept and
productive farms, and my nearest neighbor does absolutely
nothing but loaf in a hovel, according to socialistic
theories I am supposed to let him have a share of my
profits.” When the same lady further said that no intel-
ligent newspaper ever advocated even a small measure of
socialism, I ventured to lift up a small, weak voice and
remark that The Bulletin was slightly socialistic in its
principles, but the large, fat finger of scorn was pointed
at me, and a voice, shaking with emotion, informed the
meeting that everyone who read your “vile” paper was
immoral; that you had no respect for women, which was
not to be wondered at, as you never had a mother, but
were born of the Devil, and placed on this earth for the
express purpose of leading “myriads” of souls to
destruction. Now, I have had the pleasure of gazing
upon some of you, and my impression was that you were
a mild-mannered, hen-pecked sort of a man. Is it really
true. that your chief component is brimstone, and that a
tail is coiled up beneath the editorial chair?
A great truth, by “ L.W.” :
The Fall of the United States of America may be
expected any day now, that free and glorious land of
unrestricted private enterprise having compelled the
Oceanic S.S. Co. to return to Auckland from San Fran-
cisco a young man named Kenneth Harper who went to
the Golden Gate to join a bank staff. The benighted
U.S.A. regarded him as a contract laborer. The Six
Hatters were only compelled to remain on board till cer-
tain formalities were gone through. Perhaps by this
time the Deakin Cabinet recognises that it might as well
have enforced the law ; the Freetrade-and-Unrestricted-
Competition-for-the-other-Fellow crowd could have made
no more row if the Six Blathers had been sent back to
England, and the other side would have had something to
be thankful for. So far there has been no exclusion ; all
the disputed immigrants have been admitted after slight
delay. There’s a sad lack of thoroughness about Aus-
tralian politicians just now, The Freetraders admit they
don’t intend to be thorough, and asked for power
because they were thoroughly determined not to be
thorough .The Deakin crowd has never once been
thorough, enough it has never boasted of its lack of that
quality like the Dry Dog crowd. Australian political
history is choked with the wrecks of the men who
perished because they hadn’t the grit or the energy to
act up to their own alleged beliefs.
A recent Bulletin par.
stated that the refusal of M.L. House of Reps,
(by 43 votes to 19) to give absolute preference to
unionists was a good deal misunderstood. The
par. went on to correctly point out that in the
vast majority of cases already decided by the
M.L. Arbitration Court preference to unionists
had been stipulated for. Let me say, however,
that the present brand of preference gives poor
results, there being many non-unionists employed
in all the trades, receiving the full wage fought
for and gained by the unionists, but contributing
nothing to the union funds. Employers do dis-
criminate against union men on occasion, and
they will continue to do so until absolute prefer-
ence is ordered by the court; and the unions are
well advised to fight for it as they are now doing.
Only a week ago a Wellington bookbinder told
the Arbitration Court that he considered the
previous granting of preference (ordinary brand)
put no obligation on him to inquire, when
engaging a new man, if he was a member of the
union.
“ Maori ” : I don’t think either Soddon or
the Beer party was glad that the Licensing Bill
was killed. They had figured it out that a couple
of years of absolute prohibition in the districts
that carried “no license” at the last election
would effectually settle the question for ever; and
perhaps it would. That the Bill did not get
through was no fault of Seddon’s; but members
were simply swamped with telegrams from con-
stituents daring them to vote for it, and they
didn’t dare. Instead of the defeat of the Bill
being a triumph for Richard, it was a defeat, and
an evidence of the power of the prohibitionists (?)
in this country.
The hanging-up of Waddell’s Liquor Bill
saved a Cabinet disruption in N.S.’W. Fegan
and Kidd on one side and Crick and O’Sullivan
on tlie other were dangerous rocks to steer
between. Waddell’s promised but-of-Parliament
discourse won’t amount to anything. It will be
treated by the Cabinet as np.n-official, ns was
his Cowra financial speech. Liquor legisla-
tion which attempts to satisfy vested inte-
rests, and the public, and the zealots of two
factions, will only rouse all to enmity. The Bill,
to do good, must begin with a principle. Finding
interests so clashing and opinions so various, the
State must assert its right to take the traffic over
and accept responsibility.
The “Empire Navy League” is abso-
lutely the deadest “ movement ’’ that ever refused
to move in Australia. Even the promoters have
about given it up as a bad job, and the biggest
part of the space in the dozen lines which the
daily papers now give to its meetings is devoted
to weeps about lack of support.


The Bulletin learns with regret that some
verses recently printed in its columns over a
well known Australian signature had previously
appeared in another publication over quite a
different signature. The natural assumption is
tUat The Bulletin was taken in by an unscru-
pulous literary thief, and that, in getting payment
for the verses, the person who offered them obtained
The Bulletin’s money under false pretences.
The necessity for criminally prosecuting people
who do this kind of thing, and landing them m a
suitable gaol, will some day force itself on Aus-
tralian editors. Only the tiredness inseparable
from the climate has prevented The Bulletin
from doing so already, and in this case the
management is seriously thinking of some such
action.
That something would happen at the
Elections was clear from the fact that the electors
oiled their thinking machinery with D.C.L.
Whisky to a remarkable extent. Tne week s out-
put of D.O.L. rose to the importance of the
occasion. What happens to a country which
strengthens its ideas with that best of whiskies is
shown by Maoriland, where the consumption of
1).C.L., progressive legislation and increased
prosperity march together. Long may they
march.*
A new method has taken possession of Sydney
society. Formerly it was the mode to entertain at one’s
own home. Now the thing is to do it at The Hotel
Australia.*

Westralia Waihi (W.A.) is one of those mines
regarding whose management the less said the better.
When the co. was floated nearly three years ago it was
stated that there was an Bft. payable lode m No. I.shaft.
An inspection failed to disclose such a lode. In the
recently published halLyearly report it is stated that, in
crosscutting at the 110 ft. level from No. 1 shaft, four
quartz reefs had been encountered asfollows .No 1, Btt.
wide, worth loz. ; No. 2,3 ft., worth 30z.; No. 3,3 ft.,
worth lOdwt.; and No. 4,7 ft., worth loz Since that
was written the manager reports that the last-mentionea
reef, which he calls No. 3, is of very Kreat width and
worth 15dwt. Property consists of a diorite hill traversed
by numerous quartz leaders, some of which are very rich.
These leaders trend more or less north and south, and
underlie most erratically, some east, some west. When the
co. was floated it was stated that it would practically
be a paving proposition from the jump; but it is not
visibly nearer paying now than it was three years ago.
No stone has been developed, and there is no water-
supply on the property or in the district.
Queensland Menzies (W.A.) will start crushing
after the holidays with its new heavy stamps, which are
estimated to treat half as much again as the old mill.
The crosscut from 534 ft. level has still about 80ft. to go to
cut the eastern lode, and 150 ft. to cut the western. To
expedite matters, a crosscut from the bottom of tne
winze on the latter lode is being driven. When this is
complete there will he another 140 ft. of ‘ backs of rich
ore to work. Manager Ferguson is satisfied with the
appearance of the mine, but is not permitted to push on
with developments. Apparently the ore in sight was
over-estimated when the new stamps were ordered.
During the coming year the co. should have a successful
run as No. 7 level is ready for stoping, and ore from there
will be available when the crosscut is completed, which
should be early in February at recent rate of progress.
* * * *
Lancefield, Laverton (W.A.), which was under
option to Bewick, Moreing and Co. about a year ago,
and rejected, has now been acquired by that firm. Ihe
price paid is said to have been £50,000 cash, which can t
be considered extravagant. A local syndicate, originally
in 360 shares, afterwards increased to 21,600, has workea
the property very successfully for some years, and paid
monthly divs. of £360. All the work done has been
above 200 ft., at which depth the lode is 26ft. wide. One
level is 900 ft. long, and right through has produced
battery stone worth Bdwt. for a width of 18ft.
Horton and party, the original prospectors, have done
well, as, in addition to paying divs., all the equipment,
including mill and cyanide plant, has been paid for out
of gold won. The building of the railway to Laverton
vastly improves the prospects of the mine ; but, unless it
is worked on a very large scale, the ore is too low-grade
to earn any extravagant profits. £360 a month wouldn t
cover J. Bull’s cables and extras.
• • • •
St. George, Lake Darlot (W.A.), has cleaned up
40 tons for 1120 oz. Since the prospectors started, some
18 months ago, they have taken out 144 tons for 52440 z.,
worth about £20,000. The leader from which this gold
was won may possibly be the source of the enormously
rich alluvial which, in the early days, made Darlot
famous. , , * •
Harriston, Mt. Malcolm (W.A.), a show
recently pegged out on behalf of a local syndicate, is
proving good. The first clean-up of 147 tons yielded
1088 oz. worth £4OOO. On the strength of this a div. of
£5O a share has been declared to the 60 shareholders. A
good deal may be heard of this concern soon, as the
initial crushing was broken out in a very short time.
* * * *
Bellevue Consols, Mt. Sir Samuel (W.A.), is
tipped as one of the best things outside of Kalgoorlie
The co. is in 400,000 shares, and, on its reconstruction, 18
months ago, had a working capital of £70,000. A new
main shaft is being sunk, and is down 250 ft. A 20-head
mill has been treating about 2000 tons monthly for about
1600 oz. for the past three years, and a huge accumulation
of tailings, estimated at sdwt. per ton, awaits treat-
ment. The mine is now being equipped with a 40-head
mill, which will start early in the new year, and the co.
will probably become a div. payer. The working costs at
present tot up 625. a ton, but of this 245. 9d. goes in
development and 11s. lOd. in plant, owing to sinking the
new shaft and erecting the new mill. The co. is now
controlled by Bewick, Moreing and 00., who have recently
acquired the knack of making things hum.
9 * • *
Associated, Kalgoorlie (W.A.), is being vigor-
ously handled, and large divs. should be forthcoming in
the near future. The main shaft is being carried down
to 1600 ft. at full speed, and developments at IOOOit.
are also being pushed ahead. The north and east
sections of the property are practically virgin, and,
iudging from adjoining properties, these are promising
spots. On the east, the North Boulder worked out all
the ore in its ground till the lode dipped into the Asso-
dated, and won nearly 40,0000 z. of gold from 30,000 tons
of ore This was from the Brown Hill-Oroya lode, and it
looks as if a second rich chute started in the North Boul-
der ground, and its continuation should he found in the
Associated. In the north section it is now practically
certain that the bonanza chute from the Oroya will be
found, and will vastly improve the prospects of the co.
On working out a lens of ore, no attempt used to be
made to trace the indicator to a new lens, and it is now
anticipated that fresh makes of pay ore will be found on
the upper levels. To facilitate this work a new 20-head
mill will soon be jigging on the low-grade ore,
and in breaking this down, it is expected that
fresh rich lenses will be discovered. There is a large
accumulation of tailings to be re-treated, as the extrac-
tion, instead of being 90 per cent., as alleged was only
about 70 per cent., and up to 6 or 7dwt. was left in the
residues, which will all be re-treated.
* * * *
Chaffers, Kalgoorlie (W.A.), which has long
been under a cloud, has recently been showing some
revival on the strength of an alleged development. A lot
of people, who were loaded up with scrip at high prices,
are no doubt, anxious to see the stock boom. The South
Fin'gall, which was promoted by Chaffers, has evidently
been abandoned as hopeless, as the leases were recently
forfeited for the non-fulfilment of labor conditions.

“The Nondescript”: At Ngawha (Ohaeawai,
North Maoriland) there are plenty of traces of quicksilver,
both cinnabar and native. The M.L. mining boom
of ’96-7 induced a local Bung (good old public bene-
factor Bung) to subsidise a couple of miners, at the time
ffum digging, to prospect the Hot Springs, and he made
generous promises in the event of a find. As a result a
combine took over the show and started operations with
two furnaces (four retorts) with a capacity of 30 tons a
week. The much-lettered imported manager took a trip
to New Mexico to study processes, and on his return
easily out-distanced the only other man on the claim who
had seen a retort before—an ex-stoker from the Auckland
gas works. It was a fearsome spectacle to see the hands
*• clean-up.” Something seemed to go wrong with the
distillation, for the quicksilver (which was undoubtedly m
the dirt) failed to materialise; perhaps it was used to
form a lining for the dark clouds which were gathering
fast in the financial atmosphere of Bung. This person
tried to get out of his agreement, for, after lengthy
litigation, he filed his schedule. The cast-iron
retorts were worn out in a month or two. The
lode or deposit, either ran out or was non-existent, and
after expending some £5OOO the only tangible results are
half-a-dozen chronic invalids, one cemetery allotment in
a bankrupt publican. #

Anrovos recent comments anent Australians
Aprop West Coast of Africa, the following are
extracts from let ”rreceived trom , raining roan,
P fJ/hJ whole trip ; the first day being across the
through volcanic country, the train passing close
under two smoking craters, San Pedro apd Ollague, botn
about 21,000 ft. high. The third day is across the great
Bolivian plateau 12,000 ft. altitude to Oruro, which is
12,400 ft. The train is like a little toy, 2ft. 6m. gauge.
One feels the rarity of the atmosphere a good deal at
first. Fat men feel it most; some get “ mountain sick-
ness ” the first time they come, and are on their backs for
a fortnight. If one’s heart is weak, it is a case of first
train down again, and in any case one cannot take any
violent exercise at all here. Climate is nice in the .day-
time, just like Queensland winter, but very cold at night,
though it is a dry cold one doesn’t notice much. Oruro
has 14,000 inhabitants, mostly Indians, and is about as big
as an English town of 4000. There are about 60 English-
men in town and district, also a few Germans, Americans
and others. Bolivians are a cross between the old
Spaniards and Indians, and are in appearance like botn.
The town has narrow streets, very dirty, no drainage at
all, and, as the population is mainly Indian, with habits
—well, somewhat peculiar—l can leave much to your
imagination. The streets are full of dogs, which spend
their time fighting in mobs, and although fleas can t live
at this altitude, there are other things. People, English
anyway, spend their spare moments in drinking, [always
thought Australia was all right in this respect, but this
place fairly beats anything I ever imagined. Mining is
the main support of the district-principally tin, also a
little silver, though that was formerly the principal
branch. Tin is very rich, and the mines are paying
enormously. This country is, I believe, absolutely the
richest, minerally speaking, in the world, every kind of
metal being found, but on account of the difficulties of
transport (coal costs £8 per ton wholesale in Oruro), and
the fact that there is no timber here (too high), and costs
Is. per superficial foot, the expenses are too great at pre-
sent There is one property with seven lodes, each about
4ft. wide, assaying all through 20 per cent, copper, ioz.
gold, and 30oz. silver, yet they cannot be profitably
worked. I wish I were back in Queensland.’
• • * *
Reported that “a nugget of pure copper con-
taining 11 cubic feet of the metal and weighing four
tons has been found in the bed of a small stream running
into the Kuskalina River, Alaska, about 120 miles from
tide water." * . •
* • * *
Do Beers, which owns so much of the body and
soul of S’Africa, is still by far the wealthiest mining co.
in the world. At the annual meeting, held the other
dav at Kimberley, it was shown that the year s diamonds
had brought £5,241,173, while the total working expenses
were £1,703,130 ; and, after allowing for depreciation
f£738.161). redemption of debentures, &c., the profit was
£2,302,299. Besides this, about £120,000 was realised
from investments. The dividends totalled £2,176,000.
So “scientifically” has this huge co. “ worked the
diamond market that, notwithstanding its great produc-
tion (the De Beers alone turns out more now than the
world did 20 years ago), the average value per carat has
in that time been forced up from 225. Bd. to 425. sd.
• • * •
One man, anyway, comes back from England
to say that the yarn that the Westralian Arbitration Act
is scaring investors off that market is plain lie. ” was
felt ” says G. W. Hall, who was m the thick of pie Wes-
tral’ian boom and represented several Bull co. s, that
some of the awards made on the Northern fields were un-
iust, but the general opinion is that the principle of the
Act is good; and the feeling towards W.A. has been
greatly improved by the conviction that its mines can be
worked as cheaply as those of S’Africa, where th.e grade
of the ore is generally lower.”
• • * .
A British weekly thus mixes up the Coolgardie
miner, spinifex, and other things ; “ In the early days of
Coolgardie, the miners underwent extraordinary hard-
ships'amongst the spinifex bushes, where the gold mostly
grew. The sharp spikes of the bushes used to play havoc
with their hands, until the introduction of leather
gauntlets. A miner’s chances in the spinifex desert are
extremely precarious. He may be working on a good
patch one day, and perhaps during the night a willy-
willy may spring up. Should the sand storm be at
heavy, the unlucky gold gatherer wakes up next morning
to the fact that his particular spinifex bush is covered
over with sand hundreds of feet high. The only thing
he can do then is to lie down and pray for another willy-
willy to blow in the opposite direction.”

At La Monnaie Opera House, Brussels, recently, Jane
Merey sang Rosins, in “The Barber of Seville with her
arm in a sling. She had been shot by her husband as she
entered the theatre, and the bullet was not removed
untU after the performance. In the next production,
hafA Miranda took Mme. Merey’s place until the shot
lady had recovered.
Mrs. A. E. Donne (Miss Jeanne Ramsay), of Wellington,
a fellow-student in Melbourne with Ada Orossley, and a
too-seldom-heard soprano, accompanied the diva througn-
out her tour of M.L. #
Hugh Ward has probably made a mistake in accepting
a panto, engagement at Dniry Lane London where his
weak voice will be almost inaudible to th* bulk of the
audience, and the effect of his quaint unobtrusive
dancing will be to a great extent lost. It is the broad,
rasping comedian who makes a hit at Drury L*ne, pro-
vided he gets the foremost show. Newcomer Ward will
have to frivol in the background, or middle distance.
The life of a dramatic company touring in picturesque
but mouldy Asia is full of sombre anxieties and glad sur-
prises. Awhile ago, 0. A. Pollard took a co. from Mel-
bourne under a 12 months’ engagement, but when it had
finished a rather poor season in Shanghai, the experienced
Pollard thought that prospects were unfavorable, and was
for sending the people back to Australia. They were
mor£ hopeful—hope springs eternal in the mummers
breast—and preferred to continue the round trip with
Pollard’s manager, Alec Middleton, in charge of the
expedition. Did boom business at Singapore and Penang,
and, when last heard from, were flourishing at Agra, or
thereabouts, after putting in a good time at Calcutta.
It is a fairly strong touring co., with leanings towards
heavy drama, but the pieces that mostly serve to keep
the pot boiling are the same old “ What Happened to
Jones ’’ and “ Charley’s Aunt.” Anything more emotional
than “Jones’’ is a risky experiment in.those tired Asiatic
localities, where the White Man takes up his burden and
leaves the colored native to carry it.

A. RECENT TESTIMONIAL.
“ Dear Sir, “ November 11, 1903.
“ With a grateful heart I ask you to accept a word of thanks for the benefit you have bestowed on
me. I was in a fair way of being overpowered with strong drink. Again and again I resolved not to touch
it, and again and again I drank ‘ success to good old resolution I* But one day an old and tried friend,
who was sorry to see my condition, and who at intervals had read me temperance lectures, said to me, ‘ I
can get a medicine that will take the desire to drink from you altogether.’ I smiled to myself, having no
faith in such cures. However, I said, ‘ You will be my best friend if you can do that.’ He said, ‘ Will you
take the medicine ? ’ I said, ‘ I will,’ and I did. I stuck to it, and it was all right. My old energy and
will-power have returned; the horrible nightmare has departed, and ‘Richard is hiwself again.’
DRUNKENNESS IS A DISEASE. To preach at a man is useless. To sign temperance pledges is of no
avail. To tell a man he is bad is an incentive to make him worse. The disease wants an antidote ; you
have that antidote in ALCOCIDE. God preserve you to use it for those who cry in anguish, ‘ Take the
curse away 1’ Yours, dear sir, for ever, .”

A Vic. pressman on Premier Irvine’s
mind
For a still, strong, calm man Iceberg Irvine is a most
indecisive wobbler. At a gaud mayor’s banquet Irvine
deliberately announced his intention to retire from the
Ministry, and from politics altogether. For a week after-
wards he persisted in saying that he could not afford to
remain in politics. Then a meeting of his supporters wag
held to ask him to reconsider his decision, and after some
struggling with himself, Irvine announced that he would
stay and lead his party so long as the country had confi-
dence in him. Irvine has a woeful tendency' to change
his mind—that is for a still, cool, calm man. A sudden
change of mind led him to contest a seat in Parliament in
the first place, when he carefully courted the civil service
vote. A rapid change of mind enabled him to put under
the table his objections to Woman’s Suffrage and join the
M'Lean-Shiels Ministry as Attorney-General at £lOOO a
year. A violent change of mind caused him, as soon as
he was out of office, to fish his objections to Woman’s
Suffrage from beneath the table, dust them, and put
them coldly and calmly before the public again. A rapid
change of mind resulted in Irvine resigning the leader-
ship of the Opposition. r Then a staggering change of
mind ended in his sneaking back to the position of leader
of the Opposition within a few days after he had resigned.
An instantaneous change of mind enabled him to
dish his own followers after they had helped him defeat
Peacock and to propose a reduction of members to 56,
whereas, before the defeat of Peacock, he had fixed 70
members. A severe change of mind induced him to put
his objections to Woman’s Suffrage under the table again
and give that principle a place in his Constitutional
Reform Bill. Various swift changes of mind were re-
sponsible for several other things in that measure, while
an equal number of changes of mind accounted for the
fact that Woman’s Suffrage and other features of the Bill
were hacked out of it at the conference between the two
Ho ises. About this time Irvine had changed his mind so
often and so suddenly that his Tory heelers began to
write letters to the press headed “Which Mr. Irvine?”
bin ;e then Irvine has changed his mind in public so many
tunes that one begins to wonder whether his poor, old
calm, cool mind has not been almost worn out in the
changing.
A once famous whip, in the person of
Edward Devine (“Cabbage-tree Ned’’ of the
golden days), returned recently to Ballarat after
36 years on Westralian fields. From 1853 (when
he was only 17) to 1862, Ned drove a six-in-hand
between Geelong and Ballarat for Doming, Cobb
and Co., and as a whip was styled “ the Icing of
the highway.” During the first two years his
wage was £l6 per week, and his tips from lucky
diggers, for whom he conveyed gold to the banks,
averaged even more than that. The roads at the
time were frequented by “Captain” Melville,
Black Douglas, and other bushrangers. Ned had
many narrow escapes, and on one occasion his
coach was stuck up, and his passengers robbed of
£BOO, by the notorious Ned Jordan, afterwards
hanged for the murder of squatter Rutherford.
Devine got his sobriquet from the fact that he
usually sported a wide-brimmed cabbage-tree
hat—a favorite head-dress of diggers in the 50’s.
