The Place of the Dead.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Up from the cliffs where the billows surge
Merrily, madly in leaping spray,
Stretches the home of the lonely dead ;
Pallid marble and granite grey,
Stone-bound vault and graven slab,
Cross and pillar, angel and crown,
Wind-scoured sand and withered flowers,
Grass, sun-parched to a sickly brown.
And here, at the cliffs, the open sea
Like a troubled mirror, with emerald sheen,
Leaping gaily in feathered spray,
Rolling grandly in billows green,
Thundering up with a curving crest
To break in foam with a hollow roar;
Gliding back with a snake-like haste
Over the rocks and the weed-strewn shore.
Silent, sunlit, sail-less, sad,
Stretching afar through a turquoise mist;
Unbroken save when a leaping plume
Of gem-hung spray, glows, sunlight-kissed.
To right and left the guardian rocks,
Crowned with the sand-stung grasses tall,
Standing lonely, knee-deep in foam,
Front each shock with an iron wall.
Oh ! how can ye rest, ye sleeping dead,
There in your narrow, stone-bound beds,
With the drifting sand, and the withered flowers,
And the crowding crosses at your heads ?
While here at your feet, through sun and shade,
Tempest and calm, lies the aged Deep,
Laughing and whispering, thundering loud,
Calling ye ever—yet there ye sleep !
When my call comes, and ye seek a place
For that which is left of what once was me,
Lay me not thus in an earthy bed,
To sleep or dream through Eternity.
No ! Bear me down to the weed-strewn shore,
’Neath the dripping cliffs so gaunt and bare,
With the scream of the gulls for my requiem,
With no muttered mass and no mumbled prayer.
Let the wreathing arms that grip the land,
Curving in from the crested Deep,
Clutch me, and catch me, and keep me fast,
Bearing me back in their widening sweep ;
And the voice that first sang me a lullaby
As I lay asleep on iny mother’s breast,
The voice of the sea I have ever loved
Shall whisper me tenderly, “ Peace and Rest 1 ”
N.s.w.
CHAS. E. HOWELL.
A Religious Outlook.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A tame little curate, young, soulful, and be-
spectacled, struck the homestead a little after
dusk, and we took him in and scraped up a feed
for his horse. Most of our own had been trucked
down Gippsland way. After ago at some rather
tough mutton, he told us something about him-
self. The burden of our learning was that he was
three weeks out from England, and was engaged
in picking up impressions as fast, and with as
much discrimination, as a fly just out of a treacle-
tin picks up dust.
“I am to take charge of a curacy,” he explained.
“ But before doing so, I thought it well to learn
something of this great country, and so I have
embarked on a riding tour, preaching as I go. ”
Jim muttered to me that if he couldn’t preach a
bit better than he rode, it was a pity, but mother
frowned him down. Father screwed up his mouth
as if he had bitten something nasty.
“ You mean you take services on Sundays ?” he
asked.
“ Oh, no. The fields are my tabernacle.”
“You’ll find plenty of lost sheep there,” father
said, grimly. “I’m afraid you’ll have some diffi-
culty in keeping them from straying. Most of
’em are pretty dog-poor just now. There’s only
one sort of preaching that’s acceptable to empty
stomachs.”
The curate glanced in a pained way at Nell
and mother, and actually blushed.
. “I own, ” he said, “ I have found some difficulty
in making my hearers listen to the Word. There
is no outward sign. Yet, who knows but that my
ministrations are blessed inwardly? I have not
yet grown weary in well-doing.”
“How long ’ve you been at it ? ” father asked,
bluntly.
“Five days,” said the curate, modestly. And
father grinned a little.
We saddled-up for him in the morning, as he
said he wanted to make an early start Echuca
way. After breakfast, he mentioned his desire to
hold a service before he left. Father and I cleared
out, and got lost in the stable. Nell left for parts
unknown. The girl in the kitchen was “ a
Roman,” so all the congregation he got was
mother and Jim. He took about half-an-hour to
save them; then mother came out looking very
weary, and Jim said “Blast! ” as he passed me.
The curate looked happy, but the feeling seemed
confined to him.
Father told me to put him through the pad-
docks and on to the road, so we started out after
the curate had left a blessing on the house, which
didn’t seem to appreciate the favor. He talked
godliness to me all the way, so naturally I hadn’t
much to say. Anyhow, it was enough to make a
fellow silent to ride through those dried-up, dusty
paddocks. There was nothing to look at but
sheep skeletons, which don’t make cheerful
scenery.
Just inside our boundary we sighted a camp
under a tree. There was a rough waggon made
out of an old spring dray with a tilt fixed-up with
hessian, and a little way off an old grey mare-
wandered round chewing sticks. We hadn’t
troubled for weeks to keep a padlock on the gate.
I lie owners were sitting under a tree and said,
“Good morning ! ” civilly enough as we rode up.
They were the sorriest-looking lot I’d seen, in a.
season when none but sorry travellers came by.
A big man, not a bad-looking fellow either, and a
pretty woman with grey streaks in her hair that
looked as if they shouldn’t be there. She had a
baby at her breast, and there was a little chap of
three or four running round. He was almost
naked, and the others weren’t much better off.
Iliey had clothes of a sort, but no two garments-
matched, and they were in rags. The baby was
wrapped in an old green tablecloth. I’d have
taken my oath not one of them had had a decent
feed for a month ; and the old mare’s hips were
nearly through her hide. The man said she had
knocked-up the day before, and they were out of
tucker. I asked him where they came from, and
he said, “ The back of Hell.”
I heard the curate give a horrified little gasp,
and knew he was preparing for action. I could
have laughed if it hadn’t been for the white-faced
woman in front of-us. She hadn’t looked at us,
except for the first glance, but sat staring over the
baby’s head at nothing in particular.
“ My friend,’" said the curate, severely, “ I fear
you do not realise what your words mean.”
“ Gawd knows I do,” said the other man.
“ I should ljke to talk to you. ”
“ I don’t hold with preachin’ and psalm-singin’,
mister,” said the chap. “If you can give me a
job to make tucker out of I’ll be glad to talk of
that.”
“I regret I have no work to give,” said the
curate, “ but if a shilling or two ”
“ Oh, dry up; we ain’t beggin’vet! ” cut in the
other. The curate smelt a cue and pounced on it.
“Before the Most High we are all beggars,”
he said, smugly. “ I fear, my friend, you neglect
Him. Pray ! He can make rivers of running
water in the desert ! ”
“No bloke in his senses prays like that,” said
the big, ragged man ; “ all we asked Him was to
keep the creeks He’d made already goin’. If He
got full up on that contrack, one’d hardly expect
Him to take on another. It ain’t sense.”
“It is not for us to criticise Him,” the curate
said. “ Remember He holds us in the hollow of
His hand.”
“Does He?” asked the chap in a tired way.
“ Well, He’s squeezed us pretty damn dry. He’s
been better’n a patent wringer up our way.”
The curate looked as if he were going to take a
fit. His eyes bulged. He began with something
' ' at sounded like “ Alas ! ” but the other struck
in.
“Now look here, mister,” he said, “Im just
about chock-full of chaps like you. Newspaper-
reporters—bloomin’ town men and wimmen—an’
you psalm-singers are the only sorts that come
up our way now, an’ I’m blowed if I know whieli
of yous is the worst. You sling off about mercy
an’ lovin’-kindness an’ plenteousness till we’re
fair sick; an’ the only plenteousness is dust an’
dead stock, an’ the only mercy gittin’ away with
some of our lives; an’ as for the lovin’-kindness,
it’s off ! Come ’n live up in the drought for six
months, an’ try to scrape your own tucker an’
your kids’ up somehow, an’ then see how much
you’ll talk about gawspel. Gawspel’s the only
thing we get free, and we get full-up on that.”
The curate looked despairing and turned to the
woman, who didn’t seem as if she’d heard any-
thing.
“My sister,” he said, “ I trust you do not share
these fearful views ? You know the tender mercy
of our God, do you not ? ”
“I reckon I do,” she said, without looking at
him.
It was his first bit of encouragement, and it
cheered him up quite wonderfully.
“ Ah, I knew it! ”he cried. “You have faith
—you know that whom He loveth He chasteneth ! ”
“ He’s mighty fond of us, then,” she said.
“We’ve had no end of His mercies. He never
forgets us.”
The curate looked a bit less chirpy, but he kept
his end up.
“ Then you will pray with me ? ” he began.
“No; I think not, thanks,” she said, slowly.
“ I’ve given over prayin’ for the time bein’—ever
since the mercies began to get thick. Seemed a
if I couldn’t pray through them, so I pulled up.”
“I don’t understand,” he said, doubtfully.
“No; anyone c’d see that,” she agreed. “But
I gave over prayin’ when our last cow went dry.”
He looked primly shocked. “ Was that sufficient
cause, sister?” he asked.
“Oh, I think so. You see, that meant the
girls dyin’—my little girls. They was both sick,
just sick from bad food an’ water, an’ heat, an’
that cow’s milk was all we had to keep them alive.
So they died, an’ I chucked prayin’. Seems
curious, don’t it, that one cow’s milk sh’d make a
split in a thing like that? It was bloomin’poor
milk, too! ”
“So was the mercy,” said the man—“bloomin’
poor ! ”
“That’s my sister’s boy,” said- the woman,
pointing to the kid running about. “She died,
too, she’n her baby—died of heat an’ dust, an’
flies, an’ starvin’. She’d lived on pollard for a
month before her baby was born. An’ now you
come, with your gawspel-talk, an’ preach about
tender mercies. We know all about ’em, bet your
life! What is there we don’t know, livin’ in
hell?”
The man put his arm round her a hit awkwardly.
“Shut up, Liz, old girl,” he said. She never
heeded him.
“Lord, what don’t we know?” she said. Her
eyes were getting wild, like a cow’s eyes get
when it’s in the killing-pen. “We watch
those mercies all day, and we dream of
’em all night; when the kids aren’t cryin’
for food. Every night I watch my two little girls
dyin’ again, an’ they beg me for milk, an’ I give'
’em muddy water again. We weren’t in the
desert, y’see, where the rivers of runnin’ water
get made ; we carted all ours, till the horses
died.” The baby at her breast gave a little wail.
“This baby’s goin’ to die, too”’ she went on.
‘ ‘ There was two of ’em, twins, but the other died,
somehow. Y’see, there was on’y Bill here with
me when they come. It was another tender
mercy ! ”
She fell over sideways. The man caught her,
and I showed the curate, whose face was green,
out on to the road, without more politeness than
was necessary. He did not leave any more bless-
ings.
I took the travellers up to the house, and father
gave the man a job water-carting. The baby died
next day.

“ J.D.” on the registry office trouble :
Re registration fee demanded by registry office sharks.
Know two who subsist also, to some extent, on a
“ postage fee” of one shilling. It is a slight, variation of
the *• registration fee,” and guarantees free corres-
pondence concerning vacant jobs fora period of twelve
months. On three successive days recently one of these
concerns required three, four, and five barmaids (12
barmaids in all) for city and country hotels. I men-
tioned the dearth to a barmaid out of “ collar,” and she
informed me that she had paid a “postage fee” nine
weeks previously, and had not received a line regarding
a vacancy, although the shark had advertised some forty-
positions in the interval. The postage fee idea has this
great advantage—it fetches in shillings from the
country, and the country girl isn’t handy to
make a personal row about not getting any-
thing for her shilling. The registry office business
is in urgent need of regulation, but it seems to be
impossible to do anything in Australia without the
aid of Parliament. And Parliament starts by appointing
a commission to spend six months in inquiring, and six
months in drawing up a report which fills a large book.
Then it is necessary to compose a Bill and get it read six
times in Parliament, and twice passed through com-
mittee. And when someone moves the adjournment of
the House every night as soon as it meets, and there are
also 200 questions to answer, and the usual discussion
about Bill’s dirt-money to be attended to, and the want
of a quorum as well, how can anything be done '!
“Alb.”: The Pope will not remain
always bottled up in Rome. As Catholic ideas
expand, he will be seen in Vienna, Berlin, Paris,
London, Dublin, New York, Chicago—aye, in
Sydney. Travel is becoming recognised as indis-
pensable for potentates. The holy father needs to
got away from the clammy steam of the Italian
clerical atmosphere. Why should his ideas be
filtered out to him, or given like pap to an infant ?
Louis Yon Doussa, South Australia’s new
Attorney General, served articles with the same
country solicitor (the veteran Mr. Bonnar, now
resident at Manly) as his predecessor, Justice
Gordon. The S.A. Parliament holds two Von
Doussas. This one is its ecclesiastical authority,
the other is its turf. authority. One is church
advocate of the Adelaide diocese, the other seore-
tary of Onkaparinga Racing Club.

Christmas Reminiscences.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
EEMEMBER once a-spendin’Chris’mas Eve!
i £ 'pendin’ it, mind yer—no darned make-
believe,
But tip-top goin'; all the coves was bricks.
Them as was “stony ” had their drinks for nix.
’Twas “fill an’ empty, fill again,” all night,
Beer, fizz, an’ whisky—then an all-round fight,
’Cause most of us chipped in ter lend a ’and—
The others would ’ave, too, but couldn’t stand,
’Twas simple-like, the way the row began—
Squib Jinks remarked as “Billy ain’t no man,
But blanky blank,” and other blanks condensed,
So Bill ’auled off, an’ then the fun commenced,
Squib loved a scrap—it seemed ter cool ’is brain
Ter jump up, sudden-like, an’ then raise Cain ;
An’, when yer comes ter think, ’e warn’t to
blame,
’Twas ’uman natur’—Adam did the same.
Yes, them was times, there ain’t the same seen
now,
W’en men was men, takin’ ’em any’ow.
Most of ’em’s now in ’Eav’n, or else in—well,
Lord on’y knows, so ’ow am I ter tell ?
Old Squib got flattened in a “ frien’ly spar ”
An’ Parson tells me Billy’s “ crossed the bar,”
Though that ain’t strange, ’cause Billy’d never
think
Of passin’ any bar -without a drink.
Welived them days, made fortunes—spent’em, too,
In rare old style, mates ’elped each other through.
I’ve ’ad my ups an’ downs, most downs—l mean
Luck’s now N. 8., w’ich stands for “not a bean.”
An’ girls ! —where can yer see the likes to-day
Of six-foot Kate, or Jane of Bramble Bay ?
Big-limbed, with muscle— men from ’ead ter toe —
They may have been men, too, fer all I know.
This Old Men’s ’Ome, an’ inmates, ain’t my class ;
But w’at’s a man ter do ’oo’s lost ’is brass ?
’Tain’t Christian-like, at Chris’mas, sittin’ ’ere
A-drinkin’ tea, an’ dreamin’ as it’s beer.
I’ve ’ad my w’ack of life, so can’t complain—
After long spells of dry it’s bound ter rain ;
On’y with me the rule’s turned tail about—
Long spells of wet, an’ now a bloomin’ drought.
Might ’ave saved ’undreds!—what good would
they be
Now limbs is stiff an’ eyes can’t ’ardly see ?
Live while ye’re young -give the young colt ’is
’ead :
Life’s time is short, —we’ll be a long time dead.
Lived longer goin’ slow ! Maybe, can’t say !
At any rate I’ve lived ter see ter-day.
An’ if old age ’as nothin’ else ter give
But rheumatis an’ tea —’oo wants ter live ?
An’ die unmourned !—quite natcherally so,
’Cause all my pals were planted long ago ;
But in the sixties, mate, there warn’t a man
But w’at was pleased ter shake a ’and with Dan.
When shift-time comes I wants no monyment,
But just a slab ter say—“ ’Ere lies Dan Brent,
An all-round man afore ’e passed ’is prime,”
An’ writ beneath—“ ’E ’ad a good time ! ”
M.L.
KAREWAO.
His Old Man.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
MY mate George was a very quiet sort of
fellow, and not a bit communicative.
One afternoon, when we were nearing
the end of a job, I noticed an old, grey-headed
traveller making across to us.
“ Hel-10, George !” he said in a rather surprised
tone, dropping his swag.
“ Hullo !” said George.
“ Didn’t expect to see you up this way,” went
on the traveller.
“Nor me you,” answered George; “I thought
you was up Gippsland way.”
“Oh, I left those parts three years ago, arid
I’ve been pretty well all round since,” replied our
visitor.
The traveller took his pipe from his pocket, lit
it at one of the burning heaps, had a few pulls,
and, taking it from his mouth, said : “ Been down
in Melbourne lately, George ? ”
“Yes,” said George ; “a few months ago.”
“ Mother all right ? ”
Yes.”
“And the girls?”
“Yes.”
Another silence. The traveller wenton smoking.
George kicked a few sticks together, and said,
“ Clara’s married now.”
“ Oh ! ” said the old fellow, taking the pipe out
of his mouth again, “that’s good”; and as an
afterthought, “Mary’s still at home, I suppose?”
“Yes,” answered George, “Mary and mother’s
alone now.” .
“ Still at the old place ? ”
“Still at the old place,” said George. Silence
again. The traveller took a few long pulls at his
pipe, but it was out. Lighting up again, he
asked, “ Who’d Clara marry, George ? ”
George thought for awhile. “Some chap
named Parker or Parkins ; I forget which, now. ”
The traveller nodded his head two or three
times. “ Where’d they live ?” he asked.
“I dunno,” said George,
“ They got any kids ? ”
“I dun no, they ain’t. They’ve only been
married ’bout six months, I think.”
The traveller nodded his head as before and
said, “Well, good luck to them,” and after a few
more pulls he went on : “ Well, I suppose I must
be getting along. Tell the mother you saw me,
George, next time you’re home, and say I’ll be
down one of these days.”
“Yes, I will,” said George.
We watched him out of sight, and, turning to
George, I said, “The old chap seems to have
known your people once.”
“I should think he did,” he replied ; “that’s
my old man.”
“ What ! ” I cried, astonished ; “ your father ? ”
“ Yes,” he answered, evidently surprised at my
tone.
“Well,” said I, staring at him, “I must say
you seemed very glad to see him.”
“ Yes,” said he, not noticing the sarcasm. “ The
mother’ll be glad, too. That's the first we’ve
heard of him for five years. Funny I should meet
him up here, ain’t it?”
“Yes,” I said. “I suppose you’ll wiite and
tell the mother now, won’t you ? ”
“ Oh, I dunno,” he said ; “ I think I’ll wait till
I get down.”
L.S.H.
Sleeper-Getting.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Uloola ” discourses on “Sleeper-getting”—the
cutting of sleepers for railways :
“ Sleepers ” are cut from ironbark, blackbutt, spotted
gum, turpentine, flooded gum, mahogany, and some few
other kinds of timber. Ironbark is about the best, black-
butt nearly as good, and spotted gum not far behind
either. But tastes differ. Spotted gum is very faulty.
Gum rings in the tree are a frequent and especial trouble.
No good sleeper-chopper will “work” a “ring.v” tree,
for the sleepers will split along the gum-rings after a few
days’ exposure to sun and air.
Only old trees are fit for sleepers, and young timber is
never cut, except, as happens sometimes, when the
“passer" doesn’t know old wood from young ; then the
choppers “ ring in " “ sap ” on him. Sleepers vary in
size, from the “ little sleeper,” 6ft. 6in. x Bin. x 4in.
(Maoriland had a lot of these), to the “ big ” sleeper,
9ft. x lOin. x sin. A neat and compact sleeper is the
7ft. x lOin. x sin. of the N.S.W. railways. The nine-
footer, especially if it is green ironbark, is a brute to
handle.
The core and the sap of a tree are rejected as a rule,
the chopper preferring a hollow tree to a sound one,
because the sleepers can be then cut much more quickly.
The quickest and best way to cut sleepers is to “ groove ”
your tree before “ bursting out” the billets that are to
be afterwards squared into the completed article.
Having your tree down (cut “ stump-high',” or
“scaffolded”), and sawn into lengths, you measure—
with a piece of board the size of the sleeper-end—the
block to see how many billets you can split out of it.
(Sleepers are split, “ on the fiat,” the “ boarding ” way of
the tree— i.e., if you can see, in imagination, the sleepers
in the log before they are cut out, they lie side by side
round the bole. And herein is the reason why sleepers
are split and not sawn. If sawn, the most would be made
of the block and the sleepers sawn “on the flat" the
“bursting” way of the tree, and be of no use.)
The portion of the log between the two sleeper ends is
chopped out with a grooving axe, and when this is done
all round the block it is an easy matter to burst it into
billets. Squaring the billets is done with a ‘ ‘ siding ” axe,
a weapon with a blade from 10 to 14 inches long. Many
choppers can square a sleeper as true and smooth, with a
siding axe, as if it had been planed.
Nearly all the good sleeper-timber in the easily acces-
sible bush at Wyong, Ourimbah, and the districts there-
about has been cut out, and year by year the choppers
have to go farther back up the hills and gullies, where-
upon the haulage is more and more difficult and costly.
Up Jilliby way, where a lot of the “little ironbarks”
(6ft. 6in. x Bin. x 4in.) have recently been cut, as many as
26 bullocks have been hooked on to haul the empty wag-
gon up the hill. The sleepers are “spare-chained” out
of the bush from where they have been cut — i.e., a chain
is'passed round a number of them, and the bunch is
hauled along the ground to where the waggon has been
left. This is also called “ snigging-out.” When loaded
up and ready for descent, several large trees are cut
down, the branches left on, and these, chained behind
the waggon, help to steady it as it goes down.
Things have been excessively dull with the sleeper
choppers for some months back, so the recent order for
half-a-million for South Africa was welcome. However,
trouble has supervened on the contractors trying to
reduce the price for chopping from 2s. 3d. per sleeper,
delivered at the railway station (the old price for “ seven
by ten by fives ”), to Is. 9d. delivered. Out of this the
sleeper-getter has to pay for haulage to the station (from
6d. to Is. according to distance and difficulty), and also
for his license (2s. 6d. per month) and bush rent. More-
over, all the risks are thrust on the chopper. The
sleepers are “ passed ” at the railway stations, and if any
are condemned and thrown out, that is the chopper's
loss. Sometimes the condemned sleepers are sold as
inferior for about enough to cover cost of ear.age ; more
often the chopper gets no return whatever from them.
To resist the attempted reduction the choppers formed
a Sleeper-Getters’ Union. The trouble seems to be t lie old
one of sub-letting the contracts. The original contract was
secured by a big Sydney firm, then sub-let t a local mill-
owner, and then portion of it again to other local men
—storekeepers, &c. When all these get their pickings
there’s little left for the man who does the work and
takes all the risks.
One of the champion sleeper-getters of this district was
“Jack" Towell (recently gone further north). During
the three years he cut round Ourimbah he averaged £3
to £3 10s. per week. But, in addition to being an expert
sleeper-getter, he worked from “jackass to jackass,”
going to the bush at daylight and coming back when he
could no longer see to work.
Filson Young, in London Daily Mail,
writes about the Law’s barbarous method of
dealing with the criminal condemned to die-
how it tends him, fattens him, healthfully exer-
cises him, and provides ministrations for his
blessed soul before proceeding to end him. But
the gloomy hideousness of a murderer’s last days
in an English prison is relieved by privileges
which he wouldn’t get in Australia. The con-
demned “ cell ” that Young describes is an airy,
comfortable room, and the prisoner is hanged in
his free-eitizen clothes. An Australian prisoner
mostly has to meet his doom in the character of a
regulation scarecrow. He may glory in his crime,
but he dies horribly ashamed of his togs, anyhow.
In a South Queensland township, just
beyond Judas Iscariot and adjacent to the
Lamentations of Jeremiah, there was a store-
keeper called Simpson who was reckoned to be
the mildest man west of sunrise. He became the
object of adoration of two ladies of the community
and had not sufficient courage to refuse either of
them, and feeling ran high. At last No. 1 became
desperate and went to Simpson and bullied him
into leaving for Toowoomba by the next train,
determined that he should marry her in proper
style. When No. 2 heard of the flight her be-
havior was cyclonic. The happy pair returned
soon after and settled down, and for a time there
was peace. Then No. 2, by the aid of a friend in
Toowoomba, discovered that there had been no
marriage at al/ After enjoying a holiday they
had come back, naving dispensed with the for-
mality, and considered themselves one. Like a
sensible woman she said nothing, but waited her
chance. One day, when a parson happened
along, and Mrs. Simpson was gossiping to
a neighbor over the back fence, she entered
the store and, capturing Simpson under the
nose of an astonished customer, she bore
him off in his shirt sleeves to the parson, and
they were hitched up with due ceremony, wit-
nesses and all. She returned and ejected No. 1,
who was still at the back fence talking to the
neighbor, and whose first news of the trouble
came to her when the lawful wife threw her spare
clothes out to her in the yard, and told her that
the back gate was the most sudden way off the
premises. f'

Musgrove’s niggers, in “ Dahomey,” continue to draw
at the London Shaftesbury. When they were in New
York a cullud preacher is said to have prayed as follows :
—“ An’, 0 Lo’d ! pluck as bran’s frum de bu’nin’ dem
triflin’, good-fo’-nothin’ niggahs dats cuttin’ up monkey
shines in dem houses o' sin an’ ’niqiiity, de w’ite man’s
theaytres—’arnin’ de wages o’ sin in de hire o’ de debbil !
I know, O Lo’d ! dose high wages ; but, O Lo’d ! dat
ain’t no ’count ’long o’ de turrible wickedness an’ sinful-
ness o’ de business. Dev’s a bu’nin’ an’ blazin’ lake o’
fire for dem low-down niggahs, O Lo’d ! where no wages
won’t save ’em frum ’tarda! wrath ! Shoo ’em off dat
wicked stage, O Lo’d ! an’ back to de way o’ righteous-
ness an’ peace, eben if de wages ain’t so big ! ”
“ Leigh Woods There comes a time in the life of every
man when he is impelled to arise and speak the truth
that is in him, and when the urgent requests of his wife
not to make a fool of himself are as naught; so also are
the whispered beseeehings of his best girl and the warn-
ings of his friend’s wife (with whom he has no right to
be). It fell upon me the other night when I entered a
gas-lit portico at which a fiend in black collected one
shilling, and within which a young man in a white choker
and a dinner jacket was reciting Villon’s “ Ballade of
Dead Ladies.” “Where are the girls of yesterday?”
he yelled. “The wind has blown them all away,”
etc. His emphasis on the “all” annoyed me,
hut I struggled with the impulse and held it
down by the throat till the reciter had finished. Then it
got out of hand, and I arose and lifted up my voice just
as the young man had elevated his, and the mixed spirits
within me spoke thusly : “Young roan, you are a liar,
and the Villon whom you laud—the Villon who drove
Swinburne into ecstasies and long hair—was a fraud. His
poems were based upon imagination and a class of girls
who had a habit of dying suddenly through helping
themselves to other people’s belongings. His girls were
hanged by Louis Xl.’s executioner —justly hanged.
Young man, you ask where are the girls of yesterday ? I
tell you the girls of ten years ago, let alone those of
yesterday, are still prowling round asking for free theatre
passes for the sake of old times. The girl I took to
Chowder in the days before it became Clifton Gardens
and high class, for once only, just to see what
it was like, still sidles up to me when I
leave the theatre with the girl of to-night on my
strong right arm, and says in a winkful whisper, ‘He
bought me feathers once ! ’ The girl I hugged in the
boarding-house two years ago writes me agonised letters
full of ‘My love, my love, why have you left me without
a cent?’ There’s a pretty kettle of fish for you, and
neither the police nor the wind blows it away. The girl
I kissed, surreptitious like, on the stairs last night is, I’ll
bet a sovereign, waiting in exactly the same place for it
to happen again. No, my young friend, my brothers,
and fellow-men, the girls of yesterday and the last ten
years, are all here, right now ; most of them hard up, andi
all of them wanting money.” Then the heavenly impulse
gave out and I drifted through the door, leaving a heavy
and distinctly noticeable silence behind me.
